 \tableofcontents \chapter{Introduction to the data analysis program {{sasfit}}}

Small-angle scattering (SAS) is one of the powerful techniques to investigate the structure of materials on a mesoscopic length scale (10 - 10000 Å). It is used to study the shapes and sizes of the particles dispersed in a homogenous medium. The materials could be a macromolecule (biological molecule, polymer, micelle, etc) in a solvent, a precipitate of material A in a matrix of another material B, a microvoid in certain metal or a magnetic inhomogeneity in a nonmoagnetic material. This technique is also used to study the spatial distribution of particles in a medium, thus providing the information about the inter-particle interactions. The small angle scattering methods includes small angle neutron, x-ray or light scattering. The type of samples that can be studied by scattering techniques, the sample environment that can be applied, the actual length scale probed and the information that can be obtained, all depend on the nature of the radiation employed. The advantage of small-angle neutron scattering (SANS) over other SAS methods is the deuteration method. This consists in using deuterium labeled components in the sample in order to enhance their contrast. Whereas SANS has disadvantaged over small-angle x-ray scattering (SAXS) by the intrinsically low flux of neutron sources compared to the orders of magnitude higher fluxes of x-ray sources. Neutron scattering in general is sensitive to fluctuations in the density of nuclei in the sample. X-ray scattering is sensitive to inhomogeneities in electron densities whereas light scattering is sensitive to fluctuations in polarizability (refractive index). In general, irrespective of the type of radiation, they also share several similarities. Perhaps the most important of these is the fact that, with minor adjustments to account for the different types of radiation, the same basic equations and laws can be used to analyze data from these techniques. The small-angle scattering data can contain information concerning both the structure and interaction within the sytem. This information can be obtained by either performing model-independent analysis or detailed model dependent analysis. {{sasfit}} is such a software package built for analysis of small-angle neutron scattering data concerning soft matter. The main emphasis of the software is to provide easy to use visual interface for the new as well as for an expert user. The software package contains most of the tools to treat large range of scientific problems and large volume of data produced on a SAS instrument. It allows users to derive useful information from the SAS scattering data.

==System Requirements And Software Installation==

{{sasfit}} is a program for analyzing small angle scattering data. The numerical fitting routines are written in C and the menu interface in {\tt tcl/tk}. For the plotting of the data the {\tt tcl} extension {\tt blt} has been used. The last version 0.85 of {{sasfit}} has been tested with the {\tt tcl/tk} version 8.3 and the {\tt blt} version 2.4s. \\

 {{sasfit}} is available for users analysing data taken at PSI.\\{{sasfit}} has been developed at the Paul Scherrer Institute (PSI) and remains \copyright of PSI.\\{{sasfit}} is provided to users of the PSI facilities.\ {{sasfit}} is provided "as is", and with no warranty. \\

==Installation Procedure==

\\

\verb"SASfit" has has been compiled with tcl/tk 8.4 and Blt 2.4. To install the {{sasfit}} package one has to do the following:


# Download the zip-file "sasfit.zip" from the {{sasfit}}-home page\ \verb"http://kur.web.psi.ch/sans1/SANSSoft/sasfit.html" 
# extract the contents of the zip file. A new subdirectory called \verb"sasfit" will be generated, which contains all required files. 
# Execute the program  {{mono|./sasfit/sasfit.exe}}

\chapter{Quick Start Tour}

==User Interface Window==


 FIGURE DELETED \sloppy The core {{sasfit}} window consists of various tabs (shown in the oval marking in the figure (a), they are "{{mono|fit of single data set}}", "{{mono|multiple data sets for global fitting}}", "{{mono|residuum}}", "{{mono|size distrib.}}", "{{mono|integral structural parameters}}", "{{mono|parameters of analytical size distrib.}}", "{{mono|moments of analytical size distrib.}}", and "{{mono|2D detector intensity}}" as shown in the red oval selection. The tabs for single data set and multiple data sets are used to plot single or multiple data files and view the plotted graphs along with the operations to perform during fitting. Residuum shows the difference between the experimental and theoretical fits. Size distributions give the plotted view of the number density v/s radius of the particle. Integral structural parameters are obtained using model independent fitting, such as Guinier approximation, Porod law etc. Parameters of analytical size distributions provides with details of size distributions used and the numbers obtained, whereas moments of analytical size distribution shows the contribution of scattering from different size distribution. The final tab 2D detector intensity is used in case of anisotropic scattering data. The window were the graphs are generated has options of printing the graph plotted view, copying the data in the ASCII format or as an image (wmf) format for further processing or presenting (figure (b)). {{sasfit}} accepts the isotropic data in the ASCII format. The data can be imported as a single data set or for multiple data sets (several scattering curves).

==Importing data files for a single data set==


 FIGURE DELETED


 FIGURE DELETED

The single data set option allows the user to perform operation on a single data file only. The file is imported via, \verb"[File|Single Data Set...]" (Figure ). This will open a new file window as shown in figure (a). The location of the file could be browsed and respectively selected. The options buttons is supplied to select the input format, which is performed by supplying a string such as {{mono|xye}}. Where {{mono|x}}, {{mono|y}} and {{mono|e}} stands for the scattering vector <math>Q</math>, scattering intensity <math>I(Q)</math>, {{mono|e}} signifies the error bar <math>\Delta I(Q)</math> on the measured scattering intensity. The error bars are required during the fitting operation and for files which do not contain the error bar column it would be calculated by default from the smoothing of the curve. There is an option to skip lines at the beginning of the data, which is intended to be used to skip header information in a data file , which could be misinterpreted as data. The number <math>n</math> specified in the menu defines the number of lines skipped at the beginning of the data file. Furthermore a file extension can be provided, unit conversions can be performed as well as only non-negative <math>y</math>-values could be selected for plotting and performing further analysis. On pressing ok the data is loaded and the graph is plotted, with a new window labeled merge files being opened.


 FIGURE DELETED

In SAS, data can be collected at different collimator and sample to detector distances to correspond for a wide <math>Q</math> scale. Thus for a single sample at a given condition there can be more than one data files, to merge all of them together for completing the scattering profile, the above shown window comes into play. As shown in the merge files window, the new file could be browsed and selected; it has to be read using the read file button. The newly read file is listed below the first file, if it's a wrong selection it could be deleted back, also one can scale the different files measured at different <math>Q</math> windows, using the divisor column to have a continuous scattering profile. After scaling all the data profiles into one single profile, the statistically bad and unwanted data points can be removed by skipping the points at the beginning and at the end of the data files. The resolution parameters can be provided by pressing the resolution button and the required information such as sample to detector distance, collimation distance, cross section of the guide etc as shown in figure  (c) has to be entered to use resolution smearing during the fitting. The new button is use to discard all the current selections and plotted data files and starts a new session. The file could also be imported by pasting the clipboard data on the graph view as shown in the figure below. The conditions for columns are same as that for reading the file via browse method.

==Importing data files for multiple data sets==


 FIGURE DELETED The multiple data set option allows the user to perform operation on multiple data files by using common set of (global) parameters. This option is especially important in contrast variation experiments or measurements with polarized neutrons. The global fit helps to determine the fit parameters unambiguously which by analyzing a single curve would be otherwise strongly correlated. The file is imported by first going to multiple data sets for global fitting (red box in Figure ) and then via, \verb"[File|Multiple Data Set...]". This will open a new window as was the case for importing single data set as shown below. 
 FIGURE DELETED The procedure for importing the first file is same as was the case for single file. On reading the first file the merge file window opens, which has additional buttons as compared to the merge file window in single data set (shown in the red rectangular box in figure (c)). In multiple data fitting, almost any number of data files could be loaded. The present active number of data is shown next to the data set in the merge file window. One can switch over from one data file to another by clicking previous or next. Add and remove buttons are used to add or remove another file. The addition of curves for different <math>Q</math> scales is performed similar to as mentioned in the Input single data set introduction.

==Simulating scattering curves==

In addition to reading and loading data set, one can also have a realistic view of the experimental scattering data for a known structure by simulating the scattering profile beforehand to get a feel of the actual experiment scattering profile. The simulation can be performed either for a single data set or for multiple data sets using global parameters. To generate theoretical scattering profile, follow \verb"[Calc|Single Data Set...|simulate]" or \verb"[Calc|Multiple Data Sets...|simulate]", either of them to generate a single data set or multiple data sets varied by changing the global parameter. The data can be generated for vast number of form factors and structure factor included in the software. The simulation is calculated using physically relevant parameters, this is useful to plan the experiment and to know whether a given concentration and contrast would produce a measurable signal.


 FIGURE DELETED

==Fitting==

{{sasfit}} can analyse the data using both model-independent analysis and using a non-linear least square method to fit models. The model-independent analysis is a preliminary process of analyzing SAS data and does not require any advanced knowledge of the system to extract structural information this includes fittings (Guinier, Kratky, Porod, power-laws, etc.). On the other hand in case of non-linear least square methods a detailed fitting to the experimental data is performed using a wide variety of form factors and structure factors. The {{sasfit}} model library consists of large number of such functions, which can be readily used for the analysis. Moreover it can also fit different size distributions.

==Model Independent Fitting (Integral parameters)==

\ Model independent analysis requires no advanced knowledge about the sample and most importantly no experimental bias of assumed structure. It includes linearized fitting (Guinier, Porod and Zimm plot) to extract structural information. Model independent analysis are performed via \verb"[Cals|integral parameters]". In {{sasfit}} there are basically three functions available to do the analysis; they are Guinier, Zimm and Porod approximations (shown in the blue box in Figure (b). The number of data points to be included in the analysis can be accordingly varied and the resulting fit and the available parameters can be viewed instantaneously. For a large number of data a small script can be return to automate the process. This is performed by using the lower section of the integral structural parameters window (shown in red box). Prename indicates a character or string of characters with which all the data file names to be analysed starts with followed by certain numbers. The number of digits/characters in the filename could be given in the digits submission box, whereas the start number and the last file number are provided in their respective submission boxes. The step box indicates the incremental step of the file names which has to be analysed. The fitted parameters can be saved in the custom file to be viewed later. Load next file does step by step analysis of different files, whereas Do all would perform calculations on the entire file list, to be saved in the custom file for later viewing.


 FIGURE DELETED

==Model dependent analysis==

===Modeling a single data set===\\
 FIGURE DELETED For Modeling a SANS data set the {{sasfit}}-programm allows to describe experimental data with an arbitrary number of scattering objects types. Each of them can have a size distribution, whereby the user can choose over which parameter <math>a_i</math> of the form factor the integration will be performed. For example in case of a spherical shell with a core radius of <math>R</math> and a shell thickness of <math>\Delta R</math> {{sasfit}} allows to integrate either over the core radius <math>x=R</math> or the shell thickness <math>x=\Delta R</math> by marking the corresponding parameter (see option {\tt distr} in Fig . (b)). Furthermore an additional structure factor can be included for each scattering object. Several ways to account for the structure factor have been implemented like the monodisperse approximation (), decoupling approach (), local monodisperse approximation (), partial structure factor () and scaling approximation of partial structure factors (). The details are described in chapter .

Implemented size distributions, form factors and structure factors are described in chapters ,  and . Optional an additional smearing of this function with the instrument resolution function <math>R_{av}\left(Q,\langle Q\rangle\right)</math> can be activated so that

{{eqn|<math> I(\langle Q\rangle) = \int_0^\infty R_{av}\left(Q,\langle Q\rangle\right) \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}(Q) \, dQ </math>|dummy}}

A user interface shown in Fig.  is supplied to choose between the number of scattering objects and to define parameter for each of them. Next to varying the different parameters one can mark those, which one would like to fix or to vary in a fitting procedure (see option {\tt fit} in Fig. (b)) Model dependent analysis for single files are performed via \verb"[Calc|Single Data Set|Fit...]".

===Modeling multiple data sets===\\
 FIGURE DELETED The multiple data set option allows the user to perform operation on multiple data file by using common set (global) parameters. This option is especially important in contrast variation experiments or measurements with polarized neutrons. The global fitting helps the user to analyse large number of data which has a similar form or structure factor however different scaling constant. The data shown in the figure below is for a spherical monodispersed system both the data profile has identical features, except that the scaling factor between the two is of a factor of two. The data are called by the procedure explained in the input multiple data file section. The fitting of the data is performed by calling the fitting function via \verb"[Calc|Multiple Data Sets|Fit...]". The user interface for multiple data fitting has additional feature than to that for single data fitting, they are pre-factor and global parameters as shown in figure (b) red markings.


 FIGURE DELETED

The procedure for fitting the data set is similar to that mentioned in the earlier section. The only difference is to include the global parameters for each function included. The scattering profile shown in the figure (a) is for a spherical monodispersed particle, both the profiles have identical features with a scaling factor of two. The user interface for fitting shows the following window in figures . The data set number shows the active data file, whereas contribution indicates the number of scattering objects. We have selected the form factor for a spherical particle. In the global parameter a new variable is produced for both radius and {{mono|eta}} (scattering contrast). The pre-factor is kept constant at 1. A second contribution is added to the data set one by pressing add. In this case it is a background contribution, a new global parameter is introduced for it ({{mono|P3}}). A similar procedure is done for second data set where the global parameter for the radius is kept same to that for data set one, whereas new global parameters are defined for scattering contrast and background. The fitting procedure can then be started by pressing Run fit. The figures  show the graphs during the fitting process. The parameters of fitting for all the contribution can be viewed by pressing parameters of analytical size distributions (figure (d)).


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Data I/O Formats==

==Input Format==

\\

{{sasfit}} supports a simple ASCII format. Options for reading ASCII data can be set in the corresponding menu, where one can set an input format and the number of lines to be skipped at the beginning of the data file. To set an input format one has to supply a string like "{\tt xyer}". Each line which does not contain valid float numbers are skipped automatically. Each line further should at least contain as many valid numbers as the supplied format string characters. That means if the line contains only three numbers but the format string is 4 or more characters long the line will be ignored. Separators between numbers can be "white space", "tabulator", or ",". For identifying the columns the characters and their position in the string are interpreted. {\tt x}, {\tt y} and {\tt e} stands for the scattering vector <math>Q</math>, scattering intensity <math>I(Q)</math> and its error <math>\Delta I(Q)</math>, respectively. {\tt r} defines the column for a resolution parameter <math>\sigma</math>. The position of the character in the string defines which data column is assigned to <math>Q</math>, <math>I(Q)</math>, <math>\Delta I(Q)</math>, and <math>\sigma</math>. In case of double occurrence of a character the position of the last one is the significant position. Any characters not belonging to \{{\tt x},{\tt y},{\tt e},{\tt r}\} can be used to skip a column. A definition string need to contain at least the two characters {\tt x} and {\tt y}. \\

 
#[Example 1 (HMI-BerSANS format)] \\{\tiny \begin{verbatim} FileName=D0002831.200 FileDate=28-Jun-99 FileTime=11:57:16 Type=SANSDIso Title=IMF 2.651E-02, 2.372E+02, 4.650E+00 3.240E-02, 2.170E+02, 2.291E+00 3.829E-02, 1.898E+02, 1.713E+00 4.418E-02, 1.743E+02, 1.479E+00 5.007E-02, 1.528E+02, 1.318E+00 5.596E-02, 1.361E+02, 1.153E+00 \end{verbatim} } \centerline{<math>\vdots</math> \hspace{5cm}  } \\As the first lines start with a string, they will be automatically ignored. To interpret the three columns as <math>Q</math>, <math>I(Q)</math>, <math>\Delta I(Q)</math> the format string should be simply {\tt xyz}. The HMI-BerSANS format can also be read in by explicitly selecting instead of the "ASCII"-format the "HMI"-format button in the menu. \\[1cm]


#[Example 2]\\{\tiny \textcolor[rgb]{0.98,0.00,0.00}{<math>\mathbf{\lceil}</math>} \verb"      19         0         0         0         0         0         0         6"\ \verb"  0.100000E+01   0.100000E+04   0.000000E+00   0.100000E+01   0.120000E+01"\ \verb"  0.000000E+00   0.000000E+00   0.000000E+00   0.000000E+00   0.000000E+00" \textcolor[rgb]{0.98,0.00,0.00}{<math>\mathbf{\rfloor}</math>} \begin{verbatim} teflon              instrument tests 1  2.617993E-04   3.700000E+01   4.301163E+00 2  1.062462E-03   6.412500E+01   1.634587E+00 3  2.107973E-03   1.410135E+03   5.207492E+00 4  3.167636E-03   1.752197E+03   4.801586E+00 5  4.189463E-03   7.581771E+02   2.810281E+00 : : 45  1.255376E-02   1.486688E+01   2.197023E-01 46  1.360724E-02   1.204012E+01   1.927716E-01 47  1.466810E-02   1.026648E+01   1.679423E-01 \end{verbatim} } \\A definition string {\tt ixye} would ignore the leading line number at the beginning of each data line, but in the present example also the first 3 lines would also be interpreted as data points. To skip them one has to use the option for skipping leading lines in a data file. In the above case the number should be set to 3 or 4. As the 4<math>^\text{th}</math> line is anyway ignore a value of 3 is sufficient to skip non data points. \\[1cm]

If one does not skip the first 44 lines the marked lines are interpreted erroneously also as data points. The other lines at the beginning of the data file are ignored as they do not fulfill the condition that they have 3 columns containing only valid numbers.

==Export Format==

\\

 '''Example for an exported data file:'''\ {\tiny \begin{verbatim} 0.00401571,      3497.47,      90.7282,            0,   0.00401571,       260294,           -1,            0, 0.00454087,         3340,      84.9531,            0,   0.00454087,       254548,           -1,            0, 0.0050096,      3322.47,      79.6313,            0,    0.0050096,       248833,           -1,            0, 0.00552335,      2983.23,      73.7254,            0,   0.00552335,       241949,           -1,            0, 0.00598495,      2737.17,      68.4395,            0,   0.00598495,       235226,           -1,            0, 0.0065309,      2598.76,      62.3109,            0,    0.0065309,       226647,           -1,            0, 0.00706977,       2233.9,      56.4829,            0,   0.00706977,       217551,           -1,            0, 0.00764207,      2080.96,      50.6186,            0,   0.00764207,       207264,           -1,            0, 0.00815988,      1882.88,      45.6557,            0,   0.00815988,       197459,           -1,            0, \end{verbatim} \centerline{<math>\vdots</math> \hspace{5cm} <math>\vdots</math>} \begin{verbatim} ,             ,             ,             ,    0.0445634,      1535.14,           -1,            0, ,             ,             ,             ,    0.0453557,      1473.71,           -1,            0, ,             ,             ,             ,    0.0470219,      1340.34,           -1,            0, ,             ,             ,             ,    0.0490017,      1192.64,           -1,            0, ,             ,             ,             ,    0.0510837,      1055.44,           -1,            0, \end{verbatim} } If one like to export the data of an <math>xy</math>-plot all curves are stored in a single data file. Each curve will occupy four columns (<math>Q</math>, <math>I(Q)</math>, <math>\Delta I(Q)</math>, <math>\sigma</math>). If an error <math>\Delta I(Q)</math> is not available, e.g. for theoretical data curves, the corresponding column will be filled with -1. Similar is valid for the resolution parameter <math>\sigma</math> which will be set to 0 in case it is not available. The individual columns are separated by ",". If the curve have different amount of data points the column will be filled with empty space for the missing data. This comma separated data format has been chosen as it can be imported easily by many commercial plotting softwares. The drawback of this format is, however, that {{sasfit}} cannot read it correctly, if the individual curves are of different length. 

==Scattering length density calculator==

The {{mono|SLD calculator}} is using thermal neutron cross-sections only to calculate neutron scattering length density. For x-rays the energy dependent scattering coefficients <math>f'</math> and <math>f''</math> are derived using the theoretical approximation developed by Cromer and Liberman. This theory gives accurate values far from an absorption edge but does not account for the effects of neighboring atoms, which can be very substantial near an absorption edge. Before conducting an anomalous scattering experiment close to an absorption edge it is therefore advisable to determine the actual scattering behavior of the sample. The x-ray data have been taken from \url{http://skuld.bmsc.washington.edu/scatter/AS_periodic.html} and those for neutrons from \url{http://www.ncnr.nist.gov/resources/n-lengths/list.html}. 
 FIGURE DELETED The menu interface in Fig.  has for input parameters, the sum formulae of the compound, its mass density in g/cm<math>^3</math>, the x-ray energy in keV and the neutron wave length in nm. In the compound name non-integer stoichiometry is supported, e.g. H0.2O0.1 and H2O will calculate the same scattering length density. However, the molecular volume <math>v_m</math> and the molecular weight <math>M</math> of cause depend on such differences. The elements in the compound name are case sensitive. Therefore you have to use {{mono|SiO2}} instead of {{mono|SIO2}}. Also isotopes are handled like {{mono|C(13)}} (Carbon-13), {{mono|H(2)}} (Deuterium), or {{mono|O(18)}} (Oxygen-18). For Deuterium next to {{mono|H(2)}} also {{mono|D}} can be used.

Examples of how to format the compound name:  
# Magnetite: {{mono|Fe3O4}}, 5.15 g/cm<math>^3</math> 
# Eucryptite: {{mono|LiAlSiO4}}, 2.67 g/cm<math>^3</math> 
# protonated toluene, {{mono|C7H8}}, 0.865 g/cm<math>^3</math> 
# deuterated toluene, {{mono|C7D8}} or {{mono|C7H(2)8}}, 0.94 g/cm<math>^3</math> 
# mixture of 65/35 heavy water/light water, {{mono|(D2O)0.65(H2O)0.35}}, 1.065 g/cm<math>^3</math>  From the compound name and the density first the molecular weight <math>M</math>, molecular volume <math>v_m</math>, and total number of electrons <math>Z</math> are calculated. Together with the tabulated neutron scattering length and tabulated energy dependent scattering coefficient <math>f'(E)</math> and <math>f''(E)</math> the corresponding coherent neutron scattering length <math>b_c=\sum_i b_i</math>, coherent neutron scattering length density <math>\eta_{n,SLD}=b_c/v_m</math>  and for x-rays the complex energy dependent scattering scattering length density <math>\eta_{x,SLD}=\left(Z-(Z/82.5)^{2.37}+f'(E)+\imath f''(E)\right)/v_m</math> of the compound are calculated.  

==Resolution Function \cite{Pedersen1990==

}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \langle k\rangle      & = 2 \pi/\lambda\\\langle \theta\rangle  &= \arcsin(\langle Q_{av}/(2 \langle k\rangle ))\\a_1        &= \frac{r_1}{L+l/\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle )}\\a_2        &= r_2 \cos^2(2 \langle \theta\rangle )/l\\\Delta\beta_1 &= \begin{cases} a_1 \geq a_2: & \displaystyle \frac{2 r_1}{L} - \frac{r_2^2}{2 r_1} \frac{\cos^4(2\langle \theta\rangle )}{l^2L} \left(L+\frac{l}{\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle )}\right)^2\  \\a_1 < a_2: & \displaystyle 2 r_2\left(\frac{1}{L} + \frac{\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle )}{l}\right) - \frac{r_1^2}{2r_2}  \frac{l}{L}\\& \displaystyle \times \frac{1}{\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle ) \left(L+\frac{l}{\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle )}\right)} \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sigma_W  &= \langle Q\rangle \frac{\Delta\lambda}{\lambda}\frac{1}{2\sqrt{2\ln(2)}}\\\sigma_{C1} &= \langle k\rangle\cos(\langle \theta\rangle)\frac{\Delta\beta_1}{2\sqrt{2\ln(2)}} \\\sigma_{D1} &= \langle k\rangle\cos(\langle \theta\rangle)\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle) \frac{D}{l \,2\sqrt{2\ln(2)}}\\\sigma_{av} &= \langle k\rangle\cos(\langle \theta\rangle)\cos^2(2\langle \theta\rangle) \frac{\Delta D}{l \,2\sqrt{2\ln(2)}}\\\sigma &= \sqrt{\sigma^2_W+\sigma^2_{C1}+\sigma^2_{D1}+\sigma^2_{av}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math> R_{av}\left(Q,\langle Q\rangle\right) = \frac{Q}{\sigma^2} \exp\left( -\frac{1}{2}\left(Q^2+\langle Q\rangle^2\right)/\sigma^2\right) I_0(Q\langle Q\rangle/\sigma^2) </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math> I(\langle Q\rangle) = \int_0^\infty R_{av}\left(Q,\langle Q\rangle\right) \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}(Q) \, dQ </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}(Q) = \int_0^\infty N(R) F^2(Q,R) \, dR </math>|dummy}}



\chapter{Form Factors}

The different types of form factors are selected in the different submenus. Below one finds how they are ordered. The definitions of the individual form factors are defined below. Under the submenu {{mono|other functions}} all form factors under development and those functions, which are not at all form factors but which have been implemented for some other purposes are listed.

==Spheres \& Shells==

==Sphere==

 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Sphere}(Q,R) = K^2(Q,R,\Delta\eta)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The forward scattering for <math>Q=0</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\lim_{Q=0}I_\text{Sphere}(Q,R) =\left( \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \right)^2</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Sphere}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of sphere <math>R</math> 
#[- - -] not used 
#[- - -] not used 
#[{{mono|eta}}] scattering length density difference between particle and matrix <math>\Delta\eta</math> 

'''Note:'''
# The parameters {{mono|param.p[1]}} and {{mono|param.p[2]}} are not used.


 FIGURE DELETED



==Spherical Shell I==

 FIGURE DELETED

This implementation of a spherical shell is parametrised with an inner radius <math> R_2</math> and outer radius <math>R_1</math>. The scattering contrast relative to the matrix of the core is <math>\mu \Delta \eta</math> and the one of the shell <math>\Delta\eta</math>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Shell1}(Q,R_1,R_2,\Delta\eta,\mu)= \left[K(Q,R_1,\Delta\eta)-K(Q,R_2,\Delta\eta(1-\mu))\right]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The forward scattering for <math>Q=0</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\lim_{Q=0}I_\text{Shell1}(Q,R_1,R_2,\Delta\eta,\mu) = \left(\frac{4}{3}\pi \Delta\eta \left[ R_1^3 - R_2^3(1-\mu)\right]\right)^2</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Spherical Shell i}}:'''
#[{{mono|R1}}] overall radius of spherical shell <math>R_1</math> 
#[{{mono|R2}}] radius of core <math>R_2</math> 
#[{{mono|eta}}] scattering length density difference between shell and matrix <math>\Delta\eta</math> 
#[{{mono|mu}}] scattering length density difference between core and matrix relative to the shell contrast <math>\mu</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED


==Spherical Shell II==


 FIGURE DELETED

This implementation of a spherical shell is parametrised with an outer radius <math>R</math> and an inner radius <math>\nu R</math>. The scattering contrast relative to the matrix of the core is <math> \mu \Delta \eta</math> and the one of the shell <math>\Delta\eta</math>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Shell2}(Q,R,\nu,\Delta\eta,\mu)= \left(K(Q,R,\Delta\eta)-K(Q,\nu R,\Delta\eta(1-\mu))\right)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The forward scattering for <math>Q=0</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\displaystyle \lim_{Q=0}I_\text{Shell2}(Q,R,R,\Delta\eta,\mu) = \left(\frac{4}{3}\pi \Delta\eta \left[ R^3 - \nu^3 R^3(1-\mu)\right]\right)^2</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Spherical Shell ii}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] overall radius of spherical shell <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|nu}}] the radius of the core is only the fraction <math>\nu</math> of the overall radius  <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta}}] scattering length density difference between shell and matrix <math>\Delta\eta</math> 
#[{{mono|mu}}] scattering length density difference between core and matrix relative to the shell contrast <math>\mu</math> 

 FIGURE DELETED


==Spherical Shell III==

 FIGURE DELETED

This implementation of a spherical shell is parametrised with an inner radius <math>R</math> and a shell thickness <math>\Delta R</math>. The scattering contrast relative to the matrix of the core is <math> \Delta \eta_1</math> and the one of the shell <math>\Delta\eta_2</math>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Shell3}(Q,R,\Delta R,\Delta\eta_1,\Delta\eta_2)= \left[K(Q,R+\Delta R,\Delta\eta_2)-K(Q,R,\Delta\eta_2-\Delta\eta_1)\right]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The forward scattering for <math>Q=0</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\displaystyle \lim_{Q=0}I_\text{Shell3}(Q,R,\Delta R,\Delta\eta_1,\Delta\eta_2) = \left(\frac{4}{3}\pi \left[(R+\Delta R)^3\Delta\eta_2 - R^3(\Delta\eta_2-\Delta\eta_1)\right]\right)^2</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Spherical Shell iii}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of core <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|dR}}] thickness of the shell <math>\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta1}}] scattering length density difference between core and matrix <math>\Delta\eta_1</math> 
#[{{mono|eta2}}] scattering length density difference between shell and matrix <math>\Delta\eta_2</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED 



==Bilayered Vesicle==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{BLV}(Q) = \bigg( & + K(Q,R_c,\eta_{sol}-\eta_{t})+ K(Q,R_c+t_{t},\eta_{t}-\eta_{h})\\&+ K(Q,R_c+t_{t}+t_{h},\eta_{h}-\eta_{t}) + K(Q,R_c+2t_{t}+t_{h},\eta_{t}-\eta_{sol}) \bigg)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\\\underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|BilayeredVesicle}}:}\  
#[{{mono|R_c}}] radius of core <math>R_c</math> which consists of solvent 
#[{{mono|t_h}}] thickness of outer part of bilayer (in contact with solvent, head group) <math>t_\text{h}</math> 
#[{{mono|t_t}}] thickness of inner part of bilayer (tail group) <math>t_\text{t}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_h}}] scattering length density of outer part of bilayer <math>\eta_\text{h}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_t}}] scattering length density of inner part of bilayer <math>\eta_\text{t}</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED



==Multi Lamellar Vesicle==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{MLV}(Q) = \Bigg( \sum_{i=0}^{n-1} \bigg[ & K(Q,R_c+it_{sh}+it_{sol},\eta_{sol}-\eta_{sh}) \\+ &K(Q,R_c+(i+1)t_{sh}+it_{sol},\eta_{sh}-\eta_{sol}) \bigg] \Bigg)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|MultiLamellarVesicle}}:'''
#[{{mono|R_c}}] radius of core <math>R_c</math> which consists of solvent 
#[{{mono|t_sh}}] surfactant layer thickness <math>t_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|t_sol}}] thickness of solvent layer <math>t_\text{sol}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sh}}] scattering length density of surfactant layer <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 
#[{{mono|n}}] total number of surfactant layers <math>n</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED



==RNDMultiLamellarVesicle==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{RndMLV}(Q) &= \Delta\eta ^2 \sum_{i=1}^{N} F_i^2(q,R_i,t_{sh,i}) \\&+ \Delta\eta ^2 \sum_{i<j}^{N} 2 F_i(q,R_i,t_{sh,i}) F_j(q,R_j,t_{sh,j}) \frac{\sin qr_{ij}}{qr_{ij}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} r_{ij} & = {\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{R}_i-\mathbf{R}_j}\\F_i(q,R_i,t_{sol,i}) &= K(q,R_i+t_{sol,i},\Delta\eta)-K(q,R_i,\Delta\eta)\ K(q,R,\Delta\eta) &= \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin qR - qR \cos qR}{(qR)^3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R_1 &= \textrm{ran}_{lognormal}\left(\log(R_c),\sigma_{R_c}\right)\\\Delta R_{i} &= \textrm{ran}_{gaussian}\left(\sigma_{t_{sol}}\right)\\R_i &= R_{i-1}+t_{sh,i-1}+\Delta R_{i}\ \mathbf{R}_i &= R_i \; '''ran'''_{dir,3D} \text{ran}_{uniform} \; \Delta t_{sol} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|RNDMultiLamellarVesicle}}:'''
#[{{mono|t_sh}}] average surfactant layer thickness <math>t_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|s_sh}}] Gaussian thickness distribution of surfactant layer with a width of <math>\sigma_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|R_c}}] average radius of core <math>R_c</math> which consists of solvent 
#[{{mono|s_c}}] lognormal size distribution of core radius <math>R_c</math> with a width of <math>\sigma_c</math> 
#[{{mono|n}}] average number of surfactant layers <math>n</math> 
#[{{mono|s_n}}] lognormal distribution of the number of surfactant layers with a width of <math>\sigma_n</math> 
#[{{mono|t_sol}}] average thickness of solvent layer <math>t_\text{sol}</math> 
#[{{mono|s_sol}}] lognormal thickness distribution of solvent layer with a width of <math>\sigma_\text{sol}</math> 
#[{{mono|Deta_sh}}] scattering length density contrast <math>\Delta\eta</math> between surfactant layer and solvent 

'''Note:'''
#[ ] The number of Monte Carlo iterations can be set via the menu {{mono|[Options|Customize...]}}


 FIGURE DELETED



==Vesicle with aligned flat capped ends <ref name="Kaya:aj5008"> H{\aa}kon Kaya. {Scattering from cylinders with globular end-caps}. ''Journal of Applied Crystallography'', 37(2):223--230, Apr 2004.

</ref>\cite{Kaya:aj5016==




 FIGURE DELETED 
 
 The shape of this form factor consist of spherical vesicle containing to flat domains. The flat thought to be aligned in a horizontal magnetic field perpendicular to the incoming neutron beam. The size of the domains are characterized by the angles <math>\theta_1</math> and <math>theta_2</math>. The thicknesses <math>t_{c1}</math> and <math>t_{c2}</math> can be different than the thickness <math>t_{sh}</math> of the spherical part of the vesicles. The same hold for the scattering length densities <math>\eta_{c1}</math>, <math>\eta_{c2}</math> and <math>\eta_{sh}</math>. The form factor <math>F_\text{cv}(Q)</math> of this object can be calculated by performing the Fourier transformation of the scattering length density in separate steps. First one calculates the Fourier transformation of a sphere <math>F_\text{cSph}</math> with flat capped ends on each side in cylinder coordinates.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{cSph}(Q,R,\psi,\theta_1,\theta_2,\Delta\eta) = \Delta\eta \!\! \int_{-R\cos\theta_2}^{R\cos\theta_1} \!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z \!\!\! \int_0^{\sqrt{R^2-z^2}}\!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}\rho \; \int_0^{2\pi} \mathrm{d}\phi \;\; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{with} \quad \mathbf{Q}=Q\left( \begin{array}{c} 0\\\sin\psi\\\cos\psi \end{array} \right) \quad \text{and} \quad \mathbf{r}= \left( \begin{array}{c} \rho \cos\phi\\\rho \sin\phi\\z \end{array} \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The form factor of vesicle <math>F_\text{cv}(Q)</math> with a layer thickness of <math>t_\text{sh}</math> can than be calculated by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{cv}(Q,R,t_\text{sh},\theta_1,\theta_2,\Delta\eta_\text{sh}) = & + F_\text{cSph}(Q,R+t_\text{sh},\Theta_1,\Theta_2,\Delta\eta_\text{sh}) \\& - F_\text{cSph}(Q,R,            \theta_1,\theta_2,\Delta\eta_\text{sh}) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Theta_1 &= \arcsin\left(\frac{R_{c1}}{R+t_\text{sh}} \right), \quad R_{c1} = R \sin\left(\theta_1\right),\ \Theta_2 &= \arcsin\left(\frac{R_{c2}}{R+t_\text{sh}} \right), \quad R_{c2} = R \sin\left(\theta_2\right). \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

As the flat capped ends are allowed to have independent thicknesses <math>t_\text{c1}</math>, <math>t_\text{c2}</math> and scattering length densities <math>\eta_\text{1}</math>, <math>\eta_\text{2}</math> the scattering amplitude contribution of the flat capped ends, which have the shape of a disc, need to be corrected. Their contribution can be calculated by \newlength\breite \settowidth\breite{<math>\displaystyle {}+{}</math>} \begin{multline} \begin{split} F_\text{c}(Q,R,\theta_1,\theta_2,\dots) = & \hspace{\breite} F_\text{c1}(Q,R,\theta_1,\Delta\eta_\text{c1}) - F_\text{d1}(Q,R,t_\text{d1},\Delta\eta_\text{sh})\\& +                F_\text{c2}(Q,R,\theta_2,\Delta\eta_\text{c2}) - F_\text{d2}(Q,R,t_\text{d2},\Delta\eta_\text{sh}) \end{split} \\[2mm] \begin{split} = & \hspace{\breite} \Delta\eta_\text{c1} \!\!\! \int_{l_\text{c1}}^{l_\text{c1}+t_\text{c1}} \!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z\int_0^{R_\text{c1}} \!\!  \mathrm{d}\rho \int_0^{2\pi}\!\! \mathrm{d}\phi \; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho - \Delta\eta_\text{sh} \!\!\! \int_{l_\text{c1}}^{l_\text{c1}+t_\text{d1}} \!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z\int_0^{R_\text{c1}} \!\! \mathrm{d}\rho \int_0^{2\pi}\!\! \mathrm{d}\phi \; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho \\& + \Delta\eta_\text{c2} \!\!\!\!\!\!\! \int_{-(l_\text{c2}+t_\text{c2})}^{-l_\text{c2}} \!\!\!\!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z\int_0^{R_\text{c2}} \!\! \mathrm{d}\rho \int_0^{2\pi}\!\!\mathrm{d}\phi \; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho - \Delta\eta_\text{sh} \!\!\!\!\!\!\! \int_{-(l_\text{c2}+t_\text{d2})}^{-l_\text{c2}} \!\!\!\!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z\int_0^{R_\text{c2}} \!\! \mathrm{d}\rho  \int_0^{2\pi}\!\!\mathrm{d}\phi \; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho  \end{split} \end{multline} with 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Delta\eta_\text{sh} &= \eta_\text{sh}-\eta_\text{sol}, \, \Delta\eta_\text{c1} = \eta_1-\eta_\text{sol} , \, \Delta\eta_\text{c2} = \eta_2-\eta_\text{sol}\\l_\text{c1} &= R\cos{\theta_1}, \, l_\text{c2} = R\cos{\theta_2}\\R_\text{c1} &= R\sin{\theta_1}, \, R_\text{c2} = R\sin{\theta_2}\\t_\text{d1} &= \sqrt{\left(R+t_\text{sh}\right)^2-R_\text{c1}^2}-\sqrt{R^2-R_\text{c1}^2}\\t_\text{d2} &= \sqrt{\left(R+t_\text{sh}\right)^2-R_\text{c2}^2}-\sqrt{R^2-R_\text{c2}^2}\\\end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The solution of the integrals in eq.  and  are \begin{subequations} \begin{multline} F_\text{cSph}(Q,R,\psi,\theta_1,\theta_2,\Delta\eta) = \Delta\eta \!\! \int_{-R\cos\theta_2}^{R\cos\theta_1} \!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z \!\!\! \int_0^{\sqrt{R^2-z^2}}\!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}\rho \; \int_0^{2\pi} \mathrm{d}\phi \;\; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho \\\Delta\eta \!\! \int_{-R\cos\theta_2}^{R\cos\theta_1} \!\!\!\!\! \mathrm{d}z\;\; \exp\left(\imath Q z \cos\psi\right)  2\pi \left(R^2 - z^2\right) \frac{J_1\left(Q \sqrt{R^2 - z^2} \sin\psi\right)}{Q \sqrt{R^2 - z^2} \sin\psi}  \end{multline} and \begin{multline} F_\textrm{c<math>_i</math>,d<math>_i</math>}(Q,R_\textrm{c<math>_i</math>,d<math>_i</math>},\psi,\Delta\eta)   = \Delta\eta \int_{a}^{b}\!\! \mathrm{d}z \int_0^{R_\textrm{c<math>_i</math>}}\!\! \mathrm{d}\rho \int_0^{2\pi}\!\! \mathrm{d}\phi \;\; e^{\imath \mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}} \, \rho\\= 4 \pi R \frac{\imath \left(\exp\left(\imath a Q \cos\psi\right)-\exp\left(\imath b Q \cos\psi\right)\right) J_1\left(Q R \sin\psi\right)}{\sin\left(2\psi\right) Q^2} \end{multline} \end{subequations} whereby <math>\mathrm{J}_1</math> the regular cylindrical Bessel function of first order. The overall scattering intensity <math>I_{\textrm{alignedVes}}(Q,\psi,\dots)</math> is finally given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_{\textrm{alignedVes}}(Q,\psi,\dots) = {\left\vert \right\vert}{ F_\text{cv}(Q,R,\psi,t_\text{sh},\theta_1,\theta_2,\Delta\eta_\text{sh}) +F_\text{c}(Q,R,\psi,\theta_1,\theta_2,\dots)}^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for the models of {{mono|MagneticFieldAlignedVesicle}}:'''
#[{{mono|Rsh}}] radius of spherical vesicle shell 
#[{{mono|theta1}}] angle to describe size of first capped side 
#[{{mono|theta2}}] angle to describe size of second capped side 
#[{{mono|t_sh}}] thickness of spherical vesicle shell 
#[{{mono|t_c1}}] thickness of first flat capped side 
#[{{mono|t_c2}}] thickness of second flat capped side 
#[{{mono|eta_sh}}] scattering length density of spherical vesicle shell 
#[{{mono|eta_1}}] scattering length density of first capped side 
#[{{mono|eta_2}}] scattering length density of second capped side 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density of solvent

==Spherical shell with linear varying contrast profile (LinShell)==

 FIGURE DELETED

Form factor of a spherical shell with a core radius <math>R</math> and a shell thickness of <math>\Delta R</math>. Here a linear contrast profile within the shell has been assumed.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Delta\eta(r)      & = \begin{cases} \eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sol} & \text{ for } r<R\\m r + b  & \text{ for } r \in [R,R+\Delta R]\\0  & \text{ for } r>R+\Delta R \end{cases}\ m           & = (\eta_\text{sh_out}-\eta_\text{sh_in}) / \Delta R\\b           & = -m R + \eta_\text{sh_in} - \eta_\text{sol}\\\eta_\text{sh_in}  & = (1 - x_\text{in,sol})  \, \eta_\text{sh} + x_\text{in,sol} \,\eta_\text{sol}-\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh_out} & = (1 - x_\text{out,sol}) \, \eta_\text{sh} + x_\text{out,sol}\,\eta_\text{sol}-\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R+\Delta R</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh}      & : \text{scattering length density of pure shell material} \\\eta_\text{c}       & : \text{scattering length density of core}  \\|dummy}}

\end{align}</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{sph}(A,x) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi x^3 \,\, 3\frac{\sin(A)-A\cos(A)}{A^3} \\[5mm] F_\text{shlin}(A,x) & = 4\pi x^4 \frac{2\cos(A)+2A\sin(A)-A^2\cos(A)}{A^4} \\[5mm] I_\text{LinShell}    & = \big[ \,\, (\eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sol}-b)F_\text{sph}(QR,R) \\& \hspace{6mm} - mF_\text{shlin}(QR,R)\\& \hspace{6mm} + mF_\text{shlin}\left(Q(R+\Delta R),R+\Delta R\right) \\& \hspace{6mm} + bF_\text{sph}\left(Q(R+\Delta R),R+\Delta R\right) \big]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|LinShell}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of core <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|dR}}] thickness of the shell <math>\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_c}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{c}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sh}}] scattering length density of non-swollen shell <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|x_in}}] amount of solvent <math>x_\text{in,sol}</math> on core-shell interface at <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|x_out}}] amount of solvent <math>x_\text{out,sol}</math> on shell-solvent interface at <math>R+\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 

'''Note:'''
# <math>x_\text{in,sol}</math> and <math>x_\text{out,sol}</math> are only physical for values between 0 and 1.

==LinShell 2==


 FIGURE DELETED

Form factor of a spherical shell with a total radius <math>R_\text{tot}</math> and a shell thickness of <math>\Delta R</math>. The definition are the same than for {\tt LinShell} except that instead of the core radius <math>R</math> now the total radius <math>R_\text{tot}</math> is used to parameterize the form factor. The parameter definitions are the following:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R&=R_\text{tot}-\Delta R\\\Delta\eta(r)      & = \begin{cases} \eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sol} & \text{ for } r<R\\m r + b  & \text{ for } r \in [R,R_\text{tot}]\\0 & \text{ for } r>R_\text{tot} \end{cases}\\m           & = (\eta_\text{sh_out}-\eta_\text{sh_in}) / \Delta R\\b           & = -m R + \eta_\text{sh_in} - \eta_\text{sol}\\\eta_\text{sh_in}  & = (1 - x_\text{in,sol})  \, \eta_\text{sh} + x_\text{in,sol} \,\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh_out} & = (1 - x_\text{out,sol}) \, \eta_\text{sh} + x_\text{out,sol}\,\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R_\text{tot}=R+\Delta R</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh}      & : \text{scattering length density of pure shell material} \\\eta_\text{c}       & : \text{scattering length density of core} \\x_\text{in,sol}     & : \text{amount of solvent at <math>R</math>} \\x_\text{out,sol}    & : \text{amount of solvent at <math>R_\text{tot}=R+\Delta R</math>} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{sph}(A,x) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi x^3 \,\, 3\frac{\sin(A)-A\cos(A)}{A^3} \\[5mm] F_\text{shlin}(A,x) & = 4\pi x^4 \frac{2\cos(A)+2A\sin(A)-A^2\cos(A)}{A^4} \\[5mm] I_\text{LinShell2}    & = \big[ \,\, (\eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sol}-b)F_\text{sph}(QR,R) \\& \hspace{6mm} - mF_\text{shlin}(QR,R)\\& \hspace{6mm} + mF_\text{shlin}\left(QR_\text{tot},R_\text{tot}\right) \\& \hspace{6mm} + bF_\text{sph}\left(QR_\text{tot},R_\text{tot}\right) \big]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}


'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|LinShell2}}:'''
#[{{mono|Rtot}}] total overall radius <math>R_\text{tot}</math> 
#[{{mono|dR}}] thickness of the shell <math>\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_c}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{c}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sh}}] scattering length density of non-swollen shell <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|x_in}}] amount of solvent <math>x_\text{in,sol}</math> on core-shell interface at <math>R</math> <math>(x_\text{in,sol} \in [0;1])</math> 
#[{{mono|x_out}}] amount of solvent <math>x_\text{out,sol}</math> on shell-solvent interface at <math>R+\Delta R</math> <math>(x_\text{out,sol} \in [0;1])</math>. 
#[{{mono|eta_s}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 

'''Note:'''
# <math>x_\text{in,sol}</math> and <math>x_\text{out,sol}</math> are only physical for values between 0 and 1.



==ExpShell==


 FIGURE DELETED



{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \eta_\text{ExpShell}(r,R_c,\Delta R,\alpha,\phi_\text{in},\phi_\text{out}) &= \begin{cases} \eta_c              & r \leq R_c \\\eta_\text{exp}(\frac {r-R_c}{\Delta R})   & R_c < r< R_c+\Delta R\\\eta_\text{sol}     & r> R_c+\Delta R \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \eta_\text{exp} (x) &= \begin{cases} \eta_\text{sh,in}  + \left[\eta_\text{sh,out}-\eta_\text{sh,in}\right] x \exp\left( \left[ 1-x \right] \alpha\right) & \alpha<0 \\[2mm] \left[\eta_\text{sh,in}-\eta_\text{sh,out}\right] \left[1-x\right] \exp\left(-{x\alpha}\right) + \eta_\text{sh,out}& \alpha \geq 0 \end{cases} \\[2mm] \eta_\text{sh,in} & = \left[ \phi_\text{in}\,\eta_\text{sol}+ \left( 1-\phi_\text{in} \right) \eta_\text{sh} \right]\\\eta_\text{sh,out} & = \left[ \phi_\text{out}\,\eta_\text{sol}+ \left( 1-\phi_\text{out} \right) \eta_\text{sh} \right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The scattering intensity for the radial symmetric scattering length density profile <math>\eta_\text{ExpShell}(r)</math> can be calculated analytical. The integral needed to be solved for that is

{{eqn|<math> I_\text{ExpShell}(Q) = \int_0^\infty 4\pi r^2 \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr}\, \eta_\text{ExpShell}(r)\, dr </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R_c &=\text{core radius}\\\Delta R &=\text{shell thickness}\\\eta_\text{sh,in}  & = (1 - \phi_\text{in,sol})  \, \eta_\text{sh} + \phi_\text{in,sol} \,\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R_c</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh,out}     & = (1 - \phi_\text{out,sol}) \, \eta_\text{sh} + \phi_\text{out,sol}\,\eta_\text{sol}\\& : \text{scattering length density at <math>R_c+\Delta R</math>} \\\eta_\text{sh}      & : \text{scattering length density of pure shell material} \\\eta_\text{c}       & : \text{scattering length density of core} \\\phi_\text{in}     & : \text{amount of solvent  at <math>R_c</math>} \\\phi_\text{out}    & : \text{amount of solvent at <math>R_c+\Delta R</math>} \\\alpha             & : \text{parameter for exponential diffuse profile of the shell} \\|dummy}}

\end{align}</math>



\ \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|ExpShell}}:}\  
#[{{mono|R_core}}] radius of core  <math>R_c</math> 
#[{{mono|DR}}] thickness of the shell <math>\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_core}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{c}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_shell}}] scattering length density of non-swollen shell <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|x_in_sol}}] amount of solvent <math>\phi_\text{in}</math> on core-shell interface at <math>r=R</math> 
#[{{mono|x_out_sol}}] amount of solvent <math>\phi_\text{out}</math> on shell-solvent interface at <math>r=R+\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|alpha}}]  a parameter (<math>\alpha</math>) which describes the penetration profile of the solvent into the shell. A value of <math>\alpha=0</math> means linear profile and is equivalent to {\tt LinShell} and {\tt LinShell2}. For <math>\alpha>0</math> the solvent penetrates further into the shell and for <math>\alpha<0</math> less. 
#[{{mono|eta_solvent}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 

\underline{Note:}  
# <math>\phi_\text{in}</math> and <math>\phi_\text{out}</math> are only physical for values between 0 and 1.

==Ellipsoidal Objects==

==Ellipsoid with two equal semi-axis <math>R</math> and semi-principal axes <math>\nu R</math>==

 FIGURE DELETED

An ellipsoid is a quadric surface in three dimensions obtained by rotating an ellipse about one of its principal axes. Three particular cases of an ellipsoid are:  
# If the ellipse is rotated about its major axis, the surface is a prolate spheroid. 
# If the ellipse is rotated about its minor axis, the surface is an oblate spheroid. 
# If the generating ellipse is a circle, the surface is a sphere.


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{ii}(Q,R,\nu) = \left( \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \right)^2 \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}}\! K^2\left(Q,R\sqrt{\nu^2\cos^2\Theta+\sin^2\Theta}\right)\, d\Theta \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\displaystyle \lim_{Q=0} I_\text{ii}(Q,R,\nu)= \left( \frac{4}{3}\pi \nu R^3 \Delta\eta \right)^2 </math>

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Ellipsoid ii}}:''' 
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of the rotational axes 
#[{{mono|nu}}] ratio between radius of the semi-principle axes and equatorial axis. Values of <math>\nu<1</math> describe a oblate ellipsoid, a value of <math>\nu=1</math> a sphere, and <math>\nu>1</math> a prolate ellipsoid. 


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Ellipsoid with two equal equatorial semi-axis <math>R</math> and volume <math>V</math>==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{i}(Q,R,\nu) = \left( V \Delta\eta \right)^2 \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}}\! K^2\left(Q,R\sqrt{\nu^2\cos^2\Theta+\sin^2\Theta}\right)\, d\Theta \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\nu=\frac{V}{R^3}\frac{3}{4\pi} \quad \mbox{so that}\quad V =\frac{4}{3}\pi\nu R^3</math>|dummy}}

and <math>\displaystyle \lim_{Q=0} I_\text{i}(Q,R,\nu)= \left(V\Delta\eta\right)^2</math>

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Ellipsoid i}}:''' 
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of the rotational axes 
#[{{mono|V}}] total volume of the ellipsoid.

==Ellipsoidal core shell structure==

 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{ECSh}(Q) = \left\langle F^2(Q,\mu) \right\rangle & = \int_0^1 \left[F(Q,\mu)\right]^2 d\mu\\\left\langle F(Q,\mu) \right\rangle^2 & = \left[\int_0^1 F(Q,\mu) d\mu \right]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F(Q,\mu) &= \left(\eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sh}\right) V_c\left[ \frac{3j_1(x_c)}{x_c}\right] +\left(\eta_\text{sh}-\eta_\text{sol}\right) V_t\left[ \frac{3j_1(x_t)}{x_t}\right]\\j_1(x) &= \frac{\sin(x)-x\cos(x)}{x^2} \\x_c &= Q \sqrt{a^2\mu^2+b^2(1-\mu^2)} \\x_c &= Q \sqrt{(a+t)^2\mu^2+(b+t)^2(1-\mu^2)} \\V_c &= \frac{4}{3}\pi ab^2 \\V_t &= \frac{4}{3}\pi (a+t)(b+t)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \eta_\text{c} &: \text{scattering length density of core} \\\eta_\text{sh} &: \text{scattering length density of shell} \\\eta_\text{sol} &: \text{scattering length density of solvent} \\a &: \text{semi-principal axes of elliptical core} \\b &: \text{equatorial semi-axis of elliptical core} \\t &: \text{thickness of shell} \\V_c &: \text{volume of core} \\V_t &: \text{total volume of core along with shell} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==triaxial ellipsoidal core shell structure==

 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{triaxEllSh}(Q) &= \int^1_0 \int ^1_0 dx\,dy\, K_\text{sh}^2(Q,R,R_t)\ K(QR)         &= 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR\cos QR}{(QR)^3}\\K_\text{sh}(Q,R,R_t) &= \left(\eta_\text{c}-\eta_\text{sh}\right)K(QR)+\left(\eta_\text{sh}-\eta_\text{sol}\right)K(QR_t)\\R^2 &= \left[a^2\cos^2\left(\pi x/2\right) + b^2\sin^2\left(\pi x/2\right)\right](1-y^2)+c^2y^2 \\R_t^2 &= \left[(a+t)^2\cos^2\left(\pi x/2\right) + (b+t)^2\sin^2\left(\pi x/2\right)\right](1-y^2)+(c+t)^2y^2\\V_c &= \frac{4}{3}\pi abc \\V_t &= \frac{4}{3}\pi (a+t)(b+t)(c+t) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \eta_\text{c} &: \text{scattering length density of core} \\\eta_\text{sh} &: \text{scattering length density of shell} \\\eta_\text{sol} &: \text{scattering length density of solvent} \\a &: \text{semi-axes of elliptical core} \\b &: \text{semi-axes of elliptical core} \\c &: \text{semi-axes of elliptical core} \\t &: \text{thickness of shell} \\V_c &: \text{volume of core} \\V_t &: \text{total volume of core along with shell} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\vspace{3cm} \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|triaxEllShell1}}:}  
#[{{mono|a}}] semi-axes of elliptical core <math>a</math> 
#[{{mono|b}}] semi-axes of elliptical core <math>b</math> 
#[{{mono|c}}] semi-axes of elliptical core <math>c</math> 
#[{{mono|t}}] thickness of shell <math>t</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_c}}] scattering length density of core <math>\eta_\text{c}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sh}}] scattering length density of shell <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Polymers and Micelles==

==Gaussian chain==

 FIGURE DELETED

Consider a flexible polymer coil where each monomer located at a distance <math>\mathbf{R}_m</math> its scattering field amplitude is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F(\mathbf{q},t) &= \sum_{m=1}^N e^{-\imath \mathbf{q}\cdot\mathbf{R}_m(t)} . \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The scattering intensity averaged over all molecule configurations reads

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \left\langle {\left\vert \right\vert}{F(\mathbf{q})}^2\right\rangle &= \sum_{m,n} \left\langle e^{-\imath\mathbf{q}\cdot(\mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n)} \right\rangle \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

As the monomer segments <math>\mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n</math> are Gaussian distributed the averages <math>\langle\cdots\rangle</math> can be written as \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \left\langle e^{-\imath\mathbf{q}\cdot(\mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n)} \right\rangle &= e^{\frac{q^2}{6}\left\langle \left( \mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n\right)^2\right\rangle}\\&= e^{-\frac{q^2b^2}{6}{\left\vert m-n\right\vert}^{2\nu}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} Here <math>b</math> is the statistical segment length and the contour length <math>L</math> equals <math>L=Nb</math>. The average of the segment inter-distances squares is kept in the general form

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \left\langle \left( \mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n\right)^2\right\rangle &= b^2 {\left\vert m-n\right\vert}^{2\nu}. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>\nu</math> is the excluded volume parameter from the Flory mean field theory\footnote{P.J. Flory, "Statistical Mechanics of Chain Molecules", Interscience Publishers (1969)}\footnote{\href{http://www.ncnr.nist.gov/staff/hammouda/the_SANS_toolbox.pdf}{Boualem Hammouda, {{mono|the_SANS_toolbox.pdf}}}} of polymer solutions. The radius of gyration <math>R_G</math> is given by \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R_G^2 &= \frac{1}{2N^2}\sum_{m,n}^N \left\langle \left( \mathbf{R}_m-\mathbf{R}_n\right)^2\right\rangle\\&= \frac{1}{2N^2}\sum_{m,n}^N b^2 {\left\vert m-n\right\vert}^{2\nu}\\&= \frac{b^2}{N} \sum_k^N \left(1-\frac{k}{N}\right) k^{2\nu}\\&= \frac{b^2}{\left(2\nu+1\right)\left(2\nu+2\right)} N^{2\nu} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} Three cases are relevant:


# Self-avoiding walk corresponds to swollen chains with <math>\nu = 3/5</math>, for which <math>R_G^2=\frac{25}{176}b^2N^{6/5}</math>. 
# Pure random walk corresponds to chains in <math>\Theta</math>-conditions (where solvent-solvent, monomer-monomer and solvent-monomer interactions are equivalent) with <math>\nu = 1/2</math>, for which <math>R_G^2=\frac{1}{6}b^2N</math>. 
# Self attracting walk corresponds to collapsed chains with <math>\nu = 1/3</math>, for which <math>R_G^2=\frac{9}{40}b^2N^{2/3}</math>.  Using the general identity

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sum_{i,j}^N y({\left\vert i-j\right\vert}) = N+2\sum_{k=1}^N(N-k) y(k) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

the form factor reads

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P(q) &= \frac{1}{N^2}{\left\vert F(q)\right\vert}^2 = \frac{1}{N^2} \left\{N+2\sum_{k=1}^N(N-k)e^{-\frac{q^2b^2}{6}k^{2\nu}} \right\} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Going to the continuous limit <math>(N \gg 1)</math>, one obtains: \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P(q) &= 2\int_0^1 \mathrm{d}x \; (1-x)e^{-\frac{q^2b^2}{6}N^{2\nu}x^{2\nu}}\\&= \frac{U^{\frac{1}{2 \nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu}\right)- \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu}\right)-U^{\frac{1}{2\nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu},U\right)+\Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu},U\right)}{\nu U^{1/\nu}}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} with the modified variable

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} U&=\frac{q^2b^2N^{2\nu}}{6} = \left(2\nu+1\right)\left(2\nu+2\right)\frac{q^2R_G^2}{6} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and the unnormalized incomplete Gamma Function <math>\Gamma(a,x) = \int_x^\infty \mathrm{d}t \; t^{a-1} \exp(-t)</math> for <math>a</math> real and <math>x \geq 0</math> and the Gamma function <math>\Gamma(a)=\Gamma(a,0)=\int_0^\infty \mathrm{d}t\;  t^{a-1} \exp(-t)</math>. Polymer chains follow Gaussian statistics in polymer solutions: they are swollen in good solvents <math>\nu=3/5</math>, are thermally relaxed in "theta"-solvents <math>\nu=1/2</math> and partially precipitate in poor solvents <math>\nu=1/3</math>. The familiar Debye function is recovered when <math>\nu = 1/2</math>. The asymptotic limit at large <math>q</math>-values of the generalized Gaussian chain is dominated by the <math>\frac{1}{\nu U^{\frac{1}{2\nu}}}\Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2\nu}\right)</math> term which varies like <math>U^{-1/(2\nu)} \sim q^{-1/\nu}</math>. For <math>\nu =1</math> we get the limit of an infinitesimal thin rod and for <math>\nu=1/4</math> a compact object with a Porod law of <math>q^{-4}</math>.

{{sasfit}} has implemented the generalized form of a Gaussian ({{mono|generalized Gaussian coil}}) coil and the standard Debye formula {{mono|Gauss}}. In both cases three version are implemented which only differ in their parametrization of the forward scattering. In case of the the Debye-formula also the polydisperse {{mono|GaussPoly}} is implemented.

\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,1.00,1.00}{Gauss}\ \subsubsection{Gauss <ref name="Debye1947"> P. Debye. Mollecular-weight determination by light scattering. ''Journal of Physical and Colloid Chemistry'', 51:18--32, 1947.

</ref>}  \\Flexible polymer chains which are not selfavoiding and obey Gaussian statistics. Debye (1947) has calculated the form factor of such chains:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Gauss}(q) &= I_0 2\frac{\exp(-u)+u-1}{u^2}\\u &= q^2R_g^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Gauss}}:}  
#[{{mono|Rg}}] radius of gyration <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> for <math>q=0</math>  

\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,1.00,1.00}{Gauss}\ ===Gauss2 <ref name="Debye1947"/>===  \\This form factor differs only by the parametrization for the forward scattering <math>I_0=(b_p-V\eta_\text{s})^2</math> from the Debye formula in eq.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Gauss2}(q) &= \beta^2 2\frac{\exp(-u)+u-1}{u^2}\\u &= q^2R_g^2 \\\beta &= b_p-V\eta_\text{s}, \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>b_p</math> is the scattering length of a polymer molecule of molecular volume <math>V</math> dissolved in a solvent of scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{s}</math> from which the excess scattering length of a polymer molecule <math>\beta</math> can be calculated. Combining this form factor with a {{mono|Delta}} size distribution  is needed to scale the scattering intensity. With proper values for the form factor the parameter <math>N</math> of the {{mono|Delta}}-distribution yields the particle number density.

 \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Gauss2}}:}  
#[{{mono|Rg}}] radius of gyration <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|b_p}}] scattering length of polymer <math>b_p</math> in [cm] 
#[{{mono|V}}] molecular volume of a single polymer molecule <math>V</math> in [cm<math>^3</math>] 
#[{{mono|eta_s}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{s}</math> in [cm<math>^{-1}</math>] 

\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,1.00,1.00}{Gauss}\ ===Gauss3 <ref name="Debye1947"/>===  \\This form factor differs only by the parametrization for the forward scattering <math>I_0=(b_p-\frac{M_w}{N_a\rho_p}\eta_\text{s})^2</math> from the Debye formula in eq.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Gauss3}(q) &= \beta^2 2\frac{\exp(-u)+u-1}{u^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} u &= q^2R_g^2 \\\beta &= b_p-V\eta_\text{s}\\V &= \frac{M_w}{N_a\rho_p} \\N_a &= \mbox{Avogadro number} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Gauss3}}:}  
#[{{mono|Rg}}] radius of gyration <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|b_p}}] scattering length of polymer <math>b_p</math> in [cm] 
#[{{mono|M_w}}] molecular weight of polymer <math>M_w</math> in [g/mol] 
#[{{mono|rho_p}}] mass density of polymer <math>\rho_p</math> in [g cm<math>^{-3}</math>] 
#[{{mono|eta_s}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>\eta_\text{s}</math> in [cm<math>^{-1}</math>] 


 FIGURE DELETED

\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,1.00,1.00}{Gauss}\ \subsubsection{Polydisperse flexible polymers with Gaussian statistics <ref name="Pedersen2002"> Jan Skov Pedersen. ''Neutrons, X-Rays and Light'', chapter Modelling of Small-Angle Scattering Data from Colloids and Polymer Systems, pages 391--420. Elsevier Science, 2002.

</ref>}   \\Polydispersity has been included in terms of a SchulzZimm mass distribution by Zimm (1948) <ref name="zimm:1093"> Bruno H. Zimm. The scattering of light and the radial distribution function of high polymer solutions. ''The Journal of Chemical Physics'', 16(12):1093--1099, 1948.</ref>  and Greschner (1973) <ref name="Greschner1973"> Georg S. Greschner. Mathematical properties of polydisperse systems, 2. application to light scattering. ''Die Makromolekulare Chemie'', 170(1):203--229, 1973.

</ref>  \\

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{GaussPoly}(q) &= I_0 2 \frac{\left( 1+U x\right)^{-1/U}+x-1}{(1+U)x^2}\\x &= q^2R_g^2/(1+2U) \\U &= \frac{M_w}{M_n} -1 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|GaussPoly}}:}

#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> for <math>q=0</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED

\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,1.00,1.00}{Gauss}\ ===generalalized Gaussian coil <ref name="Hammouda"> B. Hammouda. the sans toolbox. \href{http://www.ncnr.nist.gov/staff/hammouda/the_SANS_toolbox.pdf==={<math>\dots</math>/the_SANS_toolbox.pdf}.

</ref>}  \\The scattering function for the generalized Gaussian coil is according to eq.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{gGc}(q) &= I_0\frac{U^{\frac{1}{2 \nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu}\right)- \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu}\right)-U^{\frac{1}{2\nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu},U\right)+\Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu},U\right)}{\nu U^{1/\nu}}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with the modified variable

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} U&= \left(2\nu+1\right)\left(2\nu+2\right)\frac{q^2R_G^2}{6} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and the unnormalized incomplete Gamma Function <math>\Gamma(a,x) = \int_x^\infty \mathrm{d}t \; t^{a-1} \exp(-t)</math> and the Gamma function <math>\Gamma(a)=\Gamma(a,0)=\int_0^\infty \mathrm{d}t\;  t^{a-1} \exp(-t)</math>. <math>\nu</math> is the excluded volume parameter from the Flory mean field theory and typical values for them are  
#[<math>\nu=1/3</math>] partially precipitate in poor solvents 
#[<math>\nu=1/2</math>] thermally relaxed in "theta"-solvents 
#[<math>\nu=3/5</math>] swollen in good solvents 

 \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|generalized Gaussian coil}}:}

===generalized Gaussian coil 2 <ref name="Hammouda"/>===  \\The scattering function for the generalized Gaussian coil is according to eq.  and differs only by the parametrization for the forward scattering <math>I_0=(b_p-V\eta_\text{s})^2</math> from the formula in eq.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{gGc2}(q) &= \left(b_p-V\eta_\text{s}\right)^2\frac{U^{\frac{1}{2 \nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu}\right)- \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu}\right)-U^{\frac{1}{2\nu}} \Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2 \nu},U\right)+\Gamma\left(\frac{1}{\nu},U\right)}{\nu U^{1/\nu}}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with the modified variable

==Star polymer with Gaussian statistic according to Benoit==

* [*Benoit 1953] H. Benoit, ''On the effect of branching and polydispersity on the angular distribution of the light scattered by gaussian coils'', J. Polym. Sci., 11:507--510, 1953

 FIGURE DELETED

[Benoit 1953] derived an expression for the scattering from branched or star polymers with a number of arms <math>f</math>, which can be expressed in the following way:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Star}(Q,R_G,f)= I_0 \frac{2}{f\nu^2} \left( \nu-\left[1-e^{-\nu}\right]+\frac{f-1}{2}\left[1-e^{-\nu}\right]^2\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\displaystyle u=R_G^2Q^2</math>, <math>\displaystyle \nu=\frac{uf}{3f-2}</math> and <math>\displaystyle \lim_{Q=0}I_\text{Star}(Q,R_G,f)=I_0</math>. <math>f</math> denotes the number of arms and <math>R_G</math> the Guinier radius of a single arm.

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Benoit}}:'''
#[{{mono|RG}}] radius of gyration of the star polymer <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|f}}] number of arms <math>f</math> 
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> for <math>q=0</math> 

 FIGURE DELETED

==Polydisperse star polymer with Gaussian statistics==

[Burchard 1974]
* [*Burchard 1974] W. Burchard, ''Statistics of star-shaped molecules. i. stars with polydisperse side chains'', Macromolecules, 7:835--841, 1974

 FIGURE DELETED

For a SchulzFlory (most probable) distribution (SchulzZimm distribution with <math>z = 1</math>) for the mass distribution of the arms, [Burchard 1974] has given the form factor:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{PolydisperseStar}(Q) &= I_0 \frac{1+\frac{u^2}{3 f}}{\left(1+\frac{u^2(f +1)}{6 f }\right)^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>f</math> is the number of arms and <math>u^2 = \langle R_g^2 \rangle_z \,Q^2</math>, where <math>\langle R_g^2 \rangle_z</math> is the <math>z</math>-average radius of gyration squared of an arm.

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|PolydisperseStar}}:'''
#[{{mono|R_G}}] radius of gyration <math>R_G</math> 
#[{{mono|f}}] number of arms <math>f</math> 
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> 

 FIGURE DELETED

==Star polymer according to Dozier==

[Dozier 1991]
* [*Dozier 1991] W. D. Dozier, J. S. Huang, and L. J. Fetters, ''Colloidal nature of star polymer dilute and semidilute solutions'', Macromolecules, 24(10):2810--2814, 1991

===Dozier===

 FIGURE DELETED 

Branched polymers having all branches emanating from the center of the macromolecule are commonly called star polymers. For a star polymer [Dozier 1991] has developed a scattering function which reads:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{DozierStar}(Q,I_0,R_G,\alpha,\nu,\xi) & = I_0 \exp\left(-\frac{Q^2R_G^2}{3}\right)\\+& \frac{4\pi\alpha}{Q\xi} \Gamma(\mu) \frac{\sin(\mu\arctan(Q\xi))}{(1+Q^2\xi^2)^{\mu/2}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\mu=1/\nu-1</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R_G      & : \text{radius of gyration} \\I_0      & : \text{scale parameter} \\\alpha   & : \text{scale parameter for fractal term} \\\xi      & : \text{exponential damping length in mass fractal} \\\nu      & : \text{Flory exponent, 3/5 in good solvent, 1/2 in theta solvent (i.e. <math>\mu=2/3</math> to 1)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Dozier}}:'''
#[{{mono|R_G}}] radius of gyration <math>R_G</math> 
#[{{mono|I_0}}] scale parameter <math>I_0</math> 
#[{{mono|alpha}}] scale parameter for fractal term <math>\alpha</math> 
#[{{mono|xi}}] exponential damping length in mass fractal <math>\xi</math> 
#[{{mono|nu}}] excluded volume parameter or Flory exponent <math>\nu</math>  

 FIGURE DELETED

===Dozier 2===

This is a re-parametrization of the {{mono|Dozier}} form factor to scale the scattering of the overall star to the local scattering of the individual arms.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{DozierStar2}(Q,I_0,R_G,N_\text{agg},\nu,\xi)= \frac{I_0}{N_\text{agg}} \Biggl( &  (N_\text{agg}-1) \exp\left(-\frac{Q^2R_G^2}{3}\right)\\+&  \frac{\Gamma(\mu)}{Q\xi} \frac{\sin(\mu\arctan(Q\xi))}{(1+Q^2\xi^2)^{\mu/2}} \Biggr) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\mu=1/\nu-1</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R_G      & : \text{radius of gyration of the star} \\I_0      & : \text{scale parameter} \\N_\text{agg} & : \text{number of arms in the star} \\\xi      & : \text{exponential damping length in mass fractal} \\\nu      & : \text{Flory exponent, 3/5 in good solvent, 1/2 in theta solvent (i.e. <math>\mu=2/3</math> to 1)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Dozier2}}:'''
#[{{mono|R_G}}] radius of gyration of the star <math>R_G</math> 
#[{{mono|I_0}}] scale parameter <math>I_0</math> 
#[{{mono|Nagg}}]  number of arms <math>N_\text{agg}</math> in the star from which the scale parameter for fractal term is calculated 
#[{{mono|xi}}] exponential damping length in mass fractal <math>\xi</math> 
#[{{mono|nu}}] Flory exponent, <math>\nu=3/5</math> in good solvent, <math>\nu=1/2</math> in theta solvent (i.e. <math>\mu=2/3</math> to 1)  

 FIGURE DELETED 

==Flexible Ring Polymer==

==m-membered twisted ring==

[Burchard 1996]


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_{mr}(q) & = I_0\left(\frac{P_{1r}(q)}{m} + \frac{2}{m^2}P_{1r}^2(q)\sum_{j=1}^{m-1}(m-j)\exp\left(-\frac{q^2R^2_{g,1r}}{2}(j-1)\right)\right)\\P_{1r}(q) & = \sqrt{\frac{2}{u_{1r}^2}} D\left[ \sqrt{\frac{u_{1r}^2}{2}} \right]\\u_{1r}^2 &= q^2R^2_{g,1r}\\R^2_{g,1r} &= \sqrt{\frac{b^2N}{12}}\\D(X) &= \exp\left(X^2\right) \int_0^X \exp(t^2)\, \mathrm{d}t \end{align}</math>|dummy}}


'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|mMemberedTwistedRing}}:'''
#[{{mono|R_G,1r}}] radius of gyration <math>R_{G,1r}</math> of one of <math>m</math> loop 
#[{{mono|m}}]  number of twists <math>m</math> 
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> 

 FIGURE DELETED
