===spherical Micelles with a homogeneous core and a corona of semi-flexible interacting self-avoiding chains===


==Sphere with Gaussian chains attached==

 FIGURE DELETED

The expressions have been derived by [Pedersen and Gerstenberg 1996] [Pedersen 2000]. For a sphere with radius <math>R</math> and total excess scattering length <math>\rho_s</math> with <math>N_\text{agg}</math> attached chains

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_{mic}(Q) & = N_\text{agg}^2 \rho_s^2 P_s(Q,R) + N_{agg} \rho_c^2 P_c(Q,R_g)\\& + N_\text{agg}(N_{agg}-1)\rho_c^2  S_{cc}(Q) + 2N_\text{agg}^2 \rho_s \rho_c S_{sc}(Q) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_s &= \Phi^2(Q,R)\\\Phi(Q,R) &= 3 \frac{\sin(QR)-QR\cos(QR)}{(QR)^3}\\P_c(Q,R_g) &= 2 \frac{\exp(-x)-1+x}{x^2}\\x &= R_g^2Q^2\\\Psi(Q,R_g) &= \frac{1-\exp(-x)}{x}\\S_\text{cc}(Q) &= \Psi^2(Q, R_g) \left(\frac{\sin(Q(R+d\,\,R_g))}{Q(R+d\,R_g)}\right)^2\\S_\text{sc}(Q) &= \Psi(Q, R_g)\Phi(Q R_g)\frac{\sin(Q(R+d\,R_g))}{Q(R+d\,R_g)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>R_g</math> is the root-mean-square radius of gyration of a chain. <math>\rho_c</math> is the total excess scattering length of a single chain. Non-penetrating of the chains into the core region is mimicked by <math>d\approx 1</math> for <math>R\gg R_g</math>.

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|SphereWithGaussChains}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] radius of core <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|Rg}}] gyration radius of chain <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] for non-penetration of the chains into the core region <math>d\approx 1</math>. 
#[{{mono|Nagg}}] aggregation number <math>N_\text{agg}</math> 
#[{{mono|rc}}] excess scattering length of a block in the chains <math>\rho_c</math> 
#[{{mono|rs}}] excess scattering length of a block in the core <math>\rho_s</math> 


==Sphere with Gaussian Chains Attached (Block Copolymer Micelle)==


This form factor is the same than for {{mono|SphereWithGaussChains}}. It has only been slightly re-parametrised. Instead of the core radius <math>R</math> and excess scattering lengths <math>\rho_s</math> and <math>\rho_c</math>  the volumes <math>V_\text{polym,c}</math> and <math>V_\text{polym,sh}</math> of the block units building the core and the shell are required together with the corresponding scattering length densities <math>\eta_\text{poly,c}</math>, <math>\eta_\text{poly,sh}</math> and that one of the solvent <math>\eta_\text{solv}</math>. Furthermore <math>x_\text{solv,c}</math> is the amount of solvent in the core which takes account for a possible swelling of the core. These parameters allow one to calculate the core radius and excess scattering lengths by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} R &= \left({\left\vert \right\vert}{ \frac{N_\text{agg}V_\text{polym,c}}{1-x_\text{solv,c}}} \frac{3}{4\pi}\right)^{1/3}  \\\rho_s &= V_\text{polym,c} \left(\eta_\text{poly,c}-\eta_\text{solv} \right)\\\rho_c &= V_\text{polym,sh} \left(\eta_\text{poly,sh}-\eta_\text{solv}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The volumes <math>V_\text{polym,c}</math> and <math>V_\text{polym,sh}</math> can be calculated by knowing the molecular weights\footnote{<math>u=1.660 538 86 \times 10^{-27}</math> kg} of the block units of the polymer in the core <math>M_\text{polym,c}</math> and in the shell <math>M_\text{polym,sh}</math> together with their bulk mass densities <math>\rho_\text{polym,c}</math> and <math>\rho_\text{polym,sh}</math>. The volumes are then given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} V_\text{polym,c} = \frac{M_\text{polym,c}}{N_a\, \rho_\text{polym,c}} \quad \text{ and } \quad V_\text{polym,sh} = \frac{M_\text{polym,sh}}{N_a\, \rho_\text{polym,sh}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>N_a</math> is Avogadro's constant<ref><math>N_a = 6.022 1415 \times 10^{23} \text{mol}^{-1}</math></ref>. The units of the block units has to be supplied in units corresponding to the scattering vector <math>Q</math>, i.e. in nm<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in nm<math>^{-1}</math> or in Å<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in Å<math>^{-1}</math>. 

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|BlockCopolymerMicelle}}:''' 
#[{{mono|Vpolym_c}}] volume of a single block unit of the chains in the core <math>V_\text{polym,c}</math>, it should be given in units of nm<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in nm<math>^{-1}</math> and in units of Å<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in Å<math>^{-1}</math>. 
#[{{mono|xsolv_c}}] amount of solvent in the core (<math>x_\text{solv,c}\neq 1</math>) 
#[{{mono|Vpolym_sh}}] volume of a single block unit of the chains in the shell <math>V_\text{polym,sh}</math>, it should be given in units of nm<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in nm<math>^{-1}</math> and in units of Å<math>^3</math> in case <math>Q</math> is given in Å<math>^{-1}</math>. 
#[{{mono|eta_poly_c}}] scattering length density of the block units in the core <math>\eta_\text{c}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_poly_sh}}] scattering length density of the block units in the chains <math>\eta_\text{sh}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_solv}}] scattering length density of the solvent <math>\eta_\text{solv}</math> 
#[{{mono|Nagg}}] aggregation number <math>N_\text{agg}</math> 
#[{{mono|Rg}}] gyration radius of chain <math>R_g</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] for non-penetration of the chains into the core region <math>d\approx 1</math>.  

==Bi-continuous and non-particular structures==

==TeubnerStrey==

The Teubner and Strey <ref name="TeubnerStrey87"> M. Teubner and R. Strey. Origin of the scattering peak in microemulsions. ''J. Chem. Phys.'', 87:3195, 1987.

</ref><ref name="StreyKlineKaler1994"> K.-V. Schubert, R. Strey, S. R. Kline, and E. W. Kaler. Small angle neutron scattering near lifshitz lines: Transition from weakly structured mixtures to microemulsions. ''J. Chem. Phys.'', 101:5343, 1994.

</ref> phenomenological model often accurately describes scattering from bi-continuous micro-emulsions. The scattered intensity for this model is

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) = \frac{8\pi\langle\eta^2\rangle/\xi}{a^2-2bq^2+q^4} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>a^2=(k^2+1/\xi^2)^2</math> is a positive quantity, and <math>b=k^2-1/\xi^2</math> can be a positive or negative depending on the relative magnitude of <math>d=2\pi/k</math> and <math>\xi</math>. A positive <math>b</math>, i.e. <math>\xi>d/2\pi</math>, leads to a peak at <math>q_\text{max}=\sqrt{b}</math> whereby for <math>\xi<d/2\pi</math>, hence negative <math>b</math>, no distinct peak appears. The length scale <math>d</math> represents a quasi-periodic repeat distance between water and oil regions within the solution, while the correlation length, <math>\xi</math> , corresponds to a characteristic length for positional correlation. <math>k</math> is defined as <math>2\pi/d</math>. The corresponding isotropic real space correlation function, <math>\gamma(r)</math>, that incorporates alternating regions of the two phases in the bi-continuous system (e.g. water and oil), is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \gamma(r) = \frac{\sin(kr)}{kr} \exp\left(-\frac{r}{\xi} \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



\underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|TeubnerStrey}}:}\  
#[{{mono|xi}}] correlation length <math>\xi</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] characteristic domain size <math>d</math> 
#[{{mono|eta2}}] squared scattering length density contrast <math>\eta^2</math> 

\underline{Note:}  
# None


 FIGURE DELETED



==Debye Anderson Brumberger(DAB)==

This form factor calculates the scattering from a randomly distributed (i.e. non-particulate), two-phase system based on the Debye-Anderson-Brumberger (DAB) [Debye et al. 1957] [Debye and Bueche 1949]

* [*Debye et al. 1957] P. Debye, R. Anderson, and H. Brumberger, ''Scattering by an inhomogeneous solid. ii. the correlation function and its application'', Journal of Applied Physics, 28(6):679, 1957
* [*Debye and Bueche 1949] P. Debye and A.M. Bueche, ''Scattering by an inhomogeneous solid'', Journal of Applied Physics, 20:518--525, 1949

</ref> model for such systems. The two-phase system is characterized by a single length scale, the correlation length <math>\xi</math>, which is a measure of the average spacing between regions of phase 1 and phase 2. The model also assumes smooth interfaces between the phases and hence exhibits Porod behavior (<math>I \propto q^{-4}</math>) at large <math>q</math> (<math>q \xi \gg 1</math>). The pair correlation function is give by [Debye and Bueche 1949]

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \gamma(r) = \exp(-r/\xi) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The macroscopic scattering cross-section in the DBA model is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) = I_0 \frac{1}{\left[1+(q\xi)^2\right]^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|DAB}}:'''
#[{{mono|xi}}] correlation length <math>\xi</math> 
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED



==Spinodal==

 FIGURE DELETED

Spinodal decomposing systems show a characteristic small angle scattering signal with a correlation peak at some scattering value <math>q_\text{max}</math>. The scattering curve <math>I(q)</math> can be approximated by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) = I_\text{max} \frac{(1+\gamma/2)x^2}{\gamma/2+x^{2+\gamma}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

according to Furukawa <ref name="Furukawa1984"> H. Furukawa. Dynamics-scaling theory for phase-separating unmixing mixtures: Growth rates of droplets and scaling properties of autocorrelation functions. ''Physica A'', 123:497, 1984.

</ref>, where <math>x=q/q_\text{max}</math>. The position of the correlation peak at <math>q_\text{max}</math> contain information about the size of the structures, which scatter. The exponent <math>\gamma</math> is equal to <math>\gamma=D+1</math> for off-critical mixtures and <math>\gamma=2D</math> for critical concentration mixtures, whereby <math>D</math> is the dimensionality of the system.



\underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Spinodal}}:}\  
#[{{mono|Qmax}}] peak maximum <math>q_\text{max}</math> 
#[{{mono|gamma}}] exponent <math>\gamma</math> 
#[{{mono|Imax}}] scattering intensity at peak position <math>I_\text{max}</math> 

\underline{Note:}  
# None


 FIGURE DELETED



==OrnsteinZernike==

\ The low-angle scattering of thermal composition fluctuations can be described according to the Ornstein-Zernike formulation by a Lorentzian profile

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) = \frac{I_0}{1+q^2\xi^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

characterizing the exponential decay of the composition fluctuations correlation function, with correlation length <math>\xi</math>. The Fourier transform of a Lorentzian function corresponds to correlations dying out as <math>\gamma(r) \simeq \frac{1}{r}exp(-r/\xi)</math>. Note that the low-<math>Q</math> limit of this empirical form reproduces the Guinier law.

\ \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|OrnsteinZernike}}:}\  
#[{{mono|I0}}] forward scattering <math>I_0</math> at <math>q=0</math>. 
#[{{mono|xi}}] correlation length <math>\xi</math> 


 FIGURE DELETED 

==BroadPeak==

Many SANS spectra are characterized by a broad peak even though they are from amorphous soft materials. The <math>d</math>-spacing corresponding to the broad peak is a characteristic distance between the scattering inhomogeneities (such as in lamellar, cylindrical, or spherical morphologies or for bicontinuous structures). The following simple functional form reproduces the broad peak feature:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) = \frac{I_0}{1+\left({\left\vert q-q_0\right\vert}\xi\right)^m} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Here the peak position is related to the <math>d</math>-spacing as <math>q_0 = 2\pi/d</math>. Soft systems that show a SANS peak include copolymers, polyelectrolytes, multiphase systems, layered structures, etc.



==Generalized Guinier approximation <ref name="Fratzl1994"> Peter Fratzl. Statistical model of the habit and arrangement of mineral crystals in the collagen of bone. ''Journal of Statistical Physics'', 77(1-2):125--143, 1994.

</ref><ref name="Hjelm1992"> Rex P. Hjelm, P. Thiyagarajan, and Hayat Alkan-Onyuksel. Organization of phosphatidylcholine and bile salt in rodlike mixed micelles. ''J. Phys. Chem'', 96:8653--8661, 1992,.

</ref><ref name="Hjelm1995"> Rex P. Hjelm, Claudio Schteingart, Alan F. Hofmann, and Devinderjit S. Sivia. Form and structure of self-assembling particles in monoolein-bile salt mixtures. ''J. Phys. Chem.'', 99:16395--16406, 1995.

</ref>\cite{Hjelm2000==

}    \\


 FIGURE DELETED Quantitative analysis of particle size and shape starts with the Guinier approximations. For three-dimensional objects the Guinier approximation is given by <math>I(q) = \exp(-Rg^2q^2/3)</math> This approximation can be extended also to rod-like and plane objects by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q) &= \left\{ \begin{array}{lll} 1 & \mbox{for} & \alpha=0\\\alpha \pi q^{-\alpha} & \mbox{for} & \alpha=1,2\\\end{array} \right\} A \,\, \exp\left(-\frac{R_\alpha^2 q^2}{3-\alpha}\right)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 
#[<math>\alpha=0</math>] spheroid 
#[<math>\alpha=1</math>] rod-like 
#[<math>\alpha=2</math>] plane  The apparent particle shape (also called the dimensionality) is represented in eq.  by <math>\alpha</math>, which has integer values of 0, 1, and 2 for a point, a line, and a plane, respectively. Equation  states that there are <math>q</math> ranges, corresponding to length scales as <math>q^{-1}</math>, from which the particle dimension or shape, <math>\alpha</math>, the radius of gyration, <math>R_\alpha</math>, and the pre-factor, <math>A</math>, characteristic of <math>\alpha</math> can be inferred. <math>\alpha</math> has a value of 0 for a <math>q</math> range such that <math>qR_g < 1-1.3</math> (the larger applies when the particle is known to be a spheroid), where <math>R_g</math> is the particle radius of gyration (computed about the particle centroid). In this case, the pre-factor <math>A</math> describes the excess differential cross-section per unit mass (cm<math>^2</math> g<math>^{-1}</math>) of a particle. If the particle has one dimension of length <math>L</math>, that is, much larger than the others (i.e., elongated, rod-like, or worm-like), then there is a <math>q</math> range such that <math>qR_c < 1 \ll qL</math>, where <math>\alpha = 1</math>. Here, <math>R_c</math> is the radius of gyration (computed about a line centered along <math>L</math>) of the cross-section perpendicular to <math>L</math>. If these conditions apply, the pre-factor <math>A</math> describes the excess differential cross section per unit length per unit mass (cm<math>^2</math> Å<math>^{-1}</math> g<math>^{-1}</math>). Finally, for planar shapes, including single bilayer vesicles, with two locally large dimensions, <math>D</math>, and planar cross-sectional radius of gyration (computed about a central plain),<math>R_d</math>, there may be a region of <math>q</math> such that <math>qR_d < 1 \ll qD</math>, where <math>\alpha = 2</math>. For such planar structures, the pre-factor is the excess differential cross-section per unit area per unit mass (cm<math>^2</math> Å<math>^{-2}</math> g<math>^{-1}</math>) of a sheet.


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Clustered Objects==

==Mass Fractal <ref name="Sorensen1999"> C. M. S{\o}rensen and G. M. Wang. Size distribution effect on the power law regime of the structure factor of fractal aggregates. ''Physical Review E'', 60(6):7143--7148, 1999.

</ref><ref name="Sorensen1992"> C. M. S{\o}rensen, J. Cai, and N. Lu. Test of static structure factors for describing light scattering from fractal soot aggregates. ''Langmuir'', 8:2064--2069, 1992.

</ref><ref name="Hurd1988"> A.J. Hurd and W.L. Flower. In situ growth and structure of fractal silica aggregates in a flame. ''Journal of Colloid and Interface Science'', 122(1):178--192, 1988.

</ref><ref name="Lin1989"> M.Y. Lin, H.M. Lindsay, D.A. Weitz, R.C. Ball, R. Klein, and P. Meakin. Universality of fractal aggregates as probed by light scattering. ''Proc. R. Soc. Lond. A'', 423:71--87, 1989.

</ref><ref name="Lin1990"> M.Y. Lin, H.M. Lindsay, D.A. Weitz, R.C. Ball, R. Klein, and P. Meakin. Universal reaction-limited colloidal aggregation. ''Physical Review A'', 41(4):2005--2020, 1990.

</ref>\cite{Lin1990a==

}   \\


 FIGURE DELETED

Aggregates and clusters often have a fractal morphology. These self-similar clusters are well described by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} N=k_0(R_g/r_0)^D \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>N</math> is the number of primary particles or monomers in the aggregate, <math>k_0</math> is a constant of order unity, <math>R_g</math> is the radius of gyration of the aggregate, <math>r_0</math> is the monomer radius, and <math>D</math> is the fractal dimension.

The scattering function and the density autocorrelation function of the aggregate are Fourier transform pairs; thus

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(q)=4\pi \int_0^\infty \, g(r)\, r^2\, \frac{\sin(qr)}{qr}\, dr  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For a fractal aggregate the autocorrelation function has the form

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} g(r) \sim r^{D-d} h(r,\xi)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Here <math>D</math> is the fractal dimension, <math>d</math> the spatial dimension, and <math>\xi</math> a measure of the linear size of the aggregate proportional to the radius of gyration <math>R_g</math>. The function <math>h(r,\xi)</math> is the cutoff function describing the perimeter of the aggregate. Its properties are that <math>h(r,\xi) \simeq 1</math> for <math>r/\xi \lesssim 1</math>, but for large <math>r/\xi</math> it falls off faster than any power law.

==Porod's approximation for a long cylinder==

==Porod's approximations for cylinder==

To different versions for a random oriented cylindrical shell with a circular cross-section has been implemented. One without  {{mono|CylShell1}} and one with {{mono|CylShell2}} capped ends. For very long cylinders a faster approximation for the uncapped version can be used {{mono|LongCylShell}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K_\text{Cyl}(Q,\Delta\eta,R,L,x) = 2 \pi R^2 L \Delta \eta \frac{J_1\left(Q R \sqrt{1-x^2}\right)}{Q R \sqrt{1-x^2}} \frac{\sin(Q L x/2)}{QL x/2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{CylShell1} = \int_0^1 \biggl( & K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{core}-\eta_\text{shell},R,L,x\right)\\+&  K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{shell}-\eta_\text{solv},R+\Delta R,L,x\right) \biggr)^2 dx \\I_\text{CylShell2} = \int_0^1 \biggl( &  K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{core}-\eta_\text{shell},R,L,x\right)\\+&  K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{shell}-\eta_\text{solv},R+\Delta R,L+2\Delta R,x\right) \biggr)^2 dx \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{LongCylShell}(Q) = & P'(Q) P_\text{cs}(Q)\\P'(Q)  = & 2 \frac{\text{Si}(Q L)}{QL} - \left(\frac{\sin(QL/2)}{QL/2}\right)\\\text{Si}(x) & = \int_0^x\!\frac{\sin t}{t}\,\,dt\\P_\text{cs}(Q)  =  & \biggl( 2\frac{J_1(QR)}{QR} \left(\eta_\text{core}-\eta_\text{shell}\right)R^2L\pi +\\& \qquad 2\frac{J_1(Q(R+\Delta R))}{Q(R+\Delta R)} \left(\eta_\text{shell}-\eta_\text{solv}\right)(R+\Delta R)^2L\pi \biggr)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For very long cylinders a faster approximation for the uncapped version can be used {{mono|Pcs:ellCylSh}} combined with the structure factor {{mono|P'(Q):Rod}}. The implemented approximation is the following

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{Long_ellCylShell}(q) = & P'(q) P_\text{cs}(q)\\P'(q)  = & 2 \frac{\text{Si}(q L)}{qL} - \left(\frac{\sin(qL/2)}{qL/2}\right)\\\text{Si}(x) & = \int_0^x\!\frac{\sin t}{t}\,\,dt\\r(R,\epsilon,\phi) &= \sqrt{R^2\sin^2(\phi)+(\epsilon R+t)^2\cos^2(\phi)}\ P_\text{cs}(q)  =  \frac{2}{\pi} \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}}& \biggl( \frac{2J_1(qr(R,\epsilon,\phi))}{qr(R,\epsilon,\phi)} \left(\eta_\text{core}-\eta_\text{shell}\right)\epsilon R^2L\pi +\\& \frac{2J_1(q(r(R,\epsilon,\phi)+t))}{Q(r(R,\epsilon,\phi)+t)} \left(\eta_\text{shell}-\eta_\text{sol}\right)(R+t)(\epsilon R+t)L\pi \biggr)^2  \, d\phi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|Pcs:ellCylSh}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] core radius <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|epsilon}}] eccentricity <math>\epsilon</math> of cross-section 
#[{{mono|t}}] shell thickness <math>t</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_core}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{core}</math> of cylinder core 
#[{{mono|eta_shell}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{shell}</math> of cylinder shell 
#[{{mono|eta_sol}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{sol}</math> of solvent

==partly aligned cylindrical shell==

[Hayter and Penfold 1984]
* [*Hayter and Penfold 1984] J. B. Hayter and J. Penfold, ''Use of viscous shear alignment to study anisotropic micellar structure by small-angle neutron scattering'', J. Phys. Chem., 88:4589--4593, 1984

 FIGURE DELETED

The scattering amplitude of a cylindrical shell is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K_\text{CylShell}\left(Q,\dots,\gamma\right)  = &K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{core}-\eta_\text{shell},R,L,\gamma\right)\\& +  K_\text{Cyl}\left(Q,\eta_\text{shell}-\eta_\text{solv},R+\Delta R,L,\gamma\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K_\text{Cyl}(Q,\Delta\eta,R,L,\gamma) & = 2 \pi R^2 L \Delta \eta \frac{J_1\left(Q R \sin\gamma\right)}{Q R \sin\gamma} \, \frac{\sin\left(\frac{QL}{2} \cos\gamma\right)}{\frac{QL}{2} \cos\gamma} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\gamma</math> is the angle between <math>\mathbf{Q}</math> and the cylinder axis <math>\mathbf{n}</math>. <math>L</math> is the length of the cylinder, <math>R</math> its radius, <math>\Delta\eta</math> the scattering length density contrast relative to the solvent and <math>J_1(x)</math> is the first order Bessel function of the first kind. <math>\gamma</math> can be calculated from the orientation (<math>\theta</math>, <math>\phi</math>) of the cylinder and the direction of the scattering vector <math>\psi</math> in the plane of the detector by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{\mathbf{Q}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{Q}}} &= \begin{pmatrix} \cos \psi\\0 \\\sin \psi \end{pmatrix} \qquad \frac{\mathbf{n}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{n}}} = \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta\\\sin \theta \sin \phi \\\sin \theta \cos \phi \end{pmatrix}\\\cos \measuredangle(\mathbf{Q,n}) &= \cos \gamma = \frac{\mathbf{Q\cdot n}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{Q}}{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{n}}} = \cos\psi \cos\theta + \sin\psi \sin\theta \cos\phi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

If the orientation distribution of the orientation vector <math>\mathbf{n}</math> is described by <math>p(\theta,\phi)</math> so that the scattering intensity is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\mathrm{p.a.CylShell}(Q) & = \int_0^\pi d\theta \int_0^{2\pi} d\phi \, \, K_\text{CylShell}\left(Q,\dots,\gamma\right)\,p(\theta,\phi)\,\sin(\theta) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For this form factor it is assumed that the orientation distribution is independent of <math>\phi</math>, i.e. <math>p(\theta,\phi)=p(\theta)</math> and that <math>p(\theta)=p(\pi-\theta)</math>, which means that turning the cylinder by 180<math>^\circ</math> results in the same scattering intensity. Instead of assuming a special parametrization of <math>p(\theta)</math> the orientation distribution was expanded in terms of Legendre polynomials <math>P_l(\cos(\theta))</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(\theta) & = \sum_{l=0,even}^\infty \frac{2l+1}{2} \left\langle P_l\right\rangle\, P_l(\cos(\theta)) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Due to the symmetrie <math>p(\theta)=p(\pi-\theta)</math> all terms with odd values for <math>l</math> are zero and only the even terms needs to be considered. For this form factor the first three terms up to <math>l=6</math> are implemented. As <math>\int_0^\pi p(\theta) \sin\theta \, d\theta =1</math> the zero order parameter is one <math>\left\langle P_0 \right\rangle=1</math>.

'''Input Parameters for model {{mono|partly aligned CylShell}}:'''
#[{{mono|R}}] core radius <math>R</math> 
#[{{mono|DR}}] shell thickness <math>\Delta R</math> 
#[{{mono|L}}] cylinder length <math>L</math> 
#[{{mono|eta_core}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{core}</math> of cylinder core 
#[{{mono|eta_shell}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{shell}</math> of cylinder shell 
#[{{mono|eta_solv}}] scattering length density <math>\eta_\text{solv}</math> of solvent 
#[{{mono|psi}}] direction <math>\psi</math> of the scattering vector in the plane of the detector 
#[{{mono|P2}}] order parameter <math>\langle P_2\rangle</math> 
#[{{mono|P4}}] order parameter <math>\langle P_4\rangle</math> 
#[{{mono|P6}}] order parameter <math>\langle P_6\rangle</math>

==Torus with elliptical shell cross-section

[Kawaguchi 2001] [Forster 1999]

* [*Kawaguchi 2001] Takeshi Kawaguchi, ''Radii of gyration and scattering functions of a torus and its derivatives'', J. Appl. Cryst.'', 34:580--584, 2001
* [*Forster 1999] S. Förster, N. Hermsdorf, W. Leube, H. Schnablegger, M. Regenbrecht,
  S. Akari, P. Lindner, and C. Böttcher, ''Fusion of charged block copolymer micelles into toroid networks'', J. Phys. Chem. B, 103:6657--6668, 1999

 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{torus}(Q,\Theta,R,x,\nu,\Delta\eta) & = \int_{R-x}^{R+x} 4\pi r \Delta\eta \frac{J_0(Qr\sin\Theta) \sin( Q\gamma(r)\cos\Theta)}{Q\cos(\Theta)} \; dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{with } \gamma(r) &= \nu \sqrt{x^2-(r-R)^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{torus}(Q,R,a,\nu,\Delta\eta) &= \int_0^{\pi/2} {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_\text{torus}(Q,\Theta,R,a,\nu,\Delta\eta)}^2 \sin\Theta  \; d\Theta \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{torus,sh}(Q,R,a,\Delta a,\nu,\Delta\eta_{sh},\Delta\eta_c) = \int_0^{\pi/2} \Bigl\lvert & F_\text{torus}(Q,\Theta,R,a+\Delta a,\nu,\Delta\eta_{sh})\\-&F_\text{torus}(Q,\Theta,R,a,\nu,\Delta\eta_c)\Bigr\rvert^2 \sin\Theta  \;  d\Theta \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

An alternative form factor for <math>F_\text{torus}</math> following [Forster 1999] is

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{torus}(Q,\Theta,R,x,\nu,\Delta\eta)  = 2\pi\int_{-x}^{x} \bigg[ & R_{(+)} J_1(QR_{(+)}\sin\theta)\\-& R_{(-)} J_1(QR_{(-)}\sin\theta) \bigg] \frac{\cos(Qz\cos\theta)}{Q\sin\theta} \; dz \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>R_{(\pm)} = R\pm\nu\sqrt{x^2-z^2}</math>

==Stacked Tori with Elliptical Shell Cross-Section==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_\text{stacked} & _\text{tori}(Q,\Theta,R,x,\nu,\Delta\eta,\Delta D,N)  = \\& \sum_{n=1}^N \quad \int_{R-x}^{R+x} 4\pi r \Delta\eta \frac{J_0(Qr\sin\Theta) \sin( Q(\gamma(r)+\frac{2(n-1)-(N-1)}{4}\Delta D)\cos\Theta)}{Q\cos(\Theta)} \; dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The scattering intensity is than calculated in the same way as for a single torus with an elliptical shell cross-section. 

==Planar Objects==

The form factor of very anisotropic particles with local planar geometry can be shown to factorize (see, e.g., Porod, 1948) into a cross section form factor <math>P_\text{cs}(Q)</math> for the shorter dimensions and a shape factor <math>P'(Q)</math> for the larger dimension:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_\text{planar}(Q) = P'(Q) P_\text{cs}(Q) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The shape form factor <math>P'(Q)</math> can be that of an infinitely thin spherical shell, elliptical shell, cylindrical shell, or disk. In the following the shape form factor is assumed to the one of an infinitely thin disk:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P'(Q,R)=\frac{2\pi^2R^4}{(QR)^2} \left(1-\frac{\text{J}_1(2QR)}{QR}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}


==homogeneousXS==

 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_\text{cs}(Q,\eta,L) = \left( \eta L\frac{\sin(QL/2)}{QL/2}\right)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_\text{cs}(Q,\eta,L) = \eta^2 \cos^2(QL/2) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

A layered centro symmetric cross-section structure with outer thickness <math>L_\text{out}</math> and a core of thickness <math>L_c</math>, where the core and the outer part have the scattering lengths density <math>\eta_\text{out}</math> and <math>\eta_c</math>, respectively, has

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_\text{cs}(Q,\eta_\text{out},L_\text{out},\eta_c,L_c) = \Biggl( & \frac{\eta_\text{out}L_\text{out}\sin\left(\frac{QL_\text{out}}{2}\right)}{QL_\text{out}/2}\\&-  \frac{(\eta_\text{out}-\eta_c)L_c\sin\left(\frac{QL_c}{2}\right)}{QL_c/2}\Biggr)^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Sheared Objects==

==ShearedCylinderHayterPenfold==

[Hayter 1984]
* [*Hayter 1984] J. B. Hayter and J. Penfold, ''Use of viscous shear alignment to study anisotropic micellar structure by small-angle neutron scattering'', J. Phys. Chem., 88:4589--4593, 1984


 FIGURE DELETED


 FIGURE DELETED

The scattering from monodisperse dilute (non-interacting) isotropic solution of anisotropic micelles is given by

{{eqn|<math> I(Q) = \left< \vert F(Q)\vert^2 \right>_Q  </math>|dummy}}

where <math>F(Q)</math> is the form factor for a micelle at a given orientation relative to the momentum transfer <math>Q</math> and <math>\left<\right>_Q</math> denotes an average over all such orientations.

For a uniform cylinder of length <math>L</math> and diameter <math>2R</math> the form factor is given by:

{{eqn|<math> F(Q) = F(Q,\gamma) = 2\Delta\eta V \frac{\sin\left(QL/2\cos\gamma\right)}{QL/2\cos\gamma} \frac{J_1(QR\sin\gamma)}{QR\sin\gamma} </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\gamma</math> is the angle between <math>Q</math> and the cylinder axis, <math>V</math> is the volume, <math>\Delta\eta</math> the scattering length density contrast relative to the solvent, <math>J_1(x)</math> is the first order Bessel function of the first kind.

The scattering geometry for shear alignment is shown in Fig. . In general perfect alignment will not be achieved, and an orientation distribution must be employed such that the resultant scattering will be given by

{{eqn|<math> I(Q,\psi) = \int_0^{2\pi}d\varPhi \int_0^\pi p(\theta,\varPhi;\Gamma)\, \left(F^2(Q,\gamma^+)+F^2(Q,\gamma^-) \right) \sin\theta d\theta </math>|dummy}}

where

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \cos\gamma^\pm & = \sin\theta\cos\phi\cos\psi\pm\cos\theta\sin\psi \\[3mm] p(\theta,\phi;\Gamma) & = \frac{(1-\cos 2\varPhi_0)(1+\sin^2\theta\cos 2\varPhi_0)^{3/2}}{4\pi\left[ 1-\sin^2\theta\cos 2\varPhi_0\cos 2(\phi-\varPhi_0)\right]^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and

{{eqn|<math> 2\varPhi_0 = \arctan(8/\Gamma) </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{\mathbf{Q}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{Q}}} &= \begin{pmatrix} \cos \psi\\0 \\\sin \psi \end{pmatrix} \qquad \frac{\mathbf{n}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{n}}} = \begin{pmatrix} \sin \theta \cos \phi\\\sin \theta \sin \phi \\\cos \theta \end{pmatrix}\\\cos \angle(\mathbf{Q,n}) &= \cos \gamma = \frac{\mathbf{Q\cdot n}}{{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{Q}}{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mathbf{n}}} =  \cos\psi \sin\theta \cos\phi + \sin\psi \cos\theta \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==ShearedCylinderBoltzmann==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(\theta,\phi;\bar{\theta}) & = \exp(-\theta/\bar{\theta})\\\cos\gamma^\pm & = \sin\theta\cos\phi\cos\psi\pm\cos\theta\sin\psi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==ShearedCylinderGaussian==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(\theta,\phi;\bar{\theta}) & = \exp(-(\theta/\bar{\theta})^2)\\\cos\gamma^\pm & = \sin\theta\cos\phi\cos\psi\pm\cos\theta\sin\psi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==ShearedCylinderHeaviside==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(\theta,\phi;\bar{\theta}) & = \Theta[\theta-\bar{\theta}]\\\cos\gamma^\pm & = \sin\theta\cos\phi\cos\psi\pm\cos\theta\sin\psi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 

==Magnetic Scattering==

In the case of magnetic moments in the sample, the neutron undergoes a magnetic interaction in addition to the nuclear interaction. The corresponding interaction potential is given by

{{eqn|<math>V(\mathbf{r}) = -\mathbf{\mu}_N \cdot \mathbf{B}(\mathbf{r}) \quad \text{with} \quad \mathbf{\mu}_N=\gamma\frac{e \hbar}{2m_N c}\mathbf{\sigma}</math>|dummy}}

where  <math>\mathbf{\mu}_N=\gamma\frac{e \hbar}{2m_N c}\mathbf{\sigma}</math> is the magnetic dipole moment of the neutron, \mathbf{\boldmath<math> \sigma</math>} the Pauli spin operator, <math>\gamma = -1.913</math> the gyromagnetic ratio and <math>\mathbf{B}(\mathbf{r})</math> the magnetic field induced by an atom at the position of the neutron. The latter has two components, one induced by the magnetic dipole moment  <math>\mathbf{\mu}_S</math> of the electrons, denoted <math>\mathbf{B}_S(\mathbf{r})</math>, and one by their orbital moment <math>\mathbf{\mu}_L</math>, denoted <math>\mathbf{B}_L(\mathbf{r})</math>. The (weak) magnetic interaction <math>V(\mathbf{r}) = -\mathbf{\mu}_N\cdot(\mathbf{B}_S(\mathbf{r})+\mathbf{B}_L(\mathbf{r}))</math> can as well be treated in first Born approximation, resulting in the magnetic scattering amplitude, in analogy to the nuclear scattering amplitude, given by the Fourier transform of the magnetic interaction potential <math>\mathcal {F}[V(\mathbf{r})]</math>:

{{eqn|<math> b_M =-\frac{m_N}{2\pi\hbar^2}\, \mathbf{\boldmath<math> \mu</math>}_N \cdot \int d^3r \, e^{\imath\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Qr</math>}}\, (\mathbf{\boldmath<math> B</math>}_S(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> r</math>})+\mathbf{\boldmath<math> B</math>}_L(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> r</math>})). </math>|dummy}}

An additional static magnetic field <math>\mathbf{H}(\mathbf{r})</math> at the point of local magnetization <math>\mathbf{H}(\mathbf{r})</math> (stemming from <math>\mathbf{B}_S(\mathbf{r})+\mathbf{B}_L(\mathbf{r})</math>) induces a total local magnetic induction of

{{eqn|<math>\mathbf{\boldmath<math> B</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> r</math>})=\mu_0(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> H</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> r</math>})+\mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> r</math>}))</math>|dummy}}

and the Fourier transform of   yields

{{eqn|<math> \mathbf{\boldmath<math> B</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}) = \mu_0\, \frac{\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}\times [\mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>})\times\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}]}{Q^2} = \mu_0\, \mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}_{\bot}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}) = \mu_0\, \mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}) \sin(\angle(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q,M</math>})) </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\mathbf{M}(\mathbf{Q}) = \int d^3r \exp(\imath\mathbf{Q}\cdot\mathbf{r}) \mathbf{M}(\mathbf{r})</math> , with  <math>\mathbf{M}(\mathbf{r})</math> given in units of Am. <math>\mathbf{M}_{\bot}(\mathbf{Q}) = \mathbf{Q}\times [\mathbf{M}(\mathbf{Q})\times\mathbf{Q}] / Q^2</math> is the magnetization component perpendicular to the scattering vector <math>Q</math>. The magnetic scattering length then is

{{eqn|<math> b_M = D_M\, \mu_0\, \mathbf{\boldmath<math>\sigma</math>}\cdot \mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}_{\bot}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}) \mathbf{ with  } D_M = \frac{m_N}{2\pi\hbar^2}\, \mu_N = 2.3161 \times 10^{14} \, \frac{1}{\mathbf{Vs}}. </math>|dummy}}

For the differential scattering cross section one finally obtains

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma_M}{d\Omega}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>}) = \frac{D_M^2}{N}{\left\vert \right\vert}{\mu_0 \mathbf{\boldmath<math> M</math>}_{\bot}(\mathbf{\boldmath<math> Q</math>})}^2 </math>|dummy}}

In the presence of a preferred direction, for example induced by an external magnetic field, the magnetic scattering depends on the spin state   of the neutrons. Let the z-axis be the preferred direction, and let (<math>+</math>) and (<math>-</math>) denote the neutron spin polarizations parallel and antiparallel to the z-axis, then the scattering is described by four scattering processes: two processes where the incident states (<math>+</math>) and (<math>-</math>) remain unchanged (<math>++</math> and <math>--</math>), the so-called 'non-spin-flip' processes, and two processes where the spin is flipped (<math>+-</math> and <math>-+</math>), the 'spin-flip' processes. Keeping in mind that the nuclear scattering does not flip the neutron spin, the four related scattering lengths are

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} b_{\pm\pm} &= b_N \mp D_M\, \mu_0\, M_{\bot x}\\b_{\pm\mp} &= - D_M\, \mu_0\, (M_{\bot z} \pm \imath \, M_{\bot y}). \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Hereby <math>b_N</math> is the nuclear scattering length. For an unpolarized neutron beam (which may be taken as composed of 50\50\scattering length is

{{eqn|<math> (b_{++}^2+b_{--}^2+b_{+-}^2+b_{-+}^2)/2 = b_N^2+D_M^2 \mu_0^2 M_{\bot}^2. </math>|dummy}}

The differential cross section of the unpolarized neutron beam can therefore be described by the sum of the nuclear and the magnetic cross section, without any cross terms.



==Magnetic Saturation==

==MagneticShellAniso==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3}\\K_\text{sh}(Q&,R,\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh},\Delta\eta_\text{c}) = K(Q,R+\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh})-K(Q,R,\Delta\eta_\text{c})\ K_\text{NUC}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}}, \eta_{\text{c NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}})\ K_\text{MAG}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}}, \eta_{\text{c MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}})\ I(Q) &= \frac{1-p}{2} \left(K^2_\text{MAG}(Q)+2K_\text{NUC}(Q)K_\text{MAG}(Q)\right)\\&+ \frac{1+p}{2} \left(K^2_\text{MAG}(Q)-2K_\text{NUC}(Q)K_\text{MAG}(Q)\right)\ &= K^2_\text{MAG}(Q) - 2p K_\text{NUC}(Q)K_\text{MAG}(Q) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p            &: \text{neutron polarization, } p\in[-1:1] \\R            &: \text{radius of particle core} \\\Delta R     &: \text{thickness of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{sh NUC}} &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle core} \\\eta_{\text{sh MAG}} &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle core} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==MagneticShellCrossTerm==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3}\\K_\text{sh}(Q&,R,\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh},\Delta\eta_\text{c}) = K(Q,R+\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh})-K(Q,R,\Delta\eta_\text{c})\ K_\text{NUC}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}}, \eta_{\text{c NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}})\ K_\text{MAG}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}}, \eta_{\text{c MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}})\ I(Q) &=4p K_\text{NUC}(Q)K_\text{MAG}(Q) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p            &: \text{neutron polarization, } p\in[-1:1] \\R            &: \text{radius of particle core} \\\Delta R     &: \text{thickness of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{sh NUC}} &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle core} \\\eta_{\text{sh MAG}} &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle core} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==MagneticShellPsi==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} K(Q,R,\Delta\eta) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \Delta\eta \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3}\\K_\text{sh}(Q&,R,\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh},\Delta\eta_\text{c}) = K(Q,R+\Delta R,\Delta\eta_\text{sh})-K(Q,R,\Delta\eta_\text{c})\ K_\text{NUC}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}}, \eta_{\text{c NUC}}-\eta_{\text{m NUC}})\ K_\text{MAG}(Q) &= K_\text{sh}(Q,R,\Delta R,\eta_{\text{sh MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}}, \eta_{\text{c MAG}}-\eta_{\text{m MAG}})\ I(Q) &= K^2_\text{NUC}(Q)+\left(K^2_\text{MAG}(Q) - 2p K_\text{NUC}(Q)K_\text{MAG}(Q)\right) \sin^2\Psi \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p            &: \text{neutron polarization, } p\in[-1:1] \\R            &: \text{radius of particle core} \\\Psi         &: \text{angle between <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> Q<math>}</math> and <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> H<math>}</math>} \\\Delta R     &: \text{thickness of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{sh NUC}} &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c NUC}}  &: \text{nuclear scattering length density of particle core} \\\eta_{\text{sh MAG}} &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle shell} \\\eta_{\text{m MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of matrix} \\\eta_{\text{c MAG}}  &: \text{magnetic scattering length density of particle core} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Superparamagnetic Particles (like ferrofluids)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{I_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})}{N} &= {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_N(\mathbf{Q}) \mp \tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q}) \left[ L(\alpha)-\gamma\right]\,\sin^2\epsilon}^2\\&+ {\left\vert \right\vert}{\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\, \left( \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\, \sin^2\epsilon - \mathcal{L}(\alpha) \, \sin^4\epsilon \right) \\\ \frac{I_{\mp\pm}(\mathbf{Q})}{N} &= \left( \sin^2\epsilon - \sin^4\epsilon \right) \left[L(\alpha)-\gamma\right]^2 {\left\vert \right\vert}{\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\\&+ {\left\vert \right\vert}{\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2  \left( \left( \sin^4\epsilon-\sin^2\epsilon \right) \mathcal{L}(\alpha) + (2-\sin^2\epsilon)\, \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\right) \\\ I_{\rm unp}(\mathbf{Q}) &=  \frac{1}{2} \left(I_{++}(\mathbf{Q})+I_{+-}(\mathbf{Q})+I_{--}(\mathbf{Q})+I_{-+}(\mathbf{Q}) \right)\\&= N\, \left({\left\vert \right\vert}{\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2 \left[ L(\alpha)-\gamma\right]^2\,\sin^2\epsilon + {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_N(\mathbf{Q})}^2 \right) \\&+ N \, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\, \left( 2\, \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha} - \mathcal{L}(\alpha)\, \sin^2\epsilon\right)   \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Hereby <math>L(\alpha) = \coth \alpha - \frac{1}{\alpha}</math> is the classical Langevin function with\\<math>\alpha = \mu_0(H+M_{\rm eff})M_s^{cr} V_P/kT</math>.\\Furthermore the following functions are defined as:\ <math>\mathcal{L}(\alpha)=L^2(\alpha)-1+3\,\frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}</math>,\\<math>F_N(\mathbf{Q})=\Delta\eta\, V_N\, f_N(\mathbf{Q})</math>,\ <math>\tilde F_M(\mathbf{Q}) = D_M\, M_s^{cr}\, V_M\,f_M(\mathbf{Q})</math> and\\<math>\gamma = M_s^{am}/M_s^{cr}</math>.\\<math>\epsilon</math> describes the angle between <math>\mathbf{Q}</math> and the applied magnetic field <math>\mathbf{B}</math>. If the magnetic field lies in the plane of the detector, i.e. perpendicular to the incoming beam direction, <math>\epsilon</math> is in practice identical to <math>\Psi</math> so that <math>\cos \epsilon = \sin\delta\, \cos\Psi \simeq \cos \Psi</math> for <math>\delta \simeq \pi/2</math> (<math>\mathbf{Q}</math> in plane of detector for SANS, only for large scattering angle this will change).

==Lorenz-Mie Form Factors for Static Light Scattering==

Mie theory, also called Lorenz-Mie theory <ref name="Mie1908"> G. Mie. {Beitr\"age zur Optik tr\"uber Medien, speziell kolloidaler Metall\"osungen}. ''Annalen der Physik'', 25:377--455, 1908.

</ref><ref name="Hulst1957"> H.C. van de Hulst. ''Light Scattering by Small Particles''. Wiley, New York, 1957.

</ref><ref name="Bohren1983"> C.F. Bohren and D.R. Huffman. ''Absorption and Scattering of Light by Small Particles''. Wiley, New York, 1983.

</ref>, is a complete mathematical-physical theory of the scattering of electromagnetic radiation by spherical particles. Mie theory is named after its developer German physicist Gustav Mie (1868 Rostock - 1957 Freiburg im Breisgau) and Danish physicist Ludvig Lorenz (1829-1891) who independently developed the theory of electromagnetic plane wave scattering by a dielectric sphere in 1908.

Mie scattering describes the scattering of electromagnetic radiation by spherical particles of any size <math>r</math>, relative to the wavelength, <math>\lambda</math>. Since the cases <math>r \ll \lambda</math> and <math>r \gg \lambda</math> are covered by Rayleigh (dipole) scattering and geometric scattering theories, respectively, Mie scattering often refers to the case of <math>r \sim \lambda</math>.

==MieSphere==

\\

The Mie scattering formulae are given in several books (Van de Hulst, 1957; Kerker, 1969; Deirmendjian, 1969) and by Dave (1968a, 1969a), although not always in the forms most suited to computation. The algorithm used here is based on the MIEV0 package described in <ref name="Wiscombe1979"> W. Wiscombe. Mie scattering calculations--advances in technique and fast, vector-speed computer codes, ncar tech note tn-140+str. Technical report, National Center For Atmospheric Research, Boulder, Colorado, 1979.

</ref><ref name="Wiscombe1980"> W. Wiscombe. Improved mie scattering algorithms. ''Appl. Opt.'', 19:1505--1509, 1980.

</ref>. The following input values are used:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Theta &= 2\arcsin(Q\lambda/(4\pi)) \text{ with } Q<Q_\text{max}=4\pi/\lambda  \\R &= \text{radius of scattering sphere} \\\lambda & = \text{wavelength of incident plane wave inside the solvent} \\m & = \text{complex refractive index of sphere relative to surrounding medium} \\& = m_\text{re} - i m_\text{im} \\& \vert m \vert \geq 1 \text{ and } m_\text{im} \geq 0 \\& \text{or} \\&  \vert m\vert  < 1 \text{ and } m_\text{im} = 0 \\\text{pol} & = 0 \text{  unpolarized light} \\\text{pol} & = 1 \text{  parallel to scattering plane polarized light} \\\text{pol} & = -1 \text{  perpendicular to scattering plane polarized light} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>\vert m\vert < 1</math> would, for example, include visible light scattering from air bubbles in water.


 FIGURE DELETED



==MieShell==

\\

This form factor is basing on the version of MieLay, which computes electromagnetic scattering by a stratified sphere, i.e. a particle consisting of a spherical core surrounded by a spherical shell.  The surrounding medium is assumed to have refractive index unity. The formulas, manipulated to avoid the ill-conditioning that plagued earlier formulations, were published in <ref name="Toon1981"> O. Toon and T. Ackerman. Algorithms for the calculation of scattering by stratified spheres. ''Applied Optics'', 20(20):3657, 1981.

</ref>. Further documentation, including definitons of input and output  arguments, is inside the single precision version of this program ({\tt SUBROUTINE MieLay}, available by anonymous ftp from {\tt climate.gsfc.nasa.gov} in directory {\tt pub/wiscombe}). The following input values are used:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Theta &= 2\arcsin(Q\lambda/(4\pi)) \text{ with } Q<Q_\text{max}=4\pi/\lambda  \\R_\text{c} &= \text{radius of the core of scattering sphere} \\R_\text{sh} &= \text{thickness of the shell of scattering sphere} \\\lambda & = \text{wavelength of incident plane wave inside the solvent} \\m_\text{c} & = \text{complex refractive index of core relative to surrounding medium} \\& = m_\text{c,re} - i m_\text{c,im} \\& \vert m_\text{c} \vert \geq 1 \text{ and } m_\text{c,im} \geq 0 \\& \text{or} \\&  \vert m_\text{c}\vert  < 1 \text{ and } m_\text{c,im} = 0 \\m_\text{s} & = \text{complex refractive index of core relative to surrounding medium} \\& = m_\text{s,re} - i m_\text{s,im} \\\text{pol} & = 0 \text{  unpolarized light} \\\text{pol} & = 1 \text{  parallel to scattering plane polarized light} \\\text{pol} & = -1 \text{  perpendicular to scattering plane polarized light} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>\vert m\vert < 1</math> would, for example, include visible light scattering from air bubbles in water.


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Other functions==

==DLS_Sphere_RDG==


This function has been implemented to simulate the relaxation signal <math>g_2(t)-1 = g_1^2(t)</math> of a DLS (dynamic light scattering) measurement. The <math>Q</math> dependent contribution to the relaxation signal by particles of different radius <math>R</math> is considered by weighting <math>g_2(t)-1</math> with the form factor of sperical particles in Raylay-Debye-Gans approximation:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{DLS_Sphere_RDG} (t,\eta,T,Q) &=  \int\limits_0^\infty N(R) \; K^2_\text{sp}(Q,R) \: e^{-D Q^2t} \; dR \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} D        & = \frac{k_BT}{6\pi\eta R} \\K_\text{sp}(Q,R) & = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3  \, 3 \frac{\sin QR - QR \cos QR}{(QR)^3}\ R & : \text {radius} \\T & : \text {temperature} \\\eta & : \text {viscosity} \\Q & : \text {scattering vector} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Langevin==

\\

This function has been implemented to simulate the magnetisation curve <math>M(B)</math> of superparamagnetic particles following the Langevin statistics with a size distribution <math>N(R)</math>: Magnetization curve of superparamagnetic particles with magnetization a <math>M_s</math> at temperature <math>T</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} M(B) & = \dfrac{\int\limits_0^\infty N(R)\, \tfrac{4}{3}\pi R^3\, M_{p}(B,R) \; dR}{\int\limits_0^\infty N(R) \tfrac{4}{3}\pi R^3\;dR} \\[3mm] M_{p}(B,R)  & = M_\infty\left(\coth(\alpha) - \tfrac{1}{\alpha}\right)  \\\alpha       & = \dfrac{B M_s \tfrac{4}{3}\pi R^3}{k_B T}|dummy}}

\end{align}</math>



==SuperParStroboPsi==


In the following the scattering of polarized incident neutrons with polarization analysis (POLARIS) is described. Experimental measured scattering signal are always a mixture of the spin dependent scattering cross-sections <math>I^{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})</math> and <math>I^{\pm\mp}(\mathbf{Q})</math>. The relative contribution of these cross-sections to the measured cross-section depend on the polarisation of the polarizer <math>P_{pol}</math> the efficiency of the spin flipper <math>\epsilon</math> and the transmission values <math>T_\pm</math> of the polarization analyzer, which is assumed to be an opaque He-filter. If the neutron polarization of the polarizer is <math>P_{pol}\in [-1;1]</math> and the spin flipper is off the incident polarisation on the sample <math>P_{in}</math> is given by 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_{in} &= P_{pol}= \frac{N_+-N_-}{N_++N_-}=n_+-n_- \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} n_+&=\frac{N_+}{N_++N_-} \text{ and } n_- =\frac{N_-}{N_++N_-}\\\Rightarrow n_+ &= \frac{1+P_{pol}}{2} \text{ and } n_- =\frac{1-P_{pol}}{2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 <math>N_\pm</math> are the number of incident neutrons with spin polarizations up (<math>+</math>) and down (<math>-</math>). After switching on the spin flipper, which works with an efficiency of <math>\epsilon \in [0;1]</math> (<math>\epsilon=0</math>: flipper off, <math>\epsilon\lessapprox 1</math> : flipper on), one gets

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  n_+(P_{pol},\epsilon) &= \epsilon \; \frac{1-P_{pol}}{2}+(1-\epsilon) \; \frac{1+P_{pol}}{2} \quad \text{ and }\\n_-(P_{pol},\epsilon) &= \epsilon \; \frac{1+P_{pol}}{2}+(1-\epsilon) \; \frac{1-P_{pol}}{2}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The efficiency of the analyzer to filter spin up (<math>+</math>) or spin down (<math>-</math>) neutrons is given in case of an opaque spin filter by its transmission <math>T_\pm(t) \in [0;1]</math>, which can be a function of time, so that the measured scattering cross section <math>I_\text{m}(\mathbf{Q})</math> is given by \newlength\breiteb \settowidth\breiteb{<math>\displaystyle {}+{}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  I_\text{m}(\mathbf{Q}) = & \hspace{\breiteb} n_+(P_{pol},\epsilon) T_+(t) I_{++}(\mathbf{Q}) + n_+(P_{pol},\epsilon) T_-(t) I_{+-}(\mathbf{Q}) \\[3mm] &+                  n_-(P_{pol},\epsilon) T_-(t) I_{--}(\mathbf{Q}) + n_-(P_{pol},\epsilon) T_+(t) I_{-+}(\mathbf{Q})  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 The spin dependent scattering intensities of magnetic particles with an orientation distribution <math>f\left(\theta,\phi\right)</math> of its magnetic moment can easily be calculated in terms of order parameters <math>S_l</math> if one assumes that the particle are spherical symmetric or the orientation of the magnetic moment of a particle is not correlated to the particle orientation. Furthermore it will be assumed that an external magnetic field is applied perpendicular to the incident neutron beam and that all orientations <math>\phi</math> for a given angle <math>\theta</math>, which is defined as the angle between the magnetisation vector of the particle and the direction of the external field <math>\mathbf{B}</math> have the same probability, i.e. the orientation distribution only depends on <math>\theta</math> so that <math>f\left(\theta,\phi\right)=f\left(\theta\right)</math>. The orientation probability distribution function can be expanded in terms of a complete set of orthogonal functions. Expanding it in terms of Legendre polynomials <math>P_l(\cos\theta)</math> gives

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f(\theta) &= \sum_l c_l P_l(\cos\theta) = \sum_l \frac{2l+1}{2} S_l P_l(\cos\theta) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The expansion coefficients can be calculated by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  c_l &= \frac{2l+1}{2} \int_0^\pi f(\theta)\, P_l(\cos\theta) \sin\theta \;\mathrm{d}\theta\ \mbox{or}\\S_l &= \int_0^\pi f(\theta) \, P_l(\cos\theta) \sin\theta\;\mathrm{d}\theta  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The first three Legendre polynomials are defined by 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} P_0(\cos\theta) &= 1\ P_1(\cos\theta) &= \cos\theta\\P_2(\cos\theta) &= \frac{1}{2}\left(3\cos^2\theta-1\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 For superparamagnetic particle the orientation probability distribution is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f(\theta) = \frac{\alpha}{4\pi\sinh\alpha} \exp(\alpha\cos\theta) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\alpha=BM_pV_p/(k_BT)</math> being the Langevin parameter. For this orientation probability distribution the first order parameters can be calculates as 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_0 & = 1\\S_1 & = L(\alpha) = \coth\alpha - \frac{1}{\alpha}\\S_2 & = 1-3\frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 The scattering from a system of many particles is obtained by summing up the scattering amplitudes of all precipitates weighted by the phase shift at each particle position. In the decoupling approach scattering intensity is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  \frac{\mathrm{d}\sigma_{{\pm\pm}\atop{\pm\mp}}}{\mathrm{d}\Omega}(\mathbf{Q}) &= N\left\{ \left\langle {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_{{\pm\pm}\atop{\pm\mp}}(\mathbf{Q})}^2\right\rangle + {\left\vert \right\vert}{\left\langle F_{{\pm\pm}\atop{\pm\mp}}(\mathbf{Q})\right\rangle}^2 (S(\mathbf{Q})-1) \right\} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

which consists of two terms. The first one depends only on the particle structure and corresponds to the independent scattering of <math>N</math> particles, while the second one is also a function of their statistical distribution and reflects the interparticle interference, which is described by <math>S(\mathbf{Q})</math>. The <math>\langle\rangle</math> indicates an average over all possible configurations, sizes and orientations of the magnetic moments of the particles. The spin dependent scattering amplitudes <math>F_{{\pm\pm}\atop{\pm\mp}}(\mathbf{Q})</math> can be calculated from the nuclear and magnetic amplitudes

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q}) & = F_N(\mathbf{Q}) \mp F_{M_\perp x}(\mathbf{Q})\\F_{\pm\mp}(\mathbf{Q}) & = -\left(F_{M_\perp y}(\mathbf{Q}) \pm \imath F_{M_\perp z}(\mathbf{Q})\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The nuclear scattering amplitude is proportional to the nuclear excess scattering <math>\beta_N=F_N(Q=0)</math> and the nuclear form factor <math>f_N(\mathbf{Q})</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} F_N(\mathbf{Q}) &= \beta_N f_N(\mathbf{Q}) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The magnetic scattering amplitude <math>\mathbf{F}_{M_\perp}(\mathbf{Q})</math> can be described as a vector, with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathbf{F}_{M_\perp}(\mathbf{Q}) =  \mathbf{}{\hat{\mu}}_\perp D_M \mu f_M(\mathbf{Q})=\mathbf{}{\hat{\mu}}_\perp F_M(\mathbf{Q}) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>f_M(Q)</math> is the magnetic form factor, <math>\mathbf{\mu}=\mathbf{M}_pV_p</math> the magnetic moment of the particle, <math>D_M=\frac{\gamma e}{2\pi\hbar}</math>, and <math>\mathbf{}{\hat{\mu}}_\perp</math> the Halpern-Johnson vector defined as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathbf{}{\hat{\mu}}_\perp &= \frac{\mathbf{Q}}{Q}\times\left(\frac{\mathbf{\mu}}{\mu}\times\frac{\mathbf{Q}}{Q}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

It is assumed here that the neutron spin polarization is parallel or antiparallel to the axes <math>\mathbf{e}_x</math> which is the direction perpendicular to the incoming neutron beam. If only the Halpern-Johnson vector <math>\mathbf{}{\hat{\mu}}_\perp</math> depends on the orientation distribution <math>f(\theta)</math> of the magnetic moments <math>\mathbf{\mu}</math> of the particles but not the form factor <math>f_M(\mathbf{Q})</math>, which is valid for spherical symmetric particles or anisotropic shaped particles where the particle shape is not correlated to the direction of the magnetic moment, the averages in  can be written in terms of the order parameters <math>S_1</math> and <math>S_2</math> 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  \left\langle F_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})\right\rangle &=  F_N(\mathbf{Q}) +F_M(\mathbf{Q})S_1\sin^2\psi  \\[3mm] \left\langle F_{\pm\mp}(\mathbf{Q})\right\rangle &= F_M(\mathbf{Q})S_1\sin\psi\cos\psi  \\[3mm]  \left\langle {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})}^2\right\rangle &= {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_N(\mathbf{Q})}^2+{\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2 \left[S_2 \sin^4\psi+ \frac{1-S_2}{3} \sin^2\psi\right]\\& \mp \left[F_M(\mathbf{Q})F^*_N(\mathbf{Q})+F^*_M(\mathbf{Q})F_N(\mathbf{Q})\right]S_1\sin^2\psi  \\[3mm]  \left\langle {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_{\pm\mp}(\mathbf{Q})}^2\right\rangle &={\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2 \left[2\;\frac{1-S_2}{3}-S_2\sin^4\psi+\frac{4S_2-1}{3}\sin^2\psi\right]  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 The spin-flip and spin-nonflip cross-section <math>\frac{\mathrm{d}\sigma_{{\pm\pm}\atop{\pm\mp}}}{\mathrm{d}\Omega}(\mathbf{Q})</math> can be obtained by combining  and . The cross-sections without polarization analysis <math>I_\pm(\mathbf{Q})</math> and for unpolarized neutrons <math>I_{unp}(\mathbf{Q})</math> are given by 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  I_{\pm}(\mathbf{Q}) &= I_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})+I_{\pm\mp}(\mathbf{Q}) \\[2mm] &= \Bigl[{\left\vert \right\vert}{F_N(\mathbf{Q})}^2 + {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2S_1^2\sin^2\psi\\& \quad  \mp \left[F_M(\mathbf{Q})F^*_N(\mathbf{Q})+F^*_M(\mathbf{Q})F_N(\mathbf{Q})\right]S_1\sin^2\psi\Bigr] S(\mathbf{Q})\\& \quad {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\left(\frac{2}{3}\left(1-S_2\right)+\left(S_2-S_1^2\right)\sin^2\psi \right)  \\[3mm]  I_{unp}(\mathbf{Q}) &= \frac{1}{2}\left(I_{+}(\mathbf{Q})+I_{-}(\mathbf{Q})\right)\\[2mm] &=  \left({\left\vert \right\vert}{F_N(\mathbf{Q})}^2 + {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2S_1^2\sin^2\psi\right) S(\mathbf{Q})\\& \quad + {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\left(\frac{2}{3}\left(1-S_2\right)+\left(S_2-S_1^2\right)\sin^2\psi\right)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



In the case of a Boltzmann orientation distribution <math>f(\theta)=\exp\left(\frac{\mathbf{B\mu}}{k_BT}\right)=\exp\left(\frac{B\mu\cos\theta}{k_BT}\right)</math> the order parameter <math>S_l</math> already have been given in eq.  and the spin dependent intensities can be written as 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{I_{\pm\pm}(\mathbf{Q})}{N} &= {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q}) L(\alpha)\,\sin^2\psi \pm F_N(\mathbf{Q})}^2 S(\mathbf{Q})\\&+ {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2\, \left( \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\, \sin^2\psi - \left( L^2(\alpha)-1+3\,\frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\right) \, \sin^4\psi \right) \\\ \frac{I_{\mp\pm}(\mathbf{Q})}{N} &= \left( \sin^2\psi - \sin^4\psi \right) L^2(\alpha) {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2 S(\mathbf{Q})\\&+ {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_M(\mathbf{Q})}^2  \left( \left( \sin^4\psi-\sin^2\psi \right) \left( L^2(\alpha)-1+3\, \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\right) + (2-\sin^2\psi)\, \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 <math>\psi</math> is the angle between <math>\mathbf{Q}</math> and the horizontal axis in the plane of the detector. <math>L(\alpha)=\coth(\alpha)-\frac{1}{\alpha}</math> is the Langevin function. In the case of a static field the superparamagnetic particle are thermodynamic equilibrium and <math>\alpha</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \alpha&=\frac{BM_PV_P}{k_B T}, \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>M_P</math> being the particle magnetization, <math>V_P</math> the particle volume, <math>T</math> the temperature in Kelvin and <math>k_B</math> the Boltzmann constant.

In our experiments we applied an oscillating magnetic field to the sample described by: 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} B(t,\nu;d_\text{SD},\lambda,\rho_0) &= B_1 - B_0 \cos(\phi(t,\nu);\dots)\\\phi(t,\nu;d_\text{SD},\lambda,\rho_0) &=2\pi\nu(t-d_\text{SD}\lambda/3956)+\rho_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 where <math>t</math> in [s] is the time between neutron detection and the trigger signal from the frequency generator, <math>d_\text{SD}</math> in [m] is the sample detector distance, <math>\lambda </math> in [Å] the neutron wavelength, and <math>\nu</math> in [Hz] the frequency of the oscillating magnetic field. As <math>t</math> is defined here as the time of the neutron detection one has therefore to correct the phase in the argument for the magnetic field with an additional phase term. The term <math>t_\text{SD}=d_\text{SD}\lambda/3956</math> takes into account the flight time <math>t_\text{SD}</math> of the neutrons between the sample and the detector. The term  <math>\rho_0</math> accounts for any other additional constant phase shift between trigger signal and the magnetic field due to phase shifts in the amplifier. If the neutron polarization can follow adiabatically the varying magnetic field needs to be verified experimentally. Therefore we introduce here a parameter <math>a_\text{ad} \in [0;1]</math> which takes into account wether (<math>a_\text{ad}=1</math>) or not (<math>a_\text{ad}=0</math>) the neutron spin adiabatically follows the change of of magnetic field direction (<math>\text{sgn}(B(t))</math>).

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathbf{r}_\text{ad} = \left( \begin{array}{c} a_\text{ad}\\(1-a_\text{ad})\\\end{array} \right) \text{ and } \mathbf{s}_\text{ad} = \left( \begin{array}{c} \text{sgn}(B(t))\\1\\\end{array} \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The measured intensity than reads as 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I_\text{m}(\mathbf{Q},t) =& \sum_{i=1,2} r_{ad,i} \Bigg[\\&\hspace{\breiteb} n_+\left(s_{ad,i}P_{pol},\epsilon\right) A_+ I_{++}(\mathbf{Q},t) +                 n_+\left(s_{ad,i}P_{pol},\epsilon\right) A_- I_{+-}(\mathbf{Q},t) \\&+                 n_-\left(s_{ad,i}P_{pol},\epsilon\right) A_- I_{--}(\mathbf{Q},t) +                 n_-\left(s_{ad,i}P_{pol},\epsilon\right) A_+ I_{-+}(\mathbf{Q},t) \Bigg]   \\[3mm] = &\sum_{i=1,2} \; \sum_{k,l=+,-} r_{ad,i}\; n_k\left(s_{ad,i}P_{pol},\epsilon\right)\; A_l \; I_{kl}(\mathbf{Q},t) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align}  A_\pm &= \left(\frac{1+s_{ad,2}}{2}r_{ad,1} + \frac{1+s_{ad,1}}{2}r_{ad,2}\right) T_\pm(t)\\&+ \left(\frac{1-s_{ad,2}}{2}r_{ad,1} + \frac{1-s_{ad,1}}{2}r_{ad,2}\right) T_\mp(t)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 To calculate the time dependent scattering cross section a model for the time evolution of the orientation distribution of the magnetic moments <math>f(\theta,t)</math> is needed, from which the time dependent order parameter <math>S_1(t)</math> and <math>S_2(t)</math> can be obtained, as well as a model for the time evolution of the structure factor <math>S(\mathbf{Q},t)</math>. 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{\mathrm{d}M}{\mathrm{d}t} &= -\frac{M(t)-M_\infty L(\alpha'(t))}{\tau}\\M(t=0) &= M_0\\\alpha'(t) &= \alpha_0\cos(\omega t+\phi_0)+\alpha_1\\\alpha_0 &= \frac{-B_0\mu}{k_BT}\\\alpha_1 &= \frac{B_1\mu}{k_BT}\\\omega &= 2\pi\nu\\\phi_0 &= \varphi_0 - \frac{\omega d_\text{SD}\lambda}{3956} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 In the limit of small values for the Langevin parameter <math>\alpha</math> the Langevin function can be approximated by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} L(\alpha) \rightarrow \frac{\alpha}{3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

for which the differential equation has an analytical solution

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} M(t) &= e^{-\frac {t}{\tau}} \left[ M_0- M_{\infty}\left(\frac{\alpha_0}{3}\;\frac{ \cos \left( \phi_0 \right) +\omega\tau\, \sin\left( \phi_0 \right) }{1+{\omega}^{2}{\tau^{2}}} +\frac{\alpha_1}{3}\right)\right] \\&+M_\infty \left(\frac{\alpha_0}{3} \; \frac{\cos \left( \omega\,t+\phi_0 \right) + \omega\tau\,\sin \left( \omega\,t+\phi_0 \right) }{1+\omega^2\tau^2}+\frac{\alpha_1}{3}\right)\\&\underset{t\gg\tau}{\simeq} M_\infty \left(\frac{\alpha_0}{3} \; \frac{\cos \left( \omega\,t+\phi_0 -\frac{\pi}{2}+\arcsin\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{1+\omega^2\tau^2}}\right)\right) }{\sqrt{1+\omega^2\tau^2}}+\frac{\alpha_1}{3}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Assuming that the system is at any time in thermodynamic equilibrium with the actual magnetic field <math>B(t)</math> than the time dependent oscillating SANS signal can be described by simply  introducing a time dependent value for <math>\alpha(t)</math>:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \alpha(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0,\mu_\text{kT}) = B(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0) \frac{M_PV_P}{k_B T} = B(\dots)\; \mu_\text{kT} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mu_\text{kT} = \frac{M_PV_P}{k_B T} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

In case that the magnetic moments can not follow the external magnetic field this could be described by an additional phase <math>\Delta \phi_\alpha</math> between <math>\alpha</math> and <math>B</math> and a damping factor <math>d_\alpha</math> so that

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \alpha(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0-\Delta \phi_\alpha,\mu_\text{kT}) = d_{\alpha} B(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0-\Delta \phi_\alpha) \frac{M_PV_P}{k_B T}. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

In such a case of such

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathbf{r}_\text{ad} = \left( \begin{array}{c} (1-a_\text{ad}) \; \text{sgn}(B(t))\\a_\text{ad} \; \text{sign}(B(t)\alpha(t))\ \end{array} \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Furthermore we assume here that the size of the form factors of the magnetic and nuclear scattering are the same except the scattering contrast that means the ratio of magnetic to nuclear form factor is <math>Q</math>-independent and equal to the squared ratio of magnetic to nuclear scattering length density

{{eqn|<math> \frac{F_N^2(Q)}{\tilde{F}_M^2(Q)}  = const = \left( \frac{\Delta b_\text{nuc}}{\Delta b_\text{mag}}\right)^2 </math>|dummy}}

Therefore the time dependent signal on the detector for a given direction <math>\psi</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(t,\dots) = \int_{\lambda_0-\Delta\lambda}^{\lambda_0+\Delta\lambda} p_\triangle(\lambda) \;\; I_m(t,\lambda,\psi,\dots) \;\;  d\lambda \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>p_\triangle(\lambda)</math> describes the triangular shaped wave length distribution of the neutron beam.



==SuperParStroboPsiSQ==


The external applied field is given by:

{{eqn|<math> B(t,d_\text{SD},\lambda,\rho_0) = B_1 - B_0 \cos(2\pi\nu(t-d_\text{SD}\lambda/3956)+\rho_0) </math>|dummy}}

where <math>t</math> in [s] is the time between neutron detection and the trigger signal from the frequency generator, <math>d_\text{SD}</math> in [m] is the sample detector distance, <math>\lambda </math> in [Å] the neutron wavelength, and <math>\nu</math> in [Hz] the frequency of the oscillating magnetic field. As <math>t</math> is defined here as the time of the neutron detection one has therefore to correct the phase in the argument for the magnetic field with an addition phase term. The term <math>t_\text{SD}=d_\text{SD}\lambda/3956</math> takes account for the flight time <math>t_\text{SD}</math> of the neutrons between the sample and the detector. The term  <math>\rho_0</math> takes account for any other additional constant phase shift between trigger signal and the magnetic field due to phase shifts in the amplifier.

The scattering intensity of superparamagnetic particles including a structure factor <math>S(Q)</math>is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(Q) &= \underbrace{\tilde{F}_M^2(Q) \; 2 \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha} + F_N^2(Q)S(Q)}_{\displaystyle A(Q)}\\&+ \underbrace{\tilde{F}_M^2(Q) \left[\left(1-3 \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}-L^2(\alpha)\right)+L^2(\alpha)S(Q)\right]}_{\displaystyle B(Q)} \sin^2\Psi|dummy}}

\end{align}</math>

Assuming that the system is in equilibrium faster than <math>1/\nu</math> than the above equation can be used to describe the time dependent oscillating SANS signal simply by introducing a time dependent value for <math>\alpha</math>:

{{eqn|<math> \alpha(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0,\mu_\text{kT}) = B(t,\lambda,d_\text{SD},\rho_0) \frac{M_PV_P}{k_B T} = B(\dots)\; \mu_\text{kT} </math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math> \mu_\text{kT} = \frac{M_PV_P}{k_B T} </math>|dummy}}

Furthermore we assume here that the size of the form factors of the magnetic and nuclear scattering are the same except the scattering contrast that means the ratio of magnetic to nuclear form factor is <math>Q</math>-independent and equal to the squared ratio of magnetic to nuclear scattering length density

{{eqn|<math> \frac{F_N^2(Q)}{\tilde{F}_M^2(Q)}  = const = \left( \frac{\Delta b_\text{nuc}}{\Delta b_\text{mag}}\right)^2 </math>|dummy}}

Therefore the time dependent signal on the detector for a given direction <math>\Psi</math> and integrated over <math>Q</math> in this direction is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} i(t,\dots) &= \int_{Q_\text{min}}^{Q_\text{min}} I(Q,t,\lambda,\Psi,d_\text{SD},\rho_0) dQ \\&= c\left[2\frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha} + \frac{F_N^2(Q)}{\tilde{F}_M^2(Q)} S(Q) + \left[\left(1-3 \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}-L^2(\alpha)\right)+L^2(\alpha)S(Q)\right]\sin^2\Psi\right] \\&= c\left[2\frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha} + \left( \frac{\Delta b_\text{nuc}}{\Delta b_\text{mag}}\right)^2 S(Q) + \left[\left(1-3 \frac{L(\alpha)}{\alpha}-L^2(\alpha)\right)+L^2(\alpha)S(Q)\right]\sin^2\Psi\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math> y(t,\dots) = \int_{\lambda_0-\Delta\lambda}^{\lambda_0+\Delta\lambda} p_\triangle(\lambda) \;\; i(t,\lambda,\Psi,d_\text{SD},\rho_0) \;\;  d\lambda </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>p_\triangle(\lambda)</math> describes the triangular shaped wave length distribution of the neutron beam.

==SuperParStroboPsiSQBt==

\ The same as {\tt SuperParStroboPsiSQ} except that the structure factor <math>S(Q,t)</math> becomes field dependent:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(Q,t) = 1+\left[S(Q)-1\right] {\left\vert \right\vert}{\frac{B(t,d_\text{SD},\lambda,\rho_0)}{{\left\vert B_1\right\vert}+\frac{2}{\pi}{\left\vert B_0\right\vert}}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==SuperParStroboPsiSQLx==

The same as {\tt SuperParStroboPsiSQ} except that the structure factor <math>S(Q,t)</math> becomes field dependent:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(Q,t) = 1+\left[S(Q)-1\right] {\left\vert L(\alpha)\right\vert} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==SuperParStroboPsiSQL2x==

\ The same as {\tt SuperParStroboPsiSQ} except that the structure factor <math>S(Q,t)</math> becomes field dependent:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(Q,t) = 1+\left[S(Q)-1\right] L^2(\alpha) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Methods to include structure factors==

For each scattering object <math>i</math> next to a size distribution <math>N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i)</math> also a structure factor <math>S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i)</math> can be included. When a structure factor is included several theoretical ways to account for it have been implemented like the monodisperse approximation (), decoupling approach (), local monodisperse approximation (), partial structure factor () and scaling approximation of partial structure factors (). At the moment it is assumed that there are no interactions between different species of scatterers so that the total scattering is given by the sum of the scattering of the individual species

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}(Q) = \sum\limits_{i=1}^N \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) </math>|dummy}}

whereby different approaches to include structure factor effects in the differential scattering cross-sections <math>\frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q)</math> of the species <math>i</math> of scatterer are defined below.

==Monodisperse approach==

\ The monodisperse approach is the simplest way to include a structure factor in the analysis. This approach simply multiplies the size averaged form factor with the structure factor. Here it is assumed that the interaction potential between particles are spherical symmetric and independent of the particle size.

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) = \left[ \int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx \right] S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i) </math>|dummy}}

==Decoupling approximation==

\ For systems with small polydispersities and small anisotropies leads to a decoupling approach of Kotlarchyk and Chen <ref name="Kotlarchyk1983"> M. Kotlarchyk and S.-H. Chen. Analysis of small angle neutron scattering spectra from polydisperse interacting colooids. ''J. Chem. Phys'', 79(5):2461--2489, 1983.

</ref>. It is assumed that interactions are independent of particle size and orientation.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) = & \int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx +  \frac{1}{n_i}\left[\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx\right]^2  \\\times & \left[S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i)-1\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math> n_i = \int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i)  dx.  </math>|dummy}}

The decoupling approximation can only be combined with those form factor, for which the scattering amplitude <math>F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math> has been implemented. However, for many form factors only the scattering intensity <math>F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math> is available. The combination of those form factors with the decoupling approach produces an error message in {{sasfit}}.

==Local monodisperse approximation==

\ The opposite limit of the approximations as used for the decoupling approximation is used in the local monodisperse approximation <ref name="Pedersen1994"> .S. Pedersen. Determination of size distributions from small-angle scattering data for systems with effective hard-sphere interactions. ''J. Appl. Cryst.'', 27:595--608, 1994.

</ref>. In this approach it is assumed that a particle of a certain size is always surrounded by particles with the same size. Following this the scattering is approximated by that of monodisperse sub-systems weighted by the size distribution:

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) = \int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i,R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)) dx </math>|dummy}}

in which it has been indicated that the structure factor is for particles of size <math>R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math>. As the distribution <math>N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i)</math> does not necessarily describe the distribution of the overall size. {{sasfit}} assumes, that the radius of a particle with the form factor <math>F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math> used in the structure factor is given by the radius of a sphere with the same volume <math>V_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math>

{{eqn|<math> R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x) = \sqrt[3]{\frac{3}{4 \pi}V_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)}.  </math>|dummy}}

This local monodisperse approximation works better than the decoupling approximation for systems with larger polydispersities and higher concentrations. As compared to the decoupling approximation and the scaling approximation described below, it has the advantage that the cross section is linear in the size distribution.

==partial structure factors==

\ For polydisperse systems it is also not possible to write the scattering cross section as a product of a form factor and a structure factor. The scattering cross section has the form

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) = &\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx\\+ & \frac{1}{n_i}\int_0^\infty\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i)N_i(x';{\underline{ l}}_i)F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x)F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x') \\\times & \left[S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i,R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x),R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x'))-1\right] dx dx' \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where monodisperse structure factor <math>S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i,\dots)</math> is evaluated for the radius <math>(R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)+R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x'))/2</math>. <math>n_i</math> and <math>R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math> have the same definition as those in eq.  and .

==Scaling approximation==

\ A scaling approximation has recently been introduced by Gazzillo et al. <ref name="Gazzillo1999"> Domenico Gazzillo, Achille Giacometti, Raffaele Guido Della Valle, Elisabetta Venuti, and Flavio Carsughi. A scaling approximation for structure factors in the integral equation theory of polydisperse nonionic colloidal fluids. ''The Journal of Chemical Physics'', 111(16):7636--7645, 1999.

</ref>. It is assumed that the pair correlation functions are identical except for a scaling constant. This leads to the following expression:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \frac{d\sigma_i}{d\Omega}(Q) = &\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i^2(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx\\+ & \frac{1}{n_i}\int_0^\infty\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) N_i(x';{\underline{ l}}_i) F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x) F_i(Q;{\underline{ a}}_i,x') \\\times & \frac{V_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)+V_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x')}{2V_{i,av}} \left[S_i(Q;{\underline{ s}}_i,R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x),R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x'))-1\right] \;dx dx' \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>V_{i,av}</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math> V_{i,av} = \frac{\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) V_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x) dx}{\int_0^\infty N_i(x;{\underline{ l}}_i) dx} </math>|dummy}}

and the monodisperse structure factor is evaluated for the radius <math>(R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)+R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x'))/2</math>. <math>n_i</math> and <math>R_i({\underline{ a}}_i,x)</math> have the same definition as those in eq.  and . Note that the expression is not linear in the size distribution and that it involves double integrations, which makes least-square fitting with this expression relatively slow.

==Hard \& Sticky Hard Sphere==

==Hard Sphere==

[Percus 1958] [Vrij 1979]
* [*Percus 1958] J. K. Percus and G. J. Yevick, ''Analysis of classical statistical mechanics by means of collective coordinates'', Phys. Rev., 110(1):1--13, 1958
* [*Vrij 1979] A. Vrij, ''Mixtures of hard spheres in the percus-yevick approximation. light scattering at finite angles'', J. Chem. Phys., 71(8):3267--3270, 1979

{{eqn|<math> U(r) = \begin{cases} \infty    & \text{for} \quad 0<r<\sigma\\0         & \text{for} \quad r>\sigma \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \alpha &= \frac{\left(1+2f_p\right)^2}{\left(1-f_p\right)^4}\\\beta  &= -6 f_p \frac{\left(1 +f_p/2 \right)^2}{\left(1-f_p\right)^4}\\\gamma &= \frac{f_p \alpha}{2} \\A &= 2 R_\text{HS} q \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} G(q) =  & \, \alpha \, \frac{\sin A -A \cos A }{A^2} + \beta \, \frac{2 A \sin A +(2-A^2) \cos A -2}{A^3} + \\& \, \gamma  \, \frac{-A^4 \cos A + 4\left[(3A^2-6)\cos A+(A^3-6A)\sin A+6\right]}{A^5} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{HS}(q,R_\text{HS},f_p)  = & \cfrac{1}{1+24 f_p \cfrac{G(f_p,R_\text{HS}q)}{R_\text{HS}q}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}
