==Sticky Hard Sphere==

In Baxter's model [Baxter 1968] [Robertus 1989] [Kruif 1989] [Barboy 1974] [Menon 1991] [Menon 1991a]

==References==
* [*Baxter 1968] R.J. Baxter, ''Percus-yevick equation for hard spheres with surface adhesion'', J. Chem. Phys., 49(6):2770--2774, 1968
* [*Robertus 1989] C. Robertus, W.H. Philipse, J.G.H. Joosten, and Y.K. Levine, ''Solution of the percus-yevick approximation of the multicomponent adhesive sphere system to the small angle x-ray scattering from microemulsions'', J. Chem. Phys., 90(8):4482--4490, 1989.
* [*Kruif 1989] C. G. de Kruif, P. W. Rouw, W. J. Briels, M. H. G. Duits, A. Vrij, and R. P. May, ''Adhesive hard-sphere colloidal dispersions. a small-angle neutron-scattering study of stickiness and the structure factor'', Langmuir, 5:422--428, 1989
* [*Barboy 1974] B. Barboy, ''On a representation of the equation of state of fluids in terms of the adhesive hard-sphere model'', J. Chem. Phys., 61(8):3194--3196, 1974
* [*Menon 1991] S.V.G. Menon, V.K. Kelkar, and C. Monohar, ''Application of baxter's model to the theory of cloud points of nonionic surfactant solutions'', Physical Review A, 43(2):1130--1133, 1991
* [*Menon 1991a] S.V.G. Menon, C. Manobar, and K. Srinivasa Rao, ''A new interpretation of a sticky hard sphere model'', J. Chem. Phys., 95(12):9186--9190, 1991
<HarvardReferences/>

of adhesive hard spheres the pair interaction potential <math>U(r)</math> is replaces by

{{eqn|<math> \frac{U(r)}{k_BT} = \begin{cases} \infty    & \text{for} \quad 0<r<\sigma\\\ln\frac{12\tau\Delta}{\sigma+\Delta} & \text{for} \quad \sigma<r<\sigma+\Delta\\0         & \text{for} \quad r>\sigma+\Delta \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

after which, when applied, the limit <math>\Delta \to 0</math> is taken. Thus, only a single parameter, the so-called stickiness parameter <math>\tau</math>, characterizes the adhesive strength. 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \kappa &= 2 q R_\text{HS}\\\eta &= f_p \left(\frac{2R_\text{HS}+\Delta}{2R_\text{HS}}\right)^3\ \epsilon &= \tau+\frac{\eta}{1-\eta}\\\gamma &= f_p\frac{1+\eta/2}{3\left(1-\eta\right)^2}\\\lambda &= \frac{6}{\eta} \left(\epsilon-\sqrt{\epsilon^2-\gamma}\right)\ \mu &= \lambda \eta (1-\eta)\\\beta &= -\frac{3\eta \left(2+\eta\right)^2-2\mu \left(1+7\eta+\eta^2\right)+\mu^2(2+\eta)}{2\left(1-\eta\right)^4}\ \alpha &= \frac{\left(1+2\eta-\mu\right)^2}{\left(1-\eta\right)^4}\\[5mm] C(q) = & 2\frac{\eta\lambda}{\kappa}\sin\kappa -2\frac{\eta^2\lambda^2}{\kappa^2}\left(1-\cos\kappa\right) -\ \Big\{ & \alpha\kappa^3(\sin\kappa-\kappa\cos\kappa) +\beta\kappa^2(2\kappa\sin\kappa-(\kappa^2-2)\cos\kappa-2)\ & +\frac{\eta\alpha}{2}\left((4\kappa^3-24\kappa)\sin\kappa-(\kappa^4-12\kappa^2+24)\cos\kappa+24\right) \Big\} \;\times\;24\frac{\eta}{\kappa^6} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{sHS}(q,R_\text{HS},f_p,\tau) & = \frac{1}{1-C(q)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Sticky Hard Sphere (2nd)==

</math> version 

[Regnaut 1989] [Regnaut 1990]
* [*Regnaut 1989] C. Regnaut and J. C. Ravey, ''Application of the adhesive sphere model to the structure of colloidal suspensions'', The Journal of Chemical Physics, 91(2):1211--1221, 1989
* [*Regnaut 1990] C. Regnaut and J. C. Ravey, ''Erratum: Application of the adhesive sphere model to the structure of colloidal suspensions [j. chem. phys. 91, 1211 (1989)]'', The Journal of Chemical Physics, 92(5):3250--3250, 1990

In Baxter's model of adhesive hard spheres the pair interaction potential <math>U(r)</math> is replaces by

{{eqn|<math> \frac{U(r)}{k_BT} = \begin{cases} \infty    & \text{for} \quad 0<r<\sigma\\\ln\frac{12\tau\Delta}{\sigma+\Delta} & \text{for} \quad \sigma<r<\sigma+\Delta\\0         & \text{for} \quad r>\sigma+\Delta \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sigma &= 2R_\text{HS}+\Delta\\\kappa & = q \sigma\\\phi   &= f_p \left(\frac{\sigma}{2R_\text{HS}}\right)^3\\\lambda_{\pm} & =6 \left(\frac{\tau}{\phi}+\frac{1}{1-\phi}\right) \pm \sqrt{ 36 \left[ \frac{\tau}{\phi}+\frac{1}{1-\phi} \right]^2 -\frac{12}{\phi}\frac{1+\frac{\phi}{2}}{\left(1-\phi\right)^{2}} }\\\lambda & = \begin{cases} \lambda_+ & \mbox{for }\lambda_+ < {\left\vert \lambda_-\right\vert}\\\lambda_- & \mbox{otherwise} \end{cases} \\[5mm] \mu & = \lambda \phi (1-\phi)\\A & = \frac12 \; \frac{1+2 \phi-\mu}{\left(1-\phi\right)^2}\\B & = \frac{\sigma}{2} \frac{\mu-3\phi}{2 \left(1-\phi\right)^2 }\\C & = -A\sigma^2-B\sigma+\lambda\sigma^2/12\\I_n(\kappa) &= \int_0^1 x^n \cos(\kappa x)\; dx\\J_n(\kappa) &= \int_0^1 x^n \sin(\kappa x)\; dx\\\alpha & = 1-12 f_p \left( C\sigma^{-2}I_0(\kappa) + B\sigma^{-1} I_1(\kappa) +AI_2(\kappa) \right)\ \beta  &=    12 f_p \left( C\sigma^{-2}J_0(\kappa) + B\sigma^{-1} J_1(\kappa) +AJ_2(\kappa) \right)\\S(Q) & = \frac{1}{\alpha^2+\beta^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Square Well Potential \cite{Sharma1977==

} \\The Square well potential can be written as

{{eqn|<math> U(r) = \begin{cases} \infty    & \text{for} \quad 0<r<\sigma\\-\epsilon & \text{for} \quad \sigma<r<\lambda\sigma\\0         & \text{for} \quad r>\lambda\sigma \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\lambda</math> and <math>\epsilon</math> correspond to the breadth and the depth of the square well potential. The structure factor <math>S(Q)</math> is than given by the following relations: \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(Q)  = & \frac{1}{1-C(Q)}\\C(Q)  = & - \frac{24\eta}{(Q\sigma)^6} \Big\{ \alpha(Q\sigma)^3 \left[\sin(Q\sigma)-Q\sigma\cos(Q\sigma)\right]\\& +\beta(Q\sigma)^2\left[2Q\sigma\sin(Q\sigma)-(Q^2\sigma^2-2)\cos(Q\sigma)-2\right]\ & +\gamma \left[(4Q^3\sigma^3-24Q\sigma)\sin(Q\sigma)-(Q^4\sigma^4-12Q^2\sigma^2+24)\cos(Q\sigma)+24\right]\\& -\frac{\epsilon}{k_BT} (Q\sigma)^3\left[\sin(\lambda Q\sigma)-\lambda Q\sigma\cos(\lambda Q\sigma)+Q\sigma\cos(Q\sigma)-\sin(Q\sigma)\right]\Big\} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\alpha</math>, <math>\beta</math> and <math>\gamma</math> are given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \alpha & = \frac{(1+2\eta)^2+\eta^3(\eta-4)}{(1-\eta)^4}\\\beta  & = -\frac{1}{3}\eta\frac{18+20\eta-12\eta^2+\eta^4}{(1-\eta)^4}\\\gamma & = \frac{1}{2}\eta\frac{(1+2\eta)^2+\eta^3(\eta-4)}{(1-\eta)^4} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} \\NOTE:\ Values for the depth of <math>\epsilon>1.5k_BT</math> and for the volume fraction of <math>\eta> 0.08</math> may give unphysical results when compared to Monte Carlo simulations according to <ref name="Sharma1977"> R. V. Sharma and K. C. Sharma. The structure factor and the transport properties of dense fluids having molecules with square well potential, a possible generalization. ''Physica A: Statistical and Theoretical Physics'', 89(1):213 -- 218, 1977.

</ref>. 
 FIGURE DELETED 

==Square Well Potential 2==

The Square well potential can be written as

{{eqn|<math> U(r) = \begin{cases} \infty    & \text{for} \quad 0<r<\sigma\\-\epsilon & \text{for} \quad \sigma<r<\sigma+\Delta\\0         & \text{for} \quad r>\sigma+\Delta \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\Delta</math> and <math>\epsilon</math> correspond to the width and the depth of the square well potential. The structure factor <math>S(Q)</math> is than given by the following relations:

{{eqn|<math> S(Q)  = 1 -4\pi\rho\sigma^3\frac{\sin(Q\sigma)-Q\sigma\cos(Q\sigma)}{Q^3\sigma^3} +4\pi\rho\sigma^2\left[e^{\frac{\epsilon}{k_BT}}-1\right]\frac{\sin(Q\sigma)}{Q\sigma} \Delta </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\sigma</math> is the particle diameter (<math>R_{HS} = \sigma/2</math>: hard sphere radius is requested by software as input parameter), <math>\Delta</math> the width of the square well potential, <math>\epsilon</math> (input value in software is <math>\epsilon/k_B</math>, i.e. in Kelvin), <math>T</math> (in Kelvin) the sample temperature, the depth and <math>\rho</math> the colloid concentration, which is related to the colloid volume fraction <math>\eta</math> by <math>\eta=\pi\rho\sigma^3/6</math>. 

==Multi Lamellar Structures <ref name="Pabst2003"> G. Pabst, R. Koschuch, B. Pozo-Navas, M. Rappolt, K. Lohner, and P. Laggner. Structural analysis of weakly ordered membrane stacks. ''Journal of Applied Crystallography'', 36(6):1378--1388, Dec 2003.

</ref>\cite{Fruhwirth2004==

}

==Multi-Lamellar Structures, Thermal Disorder==

\ 
 FIGURE DELETED The first type describes thermal disorder (TD) caused by small fluctuations of the bilayers around well defined mean layer positions of equal separation (Fig. ). In such a crystal lattice the long-range order is preserved and the structure factor for a single domain of size <math>L = Nd</math> is identical to that of a perfect finite crystal multiplied by the well known Debye-Waller temperature factor, where <math>\Delta = \left< (d_k-d)^2\right></math> denotes the mean square fluctuations of the bilayers. As shown in Fig. , <math>S_\text{TD}(Q)</math> is characterized by a set of Bragg peaks of equal width, the diffraction order amplitudes of which decrease exponentially with the Debye-Waller factor. The lost intensity is found as a diffuse background scattering, which increases to the limit of <math>N</math> for large <math>Q</math>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{TD}(Q,N,d,\Delta,N_\text{diff}) = N_\text{diff} + \sum_{N_k=N-2\sigma}^{N+2\sigma} x_k S_\text{k,TD} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{k,TD} & = \left( N_k + 2 \exp\left( -\frac{Q^2\Delta^2}{2}\right) \sum_{m=1}^{N_k-1} (N_k-m) \cos(mQd) \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>N_\text{diff}</math> accounts for an additional diffuse background, due to a number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers in <math>S_\text{TD}(Q,N,d,\Delta,N_\text{diff})</math>, which is not included in the TD theory. Its origin is attributed to bilayers with strong lattice defects or unilamellar vesicles, which display neither short-range nor (quasi-) long-range order. The structure factors <math>S_\text{k,TD}(Q)</math> with low, but fixed stacking numbers <math>N</math> show oscillations at low <math>Q</math> (as can be seen in Fig. ), but no such oscillations are found in experimental data. This can be understood as the consequence of polydispersity in the size of the different stacks. In order to eliminate these artifacts from strictly monodisperse systems, we use a `polydisperse' structure factor, i.e. we use an average of a series of structure factors with varying numbers of bilayers <ref name="Fruhwirth2004"> Thomas Fr{\"u}hwirth, Gerhard Fritz, Norbert Freiberger, and Otto Glatter. {Structure and order in lamellar phases determined by small-angle scattering}. ''Journal of Applied Crystallography'', 37(5):703--710, Oct 2004.

</ref>. The analytical form of the distribution is not known a priori. We use a Gaussian distribution approximated by a discrete series The standard deviation <math>\sigma</math> for the Gaussian-weighted distribution is chosen as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sigma = \begin{cases} \sqrt{N} & \text{for} N\geq 5 \text{,}\\0.5(N-1) & \text{for} N< 5 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Therefore, <math>N</math> must be greater or equal to 2, which is a reasonable restriction for multilamellar stacks of bilayers. In the range of <math>N \pm 2\sigma</math>, structure factors weighted by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_k & = \frac{1}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left[ -\frac{(N_k-N)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

are calculated, where <math>N</math> is the mean number of stacks and <math>N_k</math> is one of the  bilayers in the range <math>N\pm 2\sigma</math>. This polydispersity model does not introduce new free parameters and is symmetrical around the mean <math>N</math>. 
 FIGURE DELETED   \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|ThermalDisorder}}:}  
#[{{mono|N}}] mean number of stacks <math>N</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] stacking separation <math>d</math> 
#[{{mono|Delta}}]  Debye-Waller disorder parameter <math>\Delta</math> 
#[{{mono|Nu}}]   number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers <math>N_\text{diff}</math>  \underline{Note:}  
# This structure factor is intended to be used with the {{mono|monodisperse approximation}}.  

==Multi-Lamellar Structures, Paracrystalline Theory==

\ 
 FIGURE DELETED The second type of disorder accounts for the presence of small variations in the bilayer separations (Fig. ), so-called stacking disorder, and is described within the paracrystalline theory (PT) <ref name="Hosemann1962"> R. Hosemann and S.N. Bagchi. ''Direct Analysis of Diffraction by Matter''. Amsterdam: North-Holland., 1962.

</ref><ref name="Guinier1963"> A. Guinier. ''X-ray Diffraction''. San Francisco: Freeman, 1963.

</ref><ref name="Blaurock1982"> A. E. Blaurock. Evidence of bilayer structure and of membrane interactions from x-ray diffraction analysis. ''Biochimica et Biophysica Acta (BBA) - Reviews on Biomembranes'', 650(4):167 -- 207, 1982.

</ref>. As the position of an individual fluctuating layer in a paracrystal is determined solely by its nearestneighbour membranes, the crystalline long-range order is lost. Still, we are able to observe Bragg-peak scattering due to the fact that there is quasi long-range order. However, these quasi-Bragg peaks will display a typical line shape. In the case of disorder of the second kind, the structure factor derived from paracrystalline theory is given by <ref name="Guinier1963"/>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{PT}(Q,N,d,\Delta,N_\text{diff}+) = N_\text{diff}+\sum_{N_k=N-2\sigma}^{N+2\sigma} x_k S_\text{k,PT} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{k,PT} &= \left( N_k + 2 \sum_{m=1}^{N_k-1} (N_k-m) \cos(mQd) \exp\left( -\frac{m^2Q^2\Delta^2}{2}\right) \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>N_\text{diff}</math> accounts for an additional diffuse background, due to a number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers in <math>S_\text{PT}(Q,N,d,\Delta,N_\text{diff})</math>, which is not included in the paracrystalline theory. Its origin is attributed to bilayers with strong lattice defects or unilamellar vesicles, which display neither short-range nor (quasi-) long-range order. Fig.  shows that the quasi-Bragg peak intensity decreases for <math>S_\text{PT}(Q)</math>, as in the previous case of thermal disorder. However, the decrease in peak height is also accompanied by a progressive broadening proportional to the square of the diffraction order <math>h</math> <ref name="Schwartz1975"> S. Schwartz, J.E. Cain, E.A. Dratz, and J.K. Blasie. An analysis of lamellar x-ray diffraction from disordered membrane multilayers with application to data from retinal rod. ''Biophysical Journal'', 15:1201 -- 1233, 1975.

</ref>. The line shape of the tails is essentially Lorentzian with

{{eqn|<math>S_\text{k,PT}(Q) \propto (Q - Q_h)^2,</math>|dummy}}

where <math>Q_h</math> is the position of the <math>h^\text{th}</math> diffraction order in <math>Q</math> space. Again, the loss in intensity shows up as diffuse background scattering, which is stronger than from pure thermal disorder. The structure factors <math>S_\text{k,PT}(Q)</math> with low, but fixed stacking numbers <math>N</math> show oscillations at low <math>Q</math> (as can be seen in Fig. ), but no such oscillations are found in experimental data. This can be understood as the consequence of polydispersity in the size of the different stacks. In order to eliminate these artifacts from strictly monodisperse systems, we use a `polydisperse' structure factor, i.e. we use an average of a series of structure factors with varying numbers of bilayers <ref name="Fruhwirth2004"/>. The analytical form of the distribution is not known a priori. We use a Gaussian distribution approximated by a discrete series The standard deviation <math>\sigma</math> for the Gaussian-weighted distribution is chosen as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sigma = \begin{cases} \sqrt{N} & \text{for} N\geq 5 \text{,}\\0.5(N-1) & \text{for} N< 5 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Therefore, <math>N</math> must be greater or equal to 2, which is a reasonable restriction for multilamellar stacks of bilayers. In the range of <math>N \pm 2\sigma</math>, structure factors weighted by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_k & = \frac{1}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left[ -\frac{(N_k-N)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

are calculated, where <math>N</math> is the mean number of stacks and <math>N_k</math> is one of the  bilayers in the range <math>N\pm 2\sigma</math>. This polydispersity model does not introduce new free parameters and is symmetrical around the mean <math>N</math>. 
 FIGURE DELETED   \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Paracrystalline}}:}  
#[{{mono|N}}] mean number of stacks <math>N</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] stacking separation <math>d</math> 
#[{{mono|Delta}}]  stacking disorder parameter <math>\Delta</math> 
#[{{mono|Nu}}]   number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers <math>N_\text{diff}</math>  \underline{Note:}  
# This structure factor is intended to be used with the {{mono|monodisperse approximation}}.  

==Multi-Lamellar Structures, Modified Caill\'e Theory==

\ 
 FIGURE DELETED There is another type of disorder when bilayer bending fluctuations are considered (Fig. ). Such fluctuations are particularly pronounced in the fluid <math>L_\alpha</math> phase (equivalent to smectic A). Caill\'e Bending fluctuation disorder is a particular feature of the <math>L_\alpha</math> (smectic A) phase and is caused by bilayer undulations. The particular shape of the Bragg peaks is given by the modified Caill\'e theory (MCT). <ref name="Caille1972"> A. Caille. X-ray scattering in smectic a. ''Comptes Rendus des Seances de l'Academie des Sciences, Serie B: Sciences Physiques'', 274:891--893, 1972.

</ref> realized the impact on the structure factor, which in a modified version <ref name="Zhang1994"> Ruitian Zhang, Robert M. Suter, and John F. Nagle. Theory of the structure factor of lipid bilayers. ''Phys. Rev. E'', 50(6):5047--5060, Dec 1994.

</ref> of the Caill\'e theory (MCT) is

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{MC}(Q,N,d,\eta_1,\gamma, N_\text{diff}) = N_\text{diff}+ \sum_{N_k=N-2\sigma}^{N+2\sigma} x_k S_\text{k,MC} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{k,MC} = N_k + 2 \sum_{m=1}^{N_k-1} (N_k-m) \cos(mQd) \,\, e^{ -\left(\frac{d}{2\pi}\right)^2 Q^2 \eta_1 \gamma } \,\, (\pi m)^{-(d/2\pi)^2Q^2\eta_1} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Here, <math>\gamma</math> is Euler's constant and

{{eqn|<math>\eta = \pi k_B T / 2d^2(BK_c)^{1/2}</math>|dummy}}

is the Caill\'e parameter, which is a measure for the bilayer fluctuations and is inversely proportional to the square root of the bilayer bending rigidity <math>K_c</math> times the bulk modulus of compression <math>B</math> (De Gennes \& Prost, 1993). Therefore, a lineshape analysis of the quasi-Bragg peaks opens an important experimental window on interbilayer interactions. Further, <math>K_c</math> and <math>B</math> can be decoupled as demonstrated recently by hydration studies <ref name="Petrache1998"> Horia I. Petrache, Nikolai Gouliaev, Stephanie Tristram-Nagle, Ruitian Zhang, Robert M. Suter, and John F. Nagle. Interbilayer interactions from high-resolution x-ray scattering. ''Phys. Rev. E'', 57(6):7014--7024, Jun 1998.

</ref><ref name="Pabst2003"/>, or more elegantly by measuring multibilayers aligned on a solid substrate <ref name="Lyatskaya2000"> Yulia Lyatskaya, Yufeng Liu, Stephanie Tristram-Nagle, John Katsaras, and John F. Nagle. Method for obtaining structure and interactions from oriented lipid bilayers. ''Phys. Rev. E'', 63(1):011907, Dec 2000.

</ref>. <math>N_\text{diff}</math> accounts for an additional diffuse background, due to a number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers in <math>S_\text{MC}(Q,N,d,\eta_1,\gamma, N_\text{diff}) </math>, which is not included in the MCT. Its origin is attributed to bilayers with strong lattice defects or unilamellar vesicles, which display neither short-range nor (quasi-) long-range order. Fig.  shows a typical example of <math>S_\text{k,MCT}(Q)</math>, which is similar to <math>S_\text{PT}(Q)</math> with respect to the progressive decrease in peak height and increase in peak width, but which differs significantly in line shape as

{{eqn|<math>S_\text{k,MCT} \propto (Q-Q_h)^{-1+\eta h^2}</math>|dummy}}

for randomly oriented scattering domains <ref name="Roux1988"> D. Roux and C.R. Safinya. A synchrotron x-ray study of competing undulation and electrostatic interlayer interactions in fluid multimembrane lyotropic phases. ''J. Phys. France'', 49(2):307--318, 1988.

</ref><ref name="Zhang1994"/>. The structure factors <math>S_\text{k,MCT}(Q)</math> with low, but fixed stacking numbers <math>N</math> show oscillations at low <math>Q</math> (as can be seen in Fig. ), but no such oscillations are found in experimental data. This can be understood as the consequence of polydispersity in the size of the different stacks. In order to eliminate these artifacts from strictly monodisperse systems, we use a `polydisperse' structure factor, i.e. we use an average of a series of structure factors with varying numbers of bilayers <ref name="Fruhwirth2004"/>. The analytical form of the distribution is not known a priori. We use a Gaussian distribution approximated by a discrete series The standard deviation <math>\sigma</math> for the Gaussian-weighted distribution is chosen as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \sigma = \begin{cases} \sqrt{N} & \text{for} N\geq 5 \text{,}\\0.5(N-1) & \text{for} N< 5 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Therefore, <math>N</math> must be greater or equal to 2, which is a reasonable restriction for multilamellar stacks of bilayers. In the range of <math>N \pm 2\sigma</math>, structure factors weighted by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_k & = \frac{1}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left[ -\frac{(N_k-N)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

are calculated, where <math>N</math> is the mean number of stacks and <math>N_k</math> is one of the  bilayers in the range <math>N\pm 2\sigma</math>. This polydispersity model does not introduce new free parameters and is symmetrical around the mean <math>N</math>. 
 FIGURE DELETED   \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|ModifiedCaile}}:}  
#[{{mono|N}}] mean number of stacks <math>N</math> 
#[{{mono|d}}] stacking separation <math>d</math> 
#[{{mono|eta}}]  the Caill\'e parameter <math>\eta</math> is a measure for the bilayer fluctuations and inversely proportional to the square root of the bilayer bending rigidity 
#[{{mono|Nu}}]   number of uncorrelated scattering bilayers <math>N_\text{diff}</math>  \underline{Note:}  
# This structure factor is intended to be used with the {{mono|monodisperse approximation}}.  

==Mass Fractal==

For a fractal object, the structure factor <math>S(q)</math> can be calculated <ref name="Sorensen1999"/><ref name="Sorensen1992"/> via the pair correlation function <math>g(r)</math>, which describes the total number of particles within a sphere of radius <math>r</math> centered in a central particle and is given (for <math>\dim = 3</math>) by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} N(r) = \Phi \int_0^r g(r) \, 4\pi r^2 \, dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

or

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \textrm{d}N(r) = \Phi  g(r) \, 4\pi r^2 \, dr  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

On the other hand, a fractal object is characterized by a spatial distribution of the individual scatterers given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} N(r) = \left( \frac{r}{r_0} \right)^D  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>r_0</math> is the gauge of the measurement, which has the magnitude of the characteristic dimension of each individual scatterer. Differentiation of  and identification with  gives

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Phi g(r) = \frac{D}{4\pi r_0^D} r^{D-3} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Because <math>D</math> is smaller than 3, <math>g(r)</math> goes to zero at large <math>r</math>. This is clearly unphysical. At some large scale, the sample will show a macroscopic density. A good knowledge of the sample allows in general a reasonable assumption for the large-scale behavior of <math>g(r)</math>. Therefore a cut-off function <math>h(r,\xi)</math> has to be introduced, where <math>\xi</math> is a cut-off distance, to describe the behavior of the pair correlation function at large distances. To derive the analytical form of <math>S(q)</math> within this assumption, one can use the general theory of liquids, where the uniform density is subtracted to avoid a divergence in the evaluation of <math>S(q)</math>. We then write

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} 4\pi\Phi[g(r)-1] = \frac{D}{r_0^D} \, r^{D-3} h(r,\xi)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The meaning of <math>\xi</math> is only qualitative and has to be made precise in any particular situation. Generally speaking, it represents the characteristic distance above which the mass distribution in the sample is no longer described by the fractal law. In practice, it can represent the size of an aggregate or a correlation length in a disordered material. For isotropic systems

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(q) = 1+4\pi\Phi\int_0^\infty [g(r)-1] \frac{\sin(qr)}{qr} \, r^2 \, dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Combined with  one gets

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S(q) = 1 +\frac{D}{r_0^D} \int_0^\infty r^{D-3} h(r,\xi)  \frac{\sin(qr)}{qr}\, r^2 \, dr  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Several cut-off functions <math>h(r,\xi)</math> have been discussed in the literature and compared by Sorensen et al. <ref name="Sorensen1999"/><ref name="Sorensen1992"/>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} h_\text{Exp}(r,\xi)     &= \exp\left[-\tfrac{r}{\xi}\right]\\h_\text{Gauss}(r,\xi)   &= \exp\left[-\left(\tfrac{r}{\xi}\right)^2\right]\\h_\text{Exp(-x<math>\hat{ }</math>a)}(r,\xi,\alpha,D) &= \exp\left[-\left(\tfrac{r}{\xi}\right)^\alpha\right]\\h_\text{OverlapSph}(r,\xi)   &= \begin{cases} \left(1+\frac{r}{4\xi}\right)\left(1-\frac{r}{2\xi}\right)^2 & \text{for } r\leq 2\xi\\0 & \text{for } r>2\xi \end{cases}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For the cut-off functions  and  the integral  can be solved analytically and the corresponding structure factors are given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_\text{Exp}(q,\xi,D,r_0) &= 1 +  \cfrac{D \Gamma(D-1) \sin\left([D-1]\arctan(q \xi)\right)}{\left( q r_0\right)^D \left[1+\tfrac{1}{q^2\xi^2}\right]^{(D-1)/2}}\\S_\text{Gauss}(q,\xi,D,r_0) &= 1 + \Gamma\left[\tfrac{D}{2}\right]\frac{D}{2} \left(\frac{\xi}{r_0}\right)^D {}_1F_1\left[\tfrac{D}{2},\tfrac{3}{2},-\tfrac{q^2\xi^2}{8}\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>D</math> is the fractal dimension, <math>\xi</math> is a cut-off length for the fractal correlations, <math>\Gamma(x)</math> is the gamma function. <math>{}_1F_1\left[\right]</math> is the Kummer or hypergeometric function. For the cut-off functions  and  the integral  is solved numerically. 
 FIGURE DELETED

==Other Structure Factors==

==Hayter-Penfold RMSA <ref name="Hayter1981"> J.B. Hayter and J. Penfold. An analytic structure factor for macroion solutions. ''Molecular Physics'', 42:109--118, 1981.

</ref>\cite{Hansen1982==

} This is the structure factor for a system of charged, spheroidal objects in a dielectric medium. When combined with an appropriate form factor (such as sphere, core+shell, ellipsoid etc.), this allows for inclusion of the interparticle interference effects due to screened coulomb repulsion between charged particles. The salt concentration, is used to compute the ionic strength of the solution which in turn is used to compute the Debye screening length. At present there is no provision for entering the ionic strength directly nor for use of any multivalent salts. The counterions are also assumed to be monovalent.\\\underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|Hayter Penfold RMSA}}:}\  
#[{{mono|RHS}}] hard sphere radius <math>R_{HS}</math> of particles in [nm]. 
#[{{mono|Z}}] charge of particle in units of the charge of an electron <math>e=ï¿½1.602 176 53 \times 10^{ï¿½19} \mathrm{C}</math> 
#[{{mono|eta}}] volume fraction <math>\eta</math> of particles 
#[{{mono|T}}] sample temperature <math>T</math> in Kelvin 
#[{{mono|salt}}] monovalent salt concentration in [M] 
#[{{mono|eps_r}}] dielectric constant <math>\epsilon_r</math> of solvent  

==MacroIon==

\begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} ETA &= \text{volume fraction}\\AK &= \kappa \sigma = \text{inv. screening length times diameter}\\\kappa &=\sqrt{ e^2/(\epsilon \epsilon_0 k_B T)*(\rho_c+2 \rho_s) }\ \rho_c &= \text{density of counterions} = \rho_\text{colloids} Z\\\rho_s &= \text{density of salt cations or anions}\\GEK &=\text{charge}^2/( \pi k_B T \epsilon \epsilon_0 \sigma (2+AK)^2)\ \text{charge} &= \text{Ladung eines Kolloids} = e Z\ S=ETA^{1./3} &= \text{scaling factor for rescaled MSA (RMSA)}\ GAMK&=2*S*GEK*\exp(AK-AK/S). \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} 

==Critical Scattering==

{{eqn|<math> S_\text{crit}(Q)=1+\frac{\kappa}{1+\zeta^2Q^2} </math>|dummy}}

<math>\zeta</math>: correlation length, <math>\kappa</math>: scaling factor

==Correlation Hole==

{{eqn|<math> S_\text{corr. hole}(Q,h,\eta)=1+\eta \Phi(Qh) </math>|dummy}}

<math>\Phi(x) = 3\frac{\sin(x)-x\cos(x)}{x^3}</math> <math>\eta</math>: volume fraction, <math>h</math>: hole radius

==Random Distribution Model==

{{eqn|<math> S_\text{RDM}(Q) = \frac{1}{1+8\frac{V_{ca}/V_p}\epsilon\Phi(x)} </math>|dummy}}

<math>x = 2QR_{ca}</math>\\<math>V_{ca} = \frac{4}{3}\pi R_{ca}^3</math>\\<math>V_p  = \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3/f_p</math>\\<math>\Phi(x) = 3\frac{\sin(x)-x\cos(x)}{x^3}</math>

==Local Order Model==

{{eqn|<math> S_\text{LOM}(Q) = 1+4\frac{\sin(QD)}{QD}-z\Phi(x); </math>|dummy}}

<math>x = \alpha Q D</math>\ <math>\Phi(x) = 3\frac{\sin(x)-x\cos(x)}{x^3}</math>

==Cylinder(PRISM)==

{{eqn|<math> S_\text{Cyl,PRISM} = \frac{1}{1+\nu C_q P_{15}} </math>|dummy}}

<math>x = 2QR</math> \\[3mm] <math>x_{P15} = Q(L-2R)</math> \\[3mm] <math>\displaystyle P_{15} = 2\frac{\text{Si}(x_{P15})}{x_{P15}} - 4\frac{\sin^2(x_{P15}/2)}{x_{P15}^2}</math> \\[3mm] <math>\displaystyle C_q = 3\frac{\sin(x)-x\cos(x)}{x^3}</math>

==Voigt Peak==

In spectroscopy, the Voigt profile is a spectral line profile named after Woldemar Voigt and found in all branches of spectroscopy in which a spectral line is broadened by two types of mechanisms, one of which alone would produce a Doppler profile, and the other of which would produce a Lorentzian profile. All normalized line profiles can be considered to be probability distributions. The Doppler profile is essentially a normal distribution and a Lorentzian profile is essentially a Cauchy distribution. Without loss of generality, we can consider only centered profiles which peak at zero. The Voigt profile is then the convolution of a Lorentzian profile and a Doppler profile:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} V(x\vert\sigma,\gamma)& = \int_\infty^\infty D(x'\vert\sigma) \, L(x-x'\vert\gamma)\, dx' \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>x</math> is frequency from line center, <math>D(x\vert\sigma)</math> is the centered Doppler profile:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} D(x\vert\sigma) & = \frac{e^{-x^2/2\sigma^2}}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and <math>L(x\vert\gamma)</math> is the centered Lorentzian profile:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} L(x\vert\gamma) & = \frac{\gamma}{\pi(x^2+\gamma^2)} . \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The defining integral can be evaluated as:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} V(x) & = \frac{\Re[w(z)]}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\Re[w(z)]</math> is the real part of the complex error function of <math>z</math> and

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} z = \frac{x+i\gamma}{\sigma\sqrt{2}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} S_{\text{Voigt}}(Q,Q_m,A,\sigma,\gamma,c_0) = A \,\, V(Q-Q_m\vert\sigma,\gamma)+c_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



\chapter{Size Distributions}

==Delta==

Choosing {{mono|Delta}} as a size distribution simply scales the form factor with a constant value <math>N</math>

 \underline{Input Parameters for size distribution {{mono|Delta}}:}  
#[{{mono|N}}] particle number density <math>N</math> 



==Uniform distribution==

 \\


 FIGURE DELETED

The uniform distribution defines equal probability over a given range for a continuous distribution. The support is defined by the two parameters, <math>x_\text{min}</math> and <math>x_\text{max}</math>, which are its minimum and maximum values.

\begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{Uniform}(x\vert N, x_\text{min},x_\text{max}) & = \begin{cases} \displaystyle \frac{N}{x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}} & \text{for} \quad x_\text{min}\leq x\leq x_\text{max},\\&\\\displaystyle 0               & \text{for} \quad x< x_\text{min} \text{ or } x>x_\text{max} \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations}

 \underline{Input Parameters for size distribution {{mono|Uniform}}:}  
#[{{mono|N}}] particle number density <math>N</math> 
#[{{mono|Xmin}}] minimum value of the distribution (<math>x_\text{min}</math>) 
#[{{mono|Xmax}}] maximum value of the distribution (<math>x_\text{max}</math>) 



==Triangular distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

\begin{subequations} \begin{multline} \text{Triangular}(x\vert x_\text{min},x_\text{max},x_\text{mode})   = \\\begin{cases} \displaystyle \frac{2(x-x_\text{min})}{(x_\text{max}-x_\text{min})(x_\text{mode}-x_\text{min})} & \text{for} \quad x_\text{min}<x\leq x_\text{mode}\\&\\\displaystyle \frac{2(x_\text{max}-x)}{(x_\text{max}-x_\text{min})(x_\text{max}-x_\text{mode})} & \text{for} \quad x_\text{mode}<x\leq x_\text{max} \end{cases} \end{multline} \end{subequations}



==Log-Normal distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

The {{mono|LogNorm}} distribution is a continuous distribuion in which the logarithm of a variable has a normal distribution. \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{LogNorm}(x,\mu,\sigma,p) &=  \frac{N}{c_\text{LN}} \frac{1}{x^{p}}\, \exp\!\!\left(-\frac{\ln(x/\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)\\c_\text{LN} &= \sqrt{2\pi}\,\sigma \,\mu^{1-p} \,\exp\!\!\left((1-p)^2\frac{\sigma^2}{2}\right)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} where <math>\sigma</math> is the width parameter, <math>p</math> a shape parameter, <math>\mu</math> is the location parameter. <math>c_\text{LN}</math> is choosen so that <math>\int_0^\infty\! \text{LogNorm}(x,\mu,\sigma,p)\,dR = N</math> The mode of the distribution is defined as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_\text{mode} = \mu e^{-p \sigma^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and the <math>n^\text{th}</math> moment <math>\langle X^n\rangle</math> of the {{mono|LogNorm}} distribution as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \langle X^n\rangle = \frac{\int X^n\, \textrm{LogNorm}(X)\, dX}{\int \textrm{LogNorm}(X)\, dX} = \mu^n \, e^{\frac{1}{2} \sigma^2 n (2 - 2 p + n)}.  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Schultz-Zimm distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math> \text{SZ}(R,N,R_a,k) =  \frac{N}{R_a} \left(\frac{R}{R_a}\right)^{k-1} \frac{k^k\exp(-kR/R_a)}{\Gamma(k)} </math>|dummy}}

\ <math>\text{SZ}(R,N,R_a,k)</math> is normalized so that <math>\int_0^\infty\! \text{SZ}(R,N,R_a,k)\,dR = N</math>. The parameter <math>k</math> of the size distribution is related to the variance <math>\sigma</math> by <math>k={1}/{\sigma^2}</math>. <math>R_a</math> is the scaling parameter and defines the maximum of the size distribution for large values of <math>k</math>.



==Gamma distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math> \text{gammaSD}(R,N,R_m,\sigma) =  \frac{N}{\theta} \left(\frac{R}{\theta}\right)^{k-1} \frac{\exp(-R/\theta)}{\Gamma(k)}  </math>|dummy}}

with <math>k=R_m^2/\sigma^2</math> and <math>\theta = \sigma^2/R_m</math> (<math>R_m=k\theta</math>, <math>\sigma^2=\theta^2 k</math>). \\<math>\text{gammaSD}(R,N,R_m,\sigma)</math> is normalized so that <math>\int_0^\infty\! \text{gammaSD}(R,N,R_m,\sigma)\,dR = N</math>. The parameter <math>k</math> of the size distribution is related to the variance <math>\sigma</math> by <math>k=R_m/\theta=R_m^2/\sigma</math>. <math>R_m</math> is the scaling parameter and defines the mean value of the size distribution.



==PearsonIII distribution==

The Pearson distribution is a family of probability distributions that are a generalisation of the normal distribution. The Pearson Type III distribution is given by the probability density function

{{eqn|<math> f(x) = \frac{1}{\beta\,\Gamma(p)} \left(\frac{x-\alpha}{\beta}\right)^{p-1} e^{-(x-\alpha)/\beta}, \! </math>|dummy}}

where <math>x \in [\alpha,\infty)</math> and <math>\alpha</math>, <math>\beta</math> and <math>p</math> are parameters of the distribution with <math>\beta > 0</math> and <math>p > 0</math> (Abramowitz and Stegun 1954, p. 930). Here, <math>\Gamma ()</math> denotes the Gamma function.  
# Mean:

{{eqn|<math>\alpha + p\beta</math>|dummy}}


# Variance:

{{eqn|<math>p\beta^2</math>|dummy}}


# Skewness:

{{eqn|<math>\frac{2}{\sqrt{p}}</math>|dummy}}


# Kurtosis:

{{eqn|<math>\frac{6}{p}</math>|dummy}}

The Pearson Type III distribution is identical to the Gamma distribution (). When <math>\alpha=0</math>, <math>\beta=2</math>, and <math>p</math> is half-integer, the Pearson Type III distribution becomes the <math>\chi^2</math> distribution of <math>2p</math> degrees of freedom.


==Gauss distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

\begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{Gauss}(R,\sigma,R_0)&= \frac{N}{c_\text{Gauss}} e^{-\frac{\left(R-R_0\right)^2}{2\sigma^2}}\\c_\text{Gauss} &=\sqrt{\frac{\pi}{2}}\sigma\left(1 + \text{erf}\left(\frac{R_0}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}\right) \right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} <math>c_\text{Gauss}</math> is choosen so that <math>\int_0^\infty\! \text{Gauss}(R,\sigma,R_0)\,dR = N</math>


==Generalized exponential distribution (GEX)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{GEX}(R,\beta,\lambda,\gamma))&= N \frac{\beta}{\gamma}\left(\frac{x}{\gamma}\right)^{\lambda+1} \frac{e^{-(x/\gamma)^{\beta}}}{\Gamma  \left( {\frac {\lambda+2}{\beta}} \right)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}


==Generalized extreme value distribution (GEV)==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math> \text{GEV}(R,\mu,\sigma,\xi) = \frac{N}{c_1} \frac{{e^{- \left( 1+{\frac {\xi\, \left( R-\mu \right) }{\sigma} } \right) ^{-1/{\xi}}}}}{ \sigma \left( 1+{\frac {\xi\, \left( R-\mu \right) }{\sigma}} \right) ^{1+ 1/{\xi}} } </math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math> c_1 = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{for <math>\xi>0</math>}\ 1-\exp\left(-\left(1-\frac{\xi\mu}{\sigma}\right)^{-\frac{1}{\xi}}\right) & \text{for <math>\xi<0</math>} \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

The shape parameter <math>\xi</math> governs the tail behaviour of the distribution, the sub-families defined by <math>\xi\to 0</math>, <math>\xi > 0</math> and <math>\xi < 0</math> correspond, respectively, to the Gumbel, Fr\'{e}chet and Weibull families, whose cumulative distribution functions are reminded below.  
# Gumbel or type I extreme value distribution

{{eqn|<math>F(x;\mu,\sigma)=e^{-e^{-(x-\mu)/\sigma}}\;\;\; for\;\; x\in\mathbb R</math>|dummy}}


# Fr\'{e}chet or type II extreme value distribution

{{eqn|<math>F(x;\mu,\sigma,\alpha)=\begin{cases} 0 & x\leq \mu\\e^{-((x-\mu)/\sigma)^{-\alpha}} & x>\mu \end{cases}</math>|dummy}}


# Weibull or type III extreme value distribution

{{eqn|<math>F(x;\mu,\sigma,\alpha)=\begin{cases} e^{-(-(x-\mu)/\sigma)^{-\alpha}} & x<\mu\\1 & x\geq \mu \end{cases}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\xi > 0</math> and <math>\xi > 0</math>

Remark I: For reliability issues the Weibull dist ribution is used with the variable <math>t = \mu - x</math>, the time, which is strictly positive. Thus the support is positive - in contrast to the use in extreme value theory.

Remark II: Be aware of an important distinctive feature of the three extreme value distributions: The support is either unlimited, or it has an upper or lower limit.   
# Parameters\ <math>\mu \in [-\infty,\infty]</math> \, location (real)\ <math>\sigma \in (0,\infty]</math> \, scale (real)\ <math>\xi\in [-\infty,\infty]</math>\, shape (real) 
# Support\ <math>x>\mu-\sigma/\xi\,\;(\xi > 0)</math>\\<math>x<\mu-\sigma/\xi\,\;(\xi < 0)</math>\\<math>x \in [-\infty,\infty]\,\;(\xi = 0)</math>  [1] \verb"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generalized_extreme_value_distribution"



==Maxwell distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{Maxwell}(R,R_0,\sigma) &= \begin{cases} \text{if <math>R \geq R_0</math>:} & N \frac{c}{c_\text{mw}} \left(R-R_0\right)^2 e^{-\left(R-R_0\right)^2/(2\sigma^2)}\ &\\\text{else:} & 0 \end{cases}\\& \\c &= \frac{4}{\sqrt{\pi}} \left(2\sigma^2\right)^{-3/2} \\& \\c_\text{mw} &= \begin{cases} \text{if <math>R_0 < 0</math>:} & 1-\frac{1}{\sigma} \sqrt{\frac{2}{\pi}} \frac{R_0}{\sqrt{\exp\left(R_0^2/\sigma^2\right)}} + \text{erf}\left(\frac{R_0}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}\right) \\&\\\text{else:} & 1 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Weibull distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{Weibull}(R,\alpha,\lambda,\mu) = \frac{N \lambda}{\alpha} \left(\frac{R-\mu}{\alpha}\right)^{\lambda-1} e^{-\left( \frac{R-\mu}{\alpha} \right)^\lambda} e^{-\left(\frac{\mu}{\alpha}\right)^\lambda} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\lambda</math> is the shape parameter, <math>\mu</math> is the location parameter and <math>\alpha</math> is the scale parameter.



==fractal size distribution==


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math> \text{fractalSD}(R,N,R_\text{min},R_\text{max},f_D) = \frac{N f_D }{R_\text{min}^{-f_D}-R_\text{max}^{-f_D}} \;\; R^{-(1+f_D)} </math>|dummy}}

 \chapter{Peak functions}



==Beta==

  The Beta distribution is a very versatile function which can be used to model several different shapes of probability density curves. In probability theory and statistics, the beta distribution is a family of continuous probability distributions defined on the interval <math>[0, 1]</math> parameterized by two positive shape parameters, typically denoted by <math>\alpha</math> and <math>\beta</math>.

{{eqn|<math> p_\mathrm{Beta}(x;\alpha,\beta) = \begin{cases} \frac{1}{\mathrm{B}(\alpha,\beta)}\, x ^{\alpha-1}(1-x)^{\beta-1} & \mbox{ for } 0<x<1 \\0   & \mbox{ otherwise } \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

The beta function, B, appears as a normalization constant to ensure that the total probability integrates to unity. <math>\alpha</math> and <math>\beta</math> are positive numbers that define the shape parameters. The mode of the beta distribution for shape parameters <math>\alpha>1</math> and <math>\beta>1</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math> \text{mode}\left(p_\mathrm{Beta}\right) = \frac{\alpha-1}{\alpha+\beta-2} </math>|dummy}}



==Beta (Amplitude)==



{{eqn|<math> y_\mathrm{Beta (ampl)}\left(x;A,x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max},\alpha,\beta,c_0\right) = A\, \frac{p_\mathrm{Beta}\left(\frac{x-x_\mathrm{min}}{x_\mathrm{max}-x_\mathrm{min}};\alpha,\beta\right)}{p_\mathrm{Beta}\left(\frac{\alpha-1}{\alpha+\beta-2};\alpha,\beta\right)} +c_0 </math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[ampl.] amplitude <math>A</math> of the Beta peak 
#[xmin] continuous lower boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{min}</math> 
#[xmax] continuous upper boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{max}</math> 
#[alpha] first shape parameter <math>\alpha>1</math> 
#[beta]  second shape parameter <math>\beta>1</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Both shape parameter needs to be larger than one <math>(\alpha,\beta>1)</math>, as only than the distribution has a peak shape. 
# where the Beta distribution is not defined the offset value is returned:\\<math>\forall x\notin (x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max})\quad y_\mathrm{Beta (ampl)}(x) = c_0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Beta (Area)==



{{eqn|<math> y_\mathrm{Beta (area)}\left(x;A,x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max},\alpha,\beta,c_0\right) = A\, \frac{p_\mathrm{Beta}\left(\frac{x-x_\mathrm{min}}{x_\mathrm{max}-x_\mathrm{min}};\alpha,\beta\right)}{x_\mathrm{max}-x_\mathrm{min}} +c_0 </math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the beta distribution 
#[xmin] continuous lower boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{min}</math> 
#[xmax] continuous upper boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{max}</math> 
#[alpha] first shape parameter <math>\alpha>0</math> 
#[beta]  second shape parameter <math>\beta>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Both shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>(\alpha,\beta>0)</math> 
# where the Beta distribution is not defined the offset value is returned:\\<math>\forall x\notin (x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max})\quad y_\mathrm{Beta (area)}(x) = c_0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse



==Chi-Squared==

\\ \footnote{\href{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi-square_distribution}{Description taken partly from Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia}}In probability theory and statistics, the chi-square distribution (also chi-squared or <math>\xi^2</math>  distribution) is one of the most widely used theoretical probability distributions in inferential statistics, e.g., in statistical significance tests. It is useful because, under reasonable assumptions, easily calculated quantities can be proven to have distributions that approximate to the chi-square distribution if the null hypothesis is true.

The best-known situations in which the chi-square distribution are used are the common chi-square tests for goodness of fit of an observed distribution to a theoretical one, and of the independence of two criteria of classification of qualitative data. Many other statistical tests also lead to a use of this distribution, like Friedman's analysis of variance by ranks.

A probability density function of the chi-square distribution is

{{eqn|<math> f(x;k)= \begin{cases}\displaystyle \frac{1}{2^{k/2}\Gamma(k/2)}\,x^{(k/2) - 1} e^{-x/2}&\text{for }x>0\ 0&\text{for }x\le 0 \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\Gamma</math> denotes the Gamma function, which has closed-form values at the half-integers. The mode of the distribution is

{{eqn|<math> \mathrm{mode} = k-2\, \mbox{ if }\, k\geq 2. </math>|dummy}}

The <math>\chi^2</math> distribution is a special case of the gamma distribution  where <math>\theta = 2</math> in the equation .

==Chi-Squared (Amplitude)==

 \

{{eqn|<math> \chi^2(x;A,x_c,\sigma,k,c_0) = \begin{cases} c_0+A_0 \left(z+u\right)^{v} \exp\left(-\frac{z+u}{2}\right) & \mbox{ for } z+u \geq 0\\c_0 & \mbox{ otherwise} \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} z &= \frac{x-x_x}{\sigma}\\u &= k-2\\v &= \frac{k}{2}-1\\A_0 &= \frac{A\exp(v)}{u^v} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The standard statistical form has been reparameterized. The parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, and <math>\sigma</math> to enable scaling. The mode is <math>x_c</math>. The function returns 0 for those x where it is undefined <math>(z+u<0)</math>.



'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>a</math> of the Gamma peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>k>2</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>(\sigma>0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter needs to be larger than two <math>(k>2)</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED

==Chi-Squared (Area)==

\

{{eqn|<math> \chi^2(x;A,x_c,\sigma,k,c_0) = \begin{cases} c_0+A_0 \left(z+u\right)^{v} \exp\left(-\frac{z+u}{2}\right) & \mbox{ for } z+u \geq 0\\c_0 & \mbox{ otherwise} \end{cases} </math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} z &= \frac{x-x_x}{\sigma}\\u &= k-2\\v &= \frac{k}{2}-1\\A_0 &= \frac{A}{ 2^{\frac{k}{2}} \sigma\Gamma\left(\frac{k}{2}\right)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The standard statistical form has been reparameterized. The parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, and <math>\sigma</math> to enable scaling. The mode is <math>x_c</math>. The function returns 0 for those x where it is undefined <math>(z+u<0)</math>.

==Error peak==

==Error (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math> y(x;a,x_c,\sigma,k,c_0) = a \, \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\frac{\left|x-x_c\right|^{\frac{2}{k}}}{\left|\sigma\right|}\right)+c_0 </math>|dummy}}

==Error (Area)==

==Exponentially Modified Gaussian (Amplitude)==

\\ \begin{eqnarray} y(x;a,x_c,\sigma,\gamma,c_0) & = & \frac{a}{\mathrm{const}} \exp\left(\frac{\sigma^2}{2\gamma^2}+\frac{x_c-x}{\gamma}\right) \\& & \left[\mathrm{erf}\left(\frac{x-x_c}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}-\frac{\sigma}{\sqrt{2}\gamma}\right)+\frac{\gamma}{| \gamma |}\right] +c_0 \end{eqnarray} <math>\mbox{const}</math> is calculated numerically so that "<math>a</math>" represents the amplitude of the distribution. 

'''Required parameters:'''

==F-Variance==

In probability theory and statistics, the F-distribution is a continuous probability distribution. It is also known as Snedecor's F distribution or the Fisher-Snedecor distribution. The probability density is given by

{{eqn|<math> p_\mathrm{F-var}(x,\nu_1,\nu_2) = \frac{\displaystyle \sqrt{\frac{\left(\nu_1 x\right)^{\nu_1} \nu_2^{\nu_2}}{\left(\nu_1 x+\nu_2\right)^{\nu_1+\nu_2}}}}{x\,\mathrm{B}\left(\frac{\nu_1}{2},\frac{\nu_2}{2}\right)} </math>|dummy}}

for real <math>x\geq 0</math>, where <math>\nu>1</math> and <math>\nu_2</math> are positive, and <math>\mathrm{B}()</math>  is the beta function. For <math>\nu_1>2</math> the mode of the distribution is defined by

{{eqn|<math> \mathrm{mode}_F(\nu_1,\nu_2)=\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_2+2} </math>|dummy}}



==F-Variance (Amplitude)==

The amplitude version represents a re-parametrization of the standard statistical form.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x;a,x_c,\sigma,\nu_1,\nu_2) &= \begin{cases} c_0+a\,\frac{p_\mathrm{F-var}(z,\nu_1,\nu_2)}{p_\mathrm{F-var}(\mathrm{mode}_F(\nu_1,\nu_2),\nu_1,\nu_2)}  & \mbox{ for } z>0\ c_0 & \mbox{ otherwise} \end{cases}\\&= \begin{cases} c_0+a\,\frac{z^{\frac{\nu_1}{2}-1}\left(1+\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_2+2}\right)^{\frac{\nu_1+\nu2}{2}}}{\left(1+\frac{\nu1}{\nu2}z\right)^{\frac{\nu_1+\nu_2}{2}}\left(\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_2+2}\right)^{\frac{\nu_1}{2}-1}}   & \mbox{ for } z>0\ c_0 & \mbox{ otherwise} \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math> z = \frac{x-x_c}{\sigma}+\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_2+2} </math>|dummy}}

The location parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, and <math>\sigma</math> to enable scaling. The mode of the distribution function is <math>x_c</math> due to the additional term <math>\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_2+2}</math> in the definition of <math>z</math>. The distribution function returns the offset <math>c_0</math> for values <math>z\leq 0</math>.



'''Required parameters:'''  
#[ampl.] amplitude <math>a</math> of the F-distribution 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[shape1] shape parameter <math>\nu_1>2</math> 
#[shape2] shape parameter <math>\nu_2>2</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scale parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma>0</math> 
# The first shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\nu_1>2</math> 
# The second shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\nu_2>2</math> 
# Default (Size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==F-Variance (Area)==

The area version represents a re-parametrization of the standard statistical form.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x;a,x_c,\sigma,\nu_1,\nu_2) &= \begin{cases} c_0+\frac{a}{\sigma}\,p_\mathrm{F-var}(z,\nu_1,\nu_2) & \mbox{ for } z>0\ c_0 & \mbox{ otherwise} \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with

{{eqn|<math> z = \frac{x-x_c}{\sigma}+\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_2+2} </math>|dummy}}

The location parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, and <math>\sigma</math> to enable scaling. The mode of the distribution function is <math>x_c</math> due to the additional term <math>\frac{\nu_1-2}{\nu_1}\frac{\nu_2}{\nu_2+2}</math> in the definition of <math>z</math>. The distribution function returns the offset <math>c_0</math> for values <math>z\leq 0</math>.

 '''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>a</math> of the F-distribution 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[shape1] shape parameter <math>\nu_1>2</math> 
#[shape2] shape parameter <math>\nu_2>2</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scale parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma>0</math> 
# The first shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\nu_1>2</math> 
# The second shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\nu_2>2</math> 
# Default (Size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Gamma==

The gamma distribution models sums of exponentially distributed random variables.

The gamma distribution family is based on two parameters. The chi-square and exponential distributions, which are children of the gamma distribution, are one-parameter distributions that fix one of the two gamma parameters. The standard form is given by

{{eqn|<math>  p(x;k,\theta) = x^{k-1} \frac{e^{-x/\theta}}{\theta^k \, \Gamma(k)}  \mathrm{ for } x > 0\,\, \mathrm{ and }\,\, k, \theta > 0. </math>|dummy}}

When <math>k</math> is large, the gamma distribution closely approximates a normal distribution with the advantage that the gamma distribution has density only for positive real numbers. In probability theory and statistics, the gamma distribution is a two-parameter family of continuous probability distributions. It has a scale parameter <math>\theta</math> and a shape parameter <math>k</math>. If <math>k</math> is an integer then the distribution represents the sum of <math>k</math> independent exponentially distributed random variables, each of which has a mean of <math>\theta</math> (which is equivalent to a rate parameter of <math>\theta^{-1}</math>).

Alternatively, the gamma distribution can be parameterized in terms of a shape parameter <math>\alpha = k</math> and an inverse scale parameter <math>\beta = 1/\theta</math>, called a rate parameter:

{{eqn|<math> p(x;\alpha,\beta) = x^{\alpha-1} \frac{\beta^{\alpha} \, e^{-\beta\,x} }{\Gamma(\alpha)}  \mathrm{for} x > 0 \,\!. </math>|dummy}}



==Gamma (Amplitude)==

\  The parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, whereas the <math>+\theta(k-1)</math> adjusts <math>x_c</math> so that it represents the mode. <math>c_0</math> is the offset value. The function returns the offset <math>c_0</math> for those <math>x</math> where it is undefined

{{eqn|<math> y(x) = \Bigg\{ \begin{array}{ll} c_0 + a \exp(-z) \left(\frac{z+k-1}{k-1}\right)^{k-1} & \mbox{for } (z+k-1) > 0\\c_0 &  \mbox{otherwise} \end{array} </math>|dummy}}

with <math> z=\frac{x-x_c}{\theta} </math>

\\

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[ampl.] amplitude <math>a</math> of the Gamma peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\theta>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The shape parameter needs to be larger than one <math>k>1</math>. 
# The scale parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\theta>0</math> 
# Default (Size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Gamma (Area)==

The parameter <math>x_c</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning, whereas the <math>+\theta(k-1)</math> adjusts <math>x_c</math> so that it represents the mode. <math>c_0</math> is the offset value. The function returns the offset <math>c_0</math> for those <math>x</math> where it is undefined

{{eqn|<math> y(x) = \Bigg\{ \begin{array}{ll} c_0 + \frac{a}{\theta\Gamma(k)} \exp(-z) z^{k-1} & \mbox{for } z > 0\\c_0 &  \mbox{otherwise} \end{array} </math>|dummy}}

with <math>z=\frac{x-x_c}{\theta} +k-1</math>

==Gaussian-Lorentzian cross product==

This distribution function is a Voigt approximation. It combines a Gaussian and Lorentzian in a multiplicative form. The shape parameter <math>\nu</math> varies from 0 to 1. The pure Lorentzian occurs with <math>\nu=1</math> and the pure Gaussian with <math>\nu=0</math> but the transition from Lorentzian to Gaussian shape in not a linear function of <math>\nu</math>

 

==Gaussian-Lorentzian cross product (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math> y(x,a,x_c,\sigma,\nu,c_0) = a\, \frac{\exp\left(-\frac{1-\nu}{2}\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2\right)}{1+\nu\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2}+c_0 </math>|dummy}}



'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>a</math> of the peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\nu\in [0,1]</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be non-zero <math>(\sigma\neq 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter need to be between 0 and 1 (<math>\nu\in [0,1]</math>) 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse  
 FIGURE DELETED



==Gaussian-Lorentzian cross product (Area)==

{{eqn|<math> y(x,a,x_c,\sigma,\nu,c_0) = a\, \frac{\sqrt{\nu}}{|\sigma|\pi}\frac{\exp\left(-\frac{1-\nu}{2\nu}\right)}{\mbox{erfc}\left(\sqrt{\frac{1-\nu}{2\nu}}\right)} \frac{\exp\left(-\frac{1-\nu}{2}\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2\right)}{1+\nu\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2} +c_0 </math>|dummy}}



'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>a</math> below the peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\nu\in [0,1]</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be non-zero <math>(\sigma\neq 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter need to be between 0 and 1 (<math>\nu\in [0,1]</math>) 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse  
 FIGURE DELETED 

==Gaussian-Lorentzian sum==

This distribution function is another Voigt approximation, which is simply a sum of Lorentzian and Gaussian with equal FWHM. The shape parameter <math>\nu</math> varies from 0 to 1. The pure Lorentzian occurs with <math>\nu=1</math> and the pure Gaussian with <math>\nu=0</math>. The width parameter <math>\sigma</math> directly computes the full-width at half-maximum (FWHM).

 

==Gaussian-Lorentzian sum (Amplitude)==

\begin{multline} y(x,a,x_c,\sigma,\nu,c_0) =\\a\,\frac{\frac{\nu}{|\sigma|}\sqrt{\frac{\ln 2}{\pi}}\exp\left(-4\ln 2\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2\right)+\frac{1-\nu}{\pi|\sigma|\left[1+4\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2\right]}}{\frac{\nu}{|\sigma|}\sqrt{\frac{\ln 2}{\pi}}+\frac{1-\nu}{\pi|\sigma|}} + c_0 \end{multline}



'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>a</math> of the peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\nu\in [0,1]</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be non-zero <math>(\sigma\neq 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter need to be between 0 and 1 (<math>\nu\in [0,1]</math>) 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse  
 FIGURE DELETED



==Gaussian-Lorentzian sum (Area)==

\begin{multline} y(x,a,x_c,\sigma,\nu,c_0) =\\2a\,\left[\frac{\nu}{|\sigma|}\sqrt{\frac{\ln 2}{\pi}}\exp\left({ -4\ln 2\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2}\right)+\frac{1-\nu}{\pi|\sigma|\left[1+4\left(\frac{x-x_c}{|\sigma|}\right)^2\right]}\right]+c_0 \end{multline} 

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>a</math> below the peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\nu\in [0,1]</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be non-zero <math>(\sigma\neq 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter need to be between 0 and 1 (<math>\nu\in [0,1]</math>) 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

==generalized Gaussian 1==

The generalized Gaussian distribution is one of two families of continuous probability distributions, which has an additional shape parameter to the normal distribution. Known also as the exponential power distribution, or the generalized error distribution, this is a parametric family of symmetric distributions. It includes all normal and Laplace distributions, and as limiting cases it includes all continuous uniform distributions on bounded intervals of the real line.

This family includes the normal distribution when <math>\beta=2</math> (with mean <math>\mu</math> and variance <math>\frac{\alpha^2}{2}</math>) and it includes the Laplace distribution when <math>\beta=1</math>. As <math>\beta\rightarrow\infty</math>, the density converges pointwise to a uniform density on <math> (\mu-\alpha,\mu+\alpha)</math>.



==generalized Gaussian 1 (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x,a,\mu,\alpha,\beta) = a e^{-{\left\vert \right\vert}{\frac{x-\mu}{\alpha}}^\beta} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>a</math> of the peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>\mu</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\alpha \neq 0</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\beta</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The width parameter needs to be non-zero <math>(\alpha\neq 0)</math>. 
# The area parameter needs to be positive <math>(\beta> 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>a</math> below the peak

==Giddings==

The Giddings equation was derived by J. C. Giddings (Dynamics of Chromatography, Part I, Marcel Decker, New York, 1965). The equation provides a theoretical description for chromatographic peaks. The used formulae have been taken from the Manual of the Peakfit Software package (SeaSolve Software Inc.), which contains an expression for the for the {{mono|Giddings (Area)}}. As the mode <math>x_\text{mode}</math> of the peak can not be calculated analytical the amplitude version of this peaks {{mono|Giddings (Amplitude)}} calculates first numerically the mode of the peak and than normalizes the value at <math>x_\text{mode}</math> to be <math>y(x_\text{mode})=A</math>.



==Giddings (Amplitude)==

As the mode <math>x_\text{mode}</math> of this peak can not be calculated analytical this version of the Giddings peak calculates first numerically the mode of the peak and than normalizes the value at <math>x_\text{mode}</math> to be <math>y(x_\text{mode})=A</math>.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & = \frac{A}{c} \sqrt{\frac{\beta}{x}} \, \mathrm{I}_1\left(\frac{2\sqrt{\beta x}}{\sigma}\right) \exp\left(-\frac{x+\beta}{\sigma}\right)\\\text{with } & \\c & = \sqrt{\frac{\beta}{x_\text{mode}}} \, \mathrm{I}_1\left(\frac{2\sqrt{\beta x_\text{mode}}}{\sigma}\right) \exp\left(-\frac{x_\text{mode}+\beta}{\sigma}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\beta</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\sigma > 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

 FIGURE DELETED


==Giddings (Area)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & =  \frac{A}{1-\exp\left(-\frac{\beta}{\sigma}\right)} \, \frac{1}{\sigma} \sqrt{\frac{\beta}{x}} \, \mathrm{I}_1\left(\frac{2\sqrt{\beta x}}{\sigma}\right) \exp\left(-\frac{x+\beta}{\sigma}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> below the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\beta</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math>

# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\sigma > 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

 FIGURE DELETED


==Haarhoff - Van der Linde (Area)==

'''Required parameters:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & = \frac{ \frac{A\sigma}{\mu\delta\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left[-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{x-\mu}{\sigma}\right)^2\right] }{ \exp\left(1-\frac{\mu\delta}{\sigma^2}\right) + \frac{1}{2}\left[1+\mathrm{erf}\left(\frac{x-\mu}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}\right)\right] } \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> below the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\mu > 0</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
#[delta] distortion parameter <math>\delta \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The location parameter needs to be positive <math>(\mu > 0)</math>. 
# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\sigma > 0)</math>. 
# The distortion parameter needs to be nonzero <math>(\delta \neq 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Half Gaussian Modified Gaussian (Area)==

The {{mono|Half Gaussian Modified Gaussian (Area)}} is the mathematical convolution of a Gaussian with a half-Gaussian response function. There are only two components to this model, a primary Gaussian, and a response function which convolves or smears the Gaussian as in the profiles above. As the width of the half-Gaussian response increases, peaks become more asymmetric or tailed. This function directly fit both tailed and fronted peaks. The transition from tailed to smooth is continuous and occurs at <math>\delta=0</math>. The formula has been taken from the Manual of the Peakfit Software package (SeaSolve Software Inc.).

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= A \frac{ \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2} \frac{\left(x-\mu\right)^2}{\sigma^2+\delta^2}\right) \left[1+\mathrm{erf}\left(\frac{\delta \left(x-\mu\right)}{\sqrt{2}\sigma\sqrt{\sigma^2+\delta^2}}\right)\right] }{ \sqrt{2\pi}\sqrt{\sigma^2+\delta^2}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> below the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
#[distortion] distortion parameter <math>\delta \neq 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\sigma > 0)</math>. 
# The distortion parameter needs to be nonzero <math>(\delta \neq 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Inverted Gamma==

The inverse gamma distribution is a two-parameter family of continuous probability distributions on the positive real line, which is the distribution of the reciprocal of a variable distributed according to the gamma distribution. The inverse gamma distribution's probability density function is defined over the support <math>x > 0</math>. The probability density function is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x) &= \frac{\beta^\alpha}{\Gamma(\alpha)} x^{-\alpha - 1} \exp \left(\frac{-\beta}{x}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

the mode <math>x_\text{mode}</math> of the probabilty function is given by <math>x_\text{mode} = \frac{\beta}{\alpha+1}</math>. The shape parameter <math>\alpha</math> needs to be positive and non-zero as well as the scale parameter <math>\beta</math> (<math>\alpha>0</math>, <math>\beta>0</math>). The {{sasfit}} version represents a reparametrization of the standard statistical form. The parameter <math>\mu</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning. Adjustment terms have been added so that <math>\mu</math> is the mode <math>x_\text{mode}</math>. The function returns <math>c_0</math> for those <math>x</math> where it is undefined. Note that the amplitude form is much faster.



==Inverted Gamma (Amplitude)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= A\frac{\beta\exp\left(\frac{(x-\mu)(\alpha+1)^2}{x(\alpha+1)+\beta-\mu(\alpha+1)}\right)\left(\frac{x(\alpha+1)-\mu(\alpha+1)}{\beta}+1\right)^{-\alpha}}{x(\alpha+1)+\beta-\mu(\alpha+1)} +c_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\beta > 0</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\alpha > 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\beta > 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter needs to be positive <math>(\alpha > 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Inverted Gamma (Area)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= A\frac{(\alpha+1)\exp\left(\frac{\beta(\alpha+1)}{x(\alpha+1)+\beta-\mu(\alpha+1)}\right)\left(\frac{\beta(\alpha+1)}{x(\alpha+1)+\beta-\mu(\alpha+1)}\right)^{\alpha}}{\left(x(\alpha+1)+\beta-\mu(\alpha+1)\right)\Gamma\left(\alpha\right)} +c_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> below the peak 
#[location] location parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[width] scaling parameter <math>\beta > 0</math> 
#[shape] shape parameter <math>\alpha > 0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The scaling parameter needs to be positive <math>(\beta > 0)</math>. 
# The shape parameter needs to be positive <math>(\alpha > 0)</math>. 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Kumaraswamy==

The Kumaraswamy's double bounded distribution is a family of continuous probability distributions defined on the interval <math>[0,1]</math> differing in the values of their two non-negative shape parameters, <math>a</math> and <math>b</math>. It is similar to the Beta distribution, but much simpler to use especially in simulation studies due to the simple closed form of both its probability density function and cumulative distribution function. The probability density function of the Kumaraswamy distribution is

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x; \alpha,\beta) &= \alpha \beta x^{\alpha-1}{ (1-x^\alpha)}^{\beta-1}. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For <math>\alpha>1</math> and <math>\beta>1</math> the mode of the distribution reads as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_\text{mode} &= \left(\frac{\alpha-1}{\alpha\beta-1}\right)^{1/\alpha} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Kumaraswamy (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & = \begin{cases} \frac{A\alpha \beta \left(\frac{x+x_\text{min}}{x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}}\right)^{\alpha-1}{ \left(1-\left(\frac{x+x_\text{min}}{x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}}\right)^\alpha\right)}^{\beta-1}}{\alpha \beta x_\text{mode}^{\alpha-1}{ (1-x_\text{mode}^\alpha)}^{\beta-1}} +c_0 & \mbox{for } x \in [x_\text{min},x_\text{max}]\\c_0 & \mbox{for } x \notin [x_\text{min},x_\text{max}] \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[ampl.] amplitude <math>A</math> of the Kumaraswamy peak 
#[xmin] continuous lower boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{min}</math> 
#[xmax] continuous upper boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{max}</math> 
#[alpha] first shape parameter <math>\alpha>1</math> 
#[beta]  second shape parameter <math>\beta>1</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Both shape parameter needs to be larger than one <math>(\alpha,\beta>1)</math>, as only than the distribution has a peak shape. 
# where the Kumaraswamy distribution is not defined the offset value is returned:\\<math>\forall x\notin (x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max})\quad y_\mathrm{Beta (ampl)}(x) = c_0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse  
 FIGURE DELETED 

==Kumaraswamy (Area)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & = \begin{cases} \frac{A\alpha \beta \left(\frac{x+x_\text{min}}{x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}}\right)^{\alpha-1}{ \left(1-\left(\frac{x+x_\text{min}}{x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}}\right)^\alpha\right)}^{\beta-1}} {x_\text{max}-x_\text{min}} +c_0 & \mbox{for } x \in [x_\text{min},x_\text{max}]\\c_0 & \mbox{for } x \notin [x_\text{min},x_\text{max}] \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the Kumaraswamy distribution 
#[xmin] continuous lower boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{min}</math> 
#[xmax] continuous upper boundary parameters <math>x_\mathrm{max}</math> 
#[alpha] first shape parameter <math>\alpha>0</math> 
#[beta]  second shape parameter <math>\beta>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Both shape parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>(\alpha,\beta>0)</math> 
# where the Kumaraswamy distribution is not defined the offset value is returned:\\<math>\forall x\notin (x_\mathrm{min},x_\mathrm{max})\quad y_\mathrm{Beta (area)}(x) = c_0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

==Laplace==

A random variable has a Laplace distribution if its probability density function is

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x;x_0,\sigma) &= \frac{1}{2\sigma} \exp \left( -\frac{{\left\vert x-x_0\right\vert}}{\sigma}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Here, <math>x_0</math> is a location parameter and <math>\sigma > 0</math> is a scale parameter. The Laplace distribution is also sometimes called the double exponential distribution, because it can be thought of as two exponential distributions (with an additional location parameter) spliced together back-to-back, but the term double exponential distribution is also sometimes used to refer to the Gumbel distribution.

==Laplace (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x;x_0,\sigma) &= A \exp \left( -\frac{{\left\vert x-x_0\right\vert}}{\sigma}\right) + c_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the Laplace distribution 
#[center] peak center (mode) <math>x_0</math> of the Laplace distribution 
#[width] width parameter <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>(\sigma>0)</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED


==Laplace (Area)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x;x_0,\sigma) &= \frac{A}{2\sigma} \exp \left( -\frac{{\left\vert x-x_0\right\vert}}{\sigma}\right) + c_0 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the Laplace distribution 
#[center] peak center (mode) <math>x_0</math> of the Laplace distribution 
#[width] width parameter <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# Width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>(\sigma>0)</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

==Logistic==

The logistic distribution is a continuous probability distribution. It resembles the normal distribution in shape but has heavier tails (higher kurtosis). The probability density function (pdf) of the logistic  distribution is given by:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x; x_0,\sigma) &= \frac{\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)} {\sigma\left(1+\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)\right)^2} =\frac{1}{4\,\sigma} \;\operatorname{sech}^2\!\left(\frac{x-x_0}{2\,\sigma}\right). \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Because the pdf can be expressed in terms of the square of the hyperbolic secant function <math>\operatorname{sech}</math>, it is sometimes referred to as the sech-squared distribution. The mode, mean and median values are <math>x_0</math>. \vspace{1cm}

==Logistic (Amplitude)==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x; x_0,\sigma) &= 4A\frac{\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)} {\left(1+\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)\right)^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the Logistic distribution 
#[x0] location parameter (mode) <math>x_0</math> 
#[sigma] width parameters <math>\sigma</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# the width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED


==Logistic (Area)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x; x_0,\sigma) &= A\; \frac{\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)} {\sigma\left(1+\exp\left(-\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)\right)^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the Logistic distribution 
#[x0] location parameter (mode) <math>x_0</math> 
#[sigma] width parameters <math>\sigma</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# the width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

==LogLogistic==

As may be indicated by the name, the loglogistic (known as the Fisk distribution in economics) distribution has certain similarities to the logistic distribution. A random variable is loglogistically distributed if the logarithm of the random variable is logistically distributed. The {{mono|LogLogistic}} distribution is a two-parameter distribution with parameters <math>\sigma</math> and <math>x_0</math>. It is similar in shape to the log-normal distribution but has heavier tails.

The pdf for this distribution is given by:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x;\mu,\sigma) &=\frac{\exp\left(-\frac{\log\left(x\right)-\log(\mu)}{\sigma}\right)}{\sigma \left(x\right)\left(1+\exp\left(-\frac{\log(x)-\log(\mu)}{\sigma}\right)\right)^2} = \frac{ \left(\frac{x}{\mu}\right)^{-1/\sigma} } { x\sigma\left[ 1+\left(\frac{x}{\mu}\right)^{-1/\sigma} \right]^2 }. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>0 < x < \infty</math>, <math>-\infty < x_0 < \infty</math> and <math>0 < \sigma < \infty</math>. The mode of the {{mono|LogLogistic}} distribution, if <math>\sigma < 1</math>, is given by:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathrm{mode} &= \mu\left(\frac{1-\sigma}{1+\sigma}\right)^{\sigma} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==LogLogistic (Amplitude)==


{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= \begin{cases} A \frac{ \left(\frac{x-x_0}{\mu}\right)^{-1/\sigma} } { \left(x-x_0\right)\sigma\left[ 1+\left(\frac{x-x_0}{\mu}\right)^{-1/\sigma} \right]^2 } +c_0 & \mbox{for } x \geq x_0\ c_0 & \mbox{for } x<x_0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the LogLogistic distribution 
#[x0] location parameter <math>x_0</math> 
#[mu] scale parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[sigma] shape parameters <math>\sigma</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# the width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>0<\sigma <1</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED


==LogLogistic (Area)==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= A \frac{ \left(\frac{x}{x_0}\right)^{-1/\sigma} } { x\sigma\left[ 1+\left(\frac{x}{x_0}\right)^{-1/\sigma} \right]^2 }. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the LogLogistic distribution 
#[x0] location parameter <math>x_0</math> 
#[mu] scale parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[sigma] shape parameters <math>0 < \sigma < 1</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# the width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) & = \begin{cases} c_0+A \exp\left[ - \frac{\ln(2)\ln\left(\frac{(x-x_0)(\gamma^2-1)}{\sigma\gamma}+1\right)^2}{\ln(\gamma)}\right] & \mbox{for } \gamma \neq 1, \gamma > 0\\c_0+A 2^{-4\left(\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)^2} & \mbox{for } \gamma = 1 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

For <math>\left(x \geq x_0-\frac{\sigma\gamma}{\gamma^2-1} \wedge \gamma < 1\right) \vee \left(x \leq x_0-\frac{\sigma\gamma}{\gamma^2-1} \wedge \gamma > 1\right)</math> the function returns <math>c_0</math>. \vspace{2mm}
