==LogNormal==

The LogNormal distribution is defined with reference to the normal distribution. A random variable is Lognormally distributed if the logarithm of the random variable is normally distributed.

The LogNormal distribution is commonly used for general reliability analysis, cycles-to-failure in fatigue, material strengths and loading variables in probabilistic design. Another advantage of the LogNormal distribution is that it is positive-definite, so it is often useful for representing quantities that cannot have negative values. LogNormal distributions have proven useful as distributions for rainfall amounts, for the size distributions of aerosol particles or droplets, and for many other cases. The log-normal distribution has the probability density function

{{eqn|<math> f(x') = \frac{1}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{x'-\mu'}{\sigma}\right)^2\right) </math>|dummy}}

where <math>\mu'= \ln(\mu)</math> and  <math>x'= \ln(x)</math>. The lognormal pdf can be obtained, realizing that for equal probabilities under the normal and lognormal pdfs, incremental areas should also be equal, or:

{{eqn|<math>    f(x;\mu,\sigma)dx =f(x';\mu,\sigma)dx' </math>|dummy}}

Taking the derivative yields:

{{eqn|<math>    dx' =\frac{dx}{x} </math>|dummy}}

Substitution yields:

{{eqn|<math>    f(x;\mu,\sigma) =\frac{f(x';\mu,\sigma)}{x} </math>|dummy}}

where:

{{eqn|<math> f(x;\mu,\sigma) = \frac{1}{x\sigma \sqrt{2 \pi}}\exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{\ln (x) - \ln(\mu)}{\sigma}\right)^2\right) </math>|dummy}}

for <math>x\in (0,\infty]</math>, where  <math>\mu>0</math> and <math>\sigma\neq 0</math> are the location and scale parameter. The mode of the distribution is

{{eqn|<math> \mathrm{mode} =   \mu\exp\left(-\sigma^2\right) </math>|dummy}}



==LogNormal (Amplitude)==

\

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= \begin{cases} \frac{A \exp\left(-\frac12\sigma^2\right) \mu}{x-x_0}\exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{\ln (x-x_0) - \ln(\mu)}{\sigma}\right)^2\right) + c_0 & \mbox{for } x>x_0\\c_0 & \mbox{for } x \leq x_0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[amplitude] amplitude <math>A</math> of the LogNormal distribution 
#[mu] location parameter <math>\mu</math> 
#[sigma] width parameter <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
#[x0] shift parameters <math>x_0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# the width parameter needs to be larger than zero <math>\sigma > 0</math> 
# the location parameter needs to be larger than the shift parameter <math>\mu > x_0</math> 
# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==LogNormal (Area)==

\

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} y(x) &= \begin{cases} \frac{A}{(x-x_0)\sigma \sqrt{2 \pi}} \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{\ln (x-x_0) - \ln(\mu)}{\sigma}\right)^2\right) + c0 & \mbox{for } x>x0\\c_0 & \mbox{for } x \leq x0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

'''Required parameters:'''

# Default (size) distribution: Monodisperse

==Lorentzian or Cauchy distribution==

The CauchyLorentz Distribution, named after Augustin Cauchy and Hendrik Lorentz, is a continuous probability distribution. As a probability distribution, it is known as the Cauchy distribution, while among physicists, it is known as a Lorentz distribution, or a Lorentz(ian) function or the BreitWigner distribution. Its importance in physics is due to it being the solution to the differential equation describing forced resonance. The Lorntzian distribution has the probability density function

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f(x; x_0,\sigma)&= \frac{1}{\pi\sigma \left[1 + \left(\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma}\right)^2\right]} \\&= { 1 \over \pi } \left[ { \sigma \over (x - x_0)^2 + \sigma^2 } \right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>x_0</math> is the location parameter, specifying the location of the peak of the distribution, and <math>\sigma</math> is the scale parameter which specifies the half-width at half-maximum (HWHM).


==Lorentzian (Amplitude)==

 FIGURE DELETED 

==Lorentzian (Area)==


 FIGURE DELETED 

==Pearson-IV==

Pearson type IV distribution:

{{eqn|<math>p(x) = \frac{\left|\frac{\Gamma\!\left(m+\frac{\nu}{2}i\right)}{\Gamma(m)}\right|^2} {\alpha\,\mathrm{B}\!\left(m-\frac12, \frac12\right)} \left[1 + \left(\frac{x-\lambda}{\alpha}\right)^{\!2\,} \right]^{-m} \exp\left[-\nu \arctan\left(\frac{x-\lambda}{\alpha}\right)\right]</math>|dummy}}

The normalizing constant involves the complex Gamma function (<math>\Gamma</math>) and the Beta function (<math>\mathrm{B}</math>).

==Pearson-VII==

The {{mono|Pearson-VII}} model has been used as an approximation for the {{mono|Voigt}} function. The parameter <math>\sigma</math> is the FWHM (full-width at half-maxima). When <math>m</math> is 1.0, the function is an exact Lorentzian. As the <math>m</math>-power term increases, the function tends toward the Gaussian. For <math>m\sim 50</math>, the function is essentially Gaussian. The {{mono|Pearson VII}} function is a different parametrization of the Student-t distribution function and reads as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x) &= \frac{1}{\alpha\,\mathrm{B}\!\left(m-\frac12, \frac12\right)} \left[1 + \left(\frac{x-\lambda}{\alpha}\right)^{\!2\,} \right]^{-m} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

with <math>\alpha=\frac12\sigma/\sqrt{2^{1/m}-1}</math> we get

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x) &= \frac{2\sqrt{2^{1/m}-1}}{\sigma\,\mathrm{B}\!\left(m-\frac12, \frac12\right)} \left [ 1+ 4\left (\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma} \right )^2\left(2^{1/m}-1\right) \right ]^{-m} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where B is the Beta function.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mbox{mode} &= x_0 - \sigma \ln\left(\frac12\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Pulse Peak with 2nd Width Term==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x) &= \frac{\sigma_1+\sigma_2}{\sigma_2^2} \left(1-\exp\left(\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma_1}\right)\right) \exp\left(\frac{x-x_0}{\sigma_2}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mbox{mode} &= x_0 - \sigma_1 \ln\left(\frac{\sigma_1}{\sigma_2+\sigma_1}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mbox{mode} &= x_0 - \sigma \ln\left(\frac{1}{\gamma+1}\right) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Voigt==

The Voigt profile is a spectral line profile found in all branches of spectroscopy in which a spectral line is broadened by two types of mechanisms, one of which alone would produce a Gaussian profile (usually, as a result of the Doppler broadening), and the other would produce a Lorentzian profile. The Voigt profile is then a convolution of a Lorentz profile and a Gaussian profile: \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} V(x,x_c\vert\sigma,\gamma)& = \int_\infty^\infty D(x'\vert\sigma) \, L(x-x_c-x'\vert\gamma)\, dx' \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>x-x_c</math> is distance from line center <math>x_c</math>, <math>D(x\vert\sigma)</math> is the centered Doppler profile:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} D(x\vert\sigma) & = \frac{e^{-x^2/2\sigma^2}}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and <math>L(x-x_c\vert\gamma)</math> is the centered Lorentzian profile:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} L(x-x_c\vert\gamma) & = \frac{\gamma}{\pi((x-x_c)^2+\gamma^2)} . \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The defining integral can be evaluated as <ref name="David1986"> W. I. F. David. Powder diffraction peak shapes. parameterization of the pseudo-voigt as a voigt function. ''Journal of Applied Crystallography'', 19(1):63--64, Feb 1986.

</ref><ref name="Ida2000"> T. Ida, M. Ando, and H. Toraya. Extended pseudo-voigt function for approximating the voigt profile. ''Journal of Applied Crystallography'', 33(6):1311--1316, Dec 2000.

</ref>:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} V(x,x_c) & = \frac{\Re[w(z)]}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>\Re[w(z)]</math> is the real part of the complex error function of <math>z</math> and

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} z = \frac{x-x_c+i\gamma}{\sigma\sqrt{2}} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} The full width at half maximum (FWHM) of the Voigt profile can be found from the widths of the associated Gaussian and Lorentzian widths. The FWHM of the Gaussian profile is <math>f_G=2\sigma\sqrt{2\ln(2)}</math>. The FWHM of the Lorentzian profile is just <math>f_L = 2\gamma</math>. Define <math>\phi = f_\mathrm{L} / f_\mathrm{G}</math>. Then the FWHM of the Voigt profile (<math>f_\mathrm{V}</math>) can be estimated as:

{{eqn|<math> f_\mathrm{V}\approx f_\mathrm{G}\left(1-c_0c_1+\sqrt{\phi^2+2c_1\phi+c_0^2c_1^2}\right) </math>|dummy}}

where <math>c_0 = 2.0056</math> and <math>c_1 = 1.0593</math>. This estimate will have a standard deviation of error of about 2.4 percent for values of <math>\phi</math> between 0 and 10. Note that the above equation will have the proper behavior in the limit of <math>\phi=0</math> and <math>\phi=\infty</math>. A different approximation was given by <ref name="Olivero1977"> J. J. Olivero and R. L. Longbothum. Empirical fits to the voigt line width: A brief review. ''J. Quant. Spectrosc. Radiat. Transfer'', 17(233-236):1298, 1977.

</ref><ref name="Liu2001"> Yuyan Liu, Jieli Lin, Guangming Huang, Yuanqing Guo, and Chuanxi Duan. Simple empirical analytical approximation to the voigt profile. ''J. Opt. Soc. Am. B'', (5):666--672, 2001.

</ref>

{{eqn|<math> f_\mathrm{V}\approx 0.5346 f_\mathrm{L}+\sqrt{0.2166f_\mathrm{L}^2+f_\mathrm{G}^2} </math>|dummy}}

with an accuracy of 0.02 

==Voigt (Amplitude)==

\\[5mm]  The amplitude version of the Voigt peak is parameterized as

{{eqn|<math> V_\text{Amplitude}(x\vert A,\sigma,\gamma) = A \, \frac{\displaystyle \int_{-\infty}^\infty \frac{\exp(-u^2)}{\frac{\gamma^2}{2\sigma^2}+\left( \frac{x-x_c}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}-u\right)^2} \, du}{\displaystyle \int_{-\infty}^\infty \frac{\exp(-u^2)}{\frac{\gamma^2}{2\sigma^2}+u^2}\, du} = A \, \frac{V(x,x_c\vert\sigma,\gamma)}{V(x_c,x_c\vert\sigma,\gamma)} </math>|dummy}}

\\

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[ampl.] amplitude <math>A</math> of the Voigt peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[sigma] width of Doppler (Gaussian) contribution <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[gamma] width of Lorentzian contribution <math>\gamma>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The Doppler (Gaussian) width parameter needs to be larger than 0 <math>\sigma>0</math>. 
# The Lorentzian width parameter needs to be larger than 0 <math>\gamma>0</math>. 
# Default (Size) distribution: Monodisperse


 FIGURE DELETED



==Voigt (Area)==

\\[5mm]  The area version of the Voigt peak is parameterized as

{{eqn|<math> V_\text{Area}(x\vert A,\sigma,\gamma) = A \, \frac{\gamma}{2\pi\sqrt{\pi}\sigma^2} \int_{-\infty}^\infty \frac{\exp(-u^2)}{\frac{\gamma^2}{2\sigma^2}+\left( \frac{x-x_c}{\sqrt{2}\sigma}-u\right)^2} \, du = A \, V(x,x_c\vert\sigma,\gamma) </math>|dummy}}

\\

'''Required parameters:'''  
#[area] area <math>A</math> of the Voigt peak 
#[center] location parameter (mode) <math>x_c</math> 
#[sigma] width of Doppler (Gaussian) contribution <math>\sigma>0</math> 
#[gamma] width of Lorentzian contribution <math>\gamma>0</math> 
#[backgr] offset <math>c_0</math> 

'''Note''' 
# The Doppler (Gaussian) width parameter needs to be larger than 0 <math>\sigma>0</math>. 
# The Lorentzian width parameter needs to be larger than 0 <math>\gamma>0</math>. 
# Default (Size) distribution: Monodisperse

==Weibull==

The Weibull distribution is a continuous probability distribution. It is named after Waloddi Weibull who described it in detail in 1951, although it was first identified by Fr\'{e}chet (1927) and first applied by Rosin \& Rammler (1933) to describe the size distribution of particles. The probability density function of a Weibull random variable <math>x</math> is:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} p(x;\lambda,k) &= \begin{cases} \frac{k}{\lambda}\left(\frac{x}{\lambda}\right)^{k-1}e^{-(x/\lambda)^{k}} & x\geq0\ 0 & x<0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>k > 0</math> is the shape parameter and <math>\lambda > 0</math> is the scale parameter of the distribution. For <math>k>1</math> the <math>\mathrm{mode}</math> is given by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \mathrm{mode} &= \lambda \left(\frac{k-1}{k} \right)^{\frac{1}{k}}\, \mbox{if } k > 1. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

==Weibull (Amplitude)==

The amplitude version represents a reparametrization of the standard statistical form. The parameter <math>x_0</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning. An additional adjustment term has been added so that <math>x_0</math> represents the mode. The function returns <math>c_0</math> for those <math>x</math> where it is undefined.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} u & = \frac{k-1}{k} \\z & = \frac{x-x_0}{\lambda}+u^{1/k}  \\y(x;x_0,k,\lambda,c_0,A) &= \begin{cases} c_0 + A u^{-u} z^{k-1} \exp\left(-z^k\right) & z >= 0\\c_0 & z<0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 FIGURE DELETED 

==Weibull (Area)==

The area version represents a reparametrization of the standard statistical form. The parameter <math>x_0</math> has been added to enable variable <math>x</math> positioning. An additional adjustment term has been added so that <math>x_0</math> represents the mode. The function returns <math>c_0</math> for those <math>x</math> where it is undefined.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} z & = \frac{x-x_0}{\lambda}+\left(\frac{k-1}{k}\right)^{1/k}  \\y(x;x_0,k,\lambda,c_0,A) &= \begin{cases} c_0 + A\frac{k}{\lambda} z^{k-1} \exp\left(-z^k\right) & z >= 0\\c_0 & z<0 \end{cases} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 FIGURE DELETED  

\chapter{Plugin functions}

==JuelichCoreShell==

\\

This model considers a dense core and original two shells <ref name="Willner2000"> L. Willner, A. Poppe, J. Allgaier, M. Monkenbusch, P. Lindner, and D. Richter. Micellization of amphiphilic diblock copolymers: Corona shape and mean-field to scaling crossover. ''EPL (Europhysics Letters)'', 51(6):628, 2000.

</ref>. Besides, it considers two different density profiles: a parabolic and a star-like profile for the second shell.

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \eta_\text{shell}(r) & \propto r^{-x} \quad \text{for starlike profile <math>x=4/3</math>}\ \eta_\text{shell}(r) & \propto 1-\left(\frac{r}{L_p}\right)^{2} \quad \text{for parabolic profile of thickness <math>L_p</math>} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Model parameters:  
#[<math>b_\text{solv}</math>]  scattering length density of the solvent 
#[<math>I_0</math>] forward scattering 
#[<math>M_\text{core}</math>] molecular weight of core (g/mol) 
#[<math>M_\text{brush}</math>] molecular weight brush (g/mol) 
#[<math>\rho_\text{core}</math>] mass density of core matter (g/cm<math>^3</math>) 
#[<math>\rho_\text{brush}</math>] mass density of brush matter (g/cm<math>^3</math>) 
#[<math>b_\text{core}</math>] scattering length density of core material (cm<math>^{-2}</math>) 
#[<math>b_\text{brush}</math>] scattering length density of brush material (cm<math>^{-2}</math>) 
#[<math>N_\text{agg}</math>] aggregation number (real number) 
#[<math>d_c^+</math>] extra radius of core (compared to compact) 
#[<math>p_{12}</math>] relative distribution of shell amount in (1<math>^\text{st}</math>shell:2<math>^\text{nd}</math>shell) (<math>0\ldots\infty</math>) 
#[<math>d_1^+</math>] extra radius of first shell (compared to compact) 
#[<math>d_2^+</math>] extra radius of second shell (compared to compact) 
#[<math>\sigma_c</math>] core smearing 
#[<math>\sigma_1</math>] smearing of 1<math>^\text{st}</math> shell 
#[<math>\sigma_2</math>] smearing of 2<math>^\text{nd}</math> shell 
#[<math>x_\text{star}</math>] relative distribution of parbolic:starlike profile in 2<math>^\text{nd}</math> shell, one has to put a very high value in order to consider only a star-like profile. 
#[<math>\gamma</math>] for star-like profile the exponent is <math>4/3</math> and for a constant profile chose 0 
#[<math>L_p</math>] thickness of parabolic brush (must fit in 2<math>^\text{nd}</math> shell!) 

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} I(Q) = \left[\Delta b_c F_c + \Delta b_b (F_1+F_2)\right]^2 \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Delta b_c &= b_\text{core} - b_\text{solv}  (1-f_\text{core})\\\Delta b_b &= b_\text{brush} - b_\text{solv} (1-f_\text{brush}) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>V_c</math> and <math>V_b</math> are the core and shell bulk volumes respectively. \\

 '''Mass Conservation:'''\\From the given values of the molecular weights of the two blocks and their densities, and an assumed aggregation number <math>N_\text{agg}</math>, the bulk volumes of the core and the shell, <math>V_c</math> and <math>V_b</math>, can be calculated.

 '''Core:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} & \text{bulk core volume:} \quad          V_c = \frac{N_\text{agg} M_\text{core}}{\rho_\text{core}N_a}\\& \text{minimal radius of core:} \quad    R_c^0 = \left(\frac{3}{4\pi} V_c\right)^{1/3}\\& \text{effective core radius:} \quad     R_c = R_c^0 + d_c^+\\& \text{swollen core volume:} \quad       V_{sc} = \frac{4}{3}\pi R_c^3\\& \text{swelling factor:} \quad           s_c = \frac{V_{sc}}{V_c} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 '''Shell:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} & \text{bulk shell volume:} \quad         V_b = \frac{N_\text{agg} M_\text{shell}}{\rho_\text{shell}N_a} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The relative amount of shell material in the first shell <math>f_\text{shell1}</math> is controlled by the parameter <math>p_{12}</math>, so that the portion of the second shell <math>f_\text{shell2}</math> can be obtained through:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f_\text{shell1} &= \frac{p_{12}}{1+p_{12}}\\f_\text{shell2} &= 1-f_\text{shell1} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

 '''Shell 1:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} & |dummy}}
\text{portion of the total shell volume in first shell:} \quad  
V_{s1} = f_\text{shell1} V_b\\& 
\text{minimal radius of shell:} \quad    
R_{c1} = \left(\frac{3}{4\pi} (V_{sc}+V_{s1})\right)^{1/3}\\& 
\text{effective core radius:} \quad     
R_1 = R_{c1} + d_1^+\\& 
\text{swollen volume of first shell:} \quad       
V_{s1s} = \frac{4}{3}\pi R_1^3\\& 
\text{swelling factor:} \quad           
s_{s1} = \frac{V_{s1s}-V_{sc}}{V_{s1}} \end{align}</math>

 '''Shell 2:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} & |dummy}}
\text{portion of the total shell volume in second shell:} \quad  
V_{s2} = f_\text{shell2} V_b\\& 
\text{minimal radius of shell:}        \quad 
R_{c2} = \left(\frac{3}{4\pi} (V_{s1s}+V_{s2})\right)^{1/3}\\& 
\text{effective core radius:}          \quad 
R_2 = R_{c2} + d_2^+\\& 
\text{swollen volume of second shell:} \quad 
V_{s2s} = \frac{4}{3}\pi R_2^3\\& 
\text{swelling factor:}                \quad 
s_{s2} = \frac{V_{s2s}-V_{s1s}}{V_{s2}}\ & 
\text{fraction of star-like density profile in 2<math>^\text{nd}</math> shell:} \quad  
f_\text{star} =2 \frac{\arctan({\left\vert \right\vert}{p_\text{star}})}{\pi} \end{align}</math>

 Together with the profile functions <math>\Phi_c(r,R_c)</math>, <math>\Phi_1(r,R_1,R_2)</math>, <math>\Phi_2(r,R_1,R_2,f_\text{star})</math> and

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f_\text{Fermi}(x) = \frac{1}{1+\exp(x)} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

the volumes of the core and two shells and the corresponding form factor are determined by numerical integration. \sloppy \\

 '''Profiles:'''

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Phi_c(r,R_c) &=    f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_c) \, dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Phi_1(r,R_1,R_2) &=   (1-f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_1)) \, \, f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_2) \, dr \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

for  <math>r<R_1</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Phi_2(r,R_1,R_2,f_\text{star},\gamma) =   & (1-f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_1)) \, \, f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_2) \\\times & \left[(1-f_\text{star}) + \frac{f_\text{star}}{R_1^\gamma}\right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

for <math>r>R_1</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \Phi_2(r,R_1,R_2,f_\text{star},\gamma,L_p) =  &  (1-f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_1)) \, \, f_\text{Fermi}(r-R_2) \\\times & \left[(1-f_\text{star}) \left(1-\left(\frac{r-R_1}{L_p}\right)^2 \right) + \frac{f_\text{star}}{r^\gamma} \right] \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

  \underline{Input Parameters for model {{mono|JuelichCoreShell}}:}  

#[{{mono|C}}] scaling constant <math>C</math> 

#[{{mono|Mcore}}] molecular weight core (g/mol) <math>M_\text{core}</math> 

#[{{mono|Mbrush}}] molecular weight brush (g/mol) <math>M_\text{brush}</math> 

#[{{mono|rho_core}}] mass density core matter (g/cm<math>^3</math>) <math>\rho_\text{core}</math> 

#[{{mono|rho_brush}}] mass density brush matter (g/cm<math>^3</math>) <math>\rho_\text{brush}</math> 

#[{{mono|b_core}}] scattering length density of core material (cm<math>^{-2}</math>) <math>b_\text{core}</math> 

#[{{mono|b_brush}}] scattering length density of brush material (cm<math>^{-2}</math>) <math>b_\text{brush}</math> 

#[{{mono|Nagg}}] aggregation number <math>N_\text{agg}</math> 

#[{{mono|d1_plus}}] extra radius of shell1=core (compared to compact) <math>d_c^+</math> 

#[{{mono|part23}}] relative distribution of shell amount in (1<math>^\text{st}</math>shell:2<math>^\text{nd}</math>shell) (<math>0\ldots\infty</math>) <math>p_{12}</math> 
#[{{mono|d2_plus}}] extra radius of first shell2 (compared to compact) <math>d_1^+</math> 

#[{{mono|d3_plus}}] extra radius of second shell3 (compared to compact) <math>d_2^+</math> 

#[{{mono|sigma1}}] core smearing <math>\sigma_c</math> 

#[{{mono|sigma2}}] smearing of 1<math>^\text{st}</math> shell2 <math>\sigma_1</math> 

#[{{mono|sigma3}}] smearing of 2<math>^\text{nd}</math> shell3 <math>\sigma_2</math> 

#[{{mono|partstar}}] relative distribution of parbolic:starlike profile in shell3 <math>x_\text{star}</math>; one usually puts a very high value in order to consider only a star-like profile. 

#[{{mono|gamma}}] for star-like profile the exponent is <math>\gamma=4/3</math> and for a constant profile <math>\gamma=0</math> 

#[{{mono|lparabol}}] thickness of parabolic brush <math>L_p</math> (must fit in shell3!) 

#[{{mono|f_brush}}] scattering length density correction factor brush 

#[{{mono|f_core}}] scattering length density correction factor core 

#[{{mono|rhosolv}}] scattering length density of solvent <math>b_\textrm{solv}</math> 



==Ferrofluids==





==LogNorm_fp==



The {{mono|LogNorm}} distribution is a continuous distribuion in which the logarithm of a variable has a normal distribution. \begin{subequations}

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \text{LogNorm}(x,\mu,\sigma,p) &=  \frac{N}{c_\text{LN}} \frac{1}{x^{p}}\, \exp\!\!\left(-\frac{\ln(x/\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)\\c_\text{LN} &= \sqrt{2\pi}\,\sigma \,\mu^{1-p} \,\exp\!\!\left((1-p)^2\frac{\sigma^2}{2}\right)  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

\end{subequations} where <math>\sigma</math> is the width parameter, <math>p</math> a shape parameter, <math>\mu</math> is the location parameter. <math>c_\text{LN}</math> is choosen so that <math>\int_0^\infty\! \text{LogNorm}(x,\mu,\sigma,p)\,dR = N</math> The mode of the distribution is defined as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} x_\text{mode} = \mu e^{-p \sigma^2} \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and the <math>n^\text{th}</math> moment <math>\langle X^n\rangle</math> of the {{mono|LogNorm}} distribution as

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} \langle X^n\rangle = \frac{\int X^n\, \textrm{LogNorm}(X)\, dX}{\int \textrm{LogNorm}(X)\, dX} = \mu^n \, e^{\frac{1}{2} \sigma^2 n (2 - 2 p + n)}.  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Instead of using the parameter <math>N</math> (particle number density) another Log-Normal size distribution namely {\tt LogNorm_fp} with the volume fraction <math>f_p</math> as a parameter has been implemented. Using the volume fraction as a scaling parameter requires that the intensity is given in units of cm<math>^{-1}</math> and the scattering vector in nm<math>^{-1}</math>. Furthermore the scattering contrast needs to be supplied in units of cm<math>^{-2}</math>. More details about absolute intensity can be found in chapter . The volume fraction <math>f_p</math> can be obtained by the {{mono|LogNorm}}-distribution (eq. ) by integrating over the particle volume <math>V_P</math>. In case of spheres we get

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f_p &= 10^{21} \int_0^\infty \mathrm{LogNorm}(R) V_P(R) \, dR \\&= 10^{21} \int_0^\infty \mathrm{LogNorm}(R) \frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \, dR = 10^{21} N \frac{4}{3}\pi \langle X^3 \rangle . \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

The scaling factor <math>10^{21}</math> depends on the actual units. More details are given in section .

For other shapes than spheres the corresponding volume of the object has to be used in eq. . In case of cylinders the volume is given by <math>V_\text{cyl}=\pi R^2 L</math>. Depending whether the radius <math>R</math> or the cylinder length <math>L</math> has a size distribution the volume fraction <math>fp</math> is calculated differently namely in case for a radius distribution by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f_p &= 10^{21} \int_0^\infty \mathrm{LogNorm}(R) V_\text{cyl}(R,L) \, dR \\&= 10^{21} \int_0^\infty \mathrm{LogNorm}(R) \pi R^2L \, dR = 10^{21} N \pi L \langle X^2 \rangle \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

and in case of a length distribution by

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} f_p     &= 10^{21} \int_0^\infty \mathrm{LogNorm}(L) \pi R^2L \, dL = 10^{21} N \pi R^2 \langle X \rangle . \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

As the cylinder volume depends on <math>R^2</math>  and <math>L</math> either the second or the first moment of the distribution function is involved in calculating the volume fraction depending which parameter has a distribution. For a spherical shell a sum of different moments has to be used as listed in table .

::TABLE DELETED  

\chapter{Basic Analysis of Dynamic Light Scattering Data}  In a typical dynamic light scattering (DLS) or photo correlation scattering (PCS) experiment, the autocorrelation function <math>G^{(2)}(\tau)</math> of the intensity scattered by dispersed particles is determined as a function of the delay <math>\tau</math>. <math>G^{(2)}(\tau)</math> is related to the modulus of the normalized field autocorrelation function <math>g_1(\tau)</math> by a Siegert relationship

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} G^{(2)}(\tau) & = A g^2_1(\tau)  + B. \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

Here <math>B</math> is a background term often designated as the baseline and <math>A</math> can be considered as another instrumental factor. The time dependence of <math>g_1(\tau)</math> is related to the dynamics of the dispersed particles. For particles in Brownian motion, the time decay of <math>g_1(\tau)</math> is determined by the diffusion coefficient of the dispersed particles. In particular, for monodisperse samples <math>g_1(\tau)</math> is an exponentially decaying function:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} g_1(\tau) & = \exp(-\Gamma\tau)\\\text{ or } & \\G^{(2)}(\tau)  & = A \exp(-2\Gamma\tau) + B  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where the decay rate <math>\Gamma</math> is linked to the particles' diffusion coefficient <math>D</math> by <math>\Gamma=D Q^2</math>, where <math>Q</math> is the modulus of the scattering vector

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} Q = \frac{4\pi m_1}{\lambda_0} \sin(\theta/2) \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

<math>m_1</math> is the refraction index of the solution, <math>\lambda_0</math> the wavelength in vacuo of the incident light and <math>\theta</math> the scattering angle. At the end the Stokes-Einstein expression for the diffusion coefficient is used to get an average particle radius <math>R_\text{DLS}</math>

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} D = \frac{kT}{6\pi\eta R_\text{DLS}}  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

where <math>k</math> is Boltzmann's constant, <math>T</math> the absolute temperature, <math>\eta</math> the viscosity of the dispersion medium and <math>R_\text{DLS}</math> the particle radius (only valid for noninteracting particles).



==Cumulant Analysis==

The formulas in eq.  to  are valid for monodisperse dispersions only. For polydisperse dispersions the cumulants method of Koppel (1972) is widely used, which assumes a multi-exponential behaviour so that <math>g_1(\tau)</math> and <math>G^{(2)}(\tau)</math> can be written in a series expansion as:

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} g_1(\tau)  &= \exp\left(-\Gamma_1 \tau + \frac{\Gamma_2 \tau^2}{2} - \frac{\Gamma_3 \tau^3}{6} + \cdots \right)\ G^{(2)}(\tau) &=  A \exp\left(- 2\Gamma_1 \tau + \Gamma_2 \tau^2 - \frac{\Gamma_3 \tau^3}{3} + \cdots \right) + B  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}

{{sasfit}} assumes for the cumulant fit-routine that the function <math>G^{(2)}(\tau)</math> is supplied. As normally no error bar is available from the correlator a robust least square procedure is implemented.

  REFERENCE:\ Dennis E. Koppel, Analysis of Macromolecular Polydispersity in Intensity Correlation Spectroscopy: The Method of Cumulants, The Journal of Chemical Physics, Vol.1, No. 11 (1972), 4815- 4820



==Double Decay Cumulant Analysis==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} G^{(2)}(\tau) &=  A \left[p \, e^{- 2\Gamma_{a,1}\, \tau + \Gamma_{a,2}\, \tau^2} +(1-p) \, e^{- 2\Gamma_{b,1}\, \tau + \Gamma_{b,2}\, \tau^2}\right] + B  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==Fit of Double Stretched Exponentials==

{{eqn|<math>\begin{align} G^{(2)}(t) = A \left\{p \, \exp\left(\left[\frac{t}{\tau_1}\right]^{\gamma_1}\right) + (1-p) \, \exp\left(\left[\frac{t}{\tau_2}\right]^{\gamma_2}\right) \right\} + B  \end{align}</math>|dummy}}



==The least squares minimiser and the robust least squares procedure==

The function to be minimised is

{{eqn|<math>\chi^2 = \sum_i \left( \frac{r_i}{\Delta I_i} \right)^2</math>|dummy}}

where the residual is defined as

{{eqn|<math>r_i = I_i - I_{i,th}.</math>|dummy}}

Here <math>I_i</math>  is the intensity correlation function <math>G^{(2)}(t_i)</math> at time <math>t_i</math> with already subtracted baseline as received from a correlator, <math>I_{i,th}</math> is the value of the cumulant fit according to eq.  or a double exponential decay according to eq. . Normally no error values <math>\Delta I_i</math> are supplied from the correlator so that all data points are weighted the same. A robust fitting with bisquare weights is implemented which uses an iteratively reweighted least squares algorithm, and follows the procedure:


# Fit the model by an unweighted least squares (that is,  <math>\chi</math>). 
# Standardize the residuals via <math>u_i=r_i/(Ks)</math>. Here <math>K</math> is a tuning constant equal to 4.685, and <math>s</math> is the robust variance given by <math>MAD/0.6745</math>, where <math>MAD</math> is the median absolute deviation of the residuals

{{eqn|<math>MAD = \sum_{i=1}^N \frac{1}{N}{\left\vert \right\vert}{I_{i,th}-I_i}</math>|dummy}}


# Compute the robust weights <math>w_i</math> as a function of the standardized residuals <math>u_i</math>. The bisquare weights are given by

{{eqn|<math>w_i = \begin{cases} \left(1-u_i^2 \right)^2 & {\left\vert u_i\right\vert} < 1\\0                       & {\left\vert u_i\right\vert} \geq 1 \end{cases}</math>|dummy}}


#  Re-do the fit using the weighted minimiser:

{{eqn|<math>\chi^2 = \sum_i w_i \left( \frac{r_i}{\Delta I_i} \right)^2</math>|dummy}}


# The fit converges when the MAD changes by no more than the fraction set by {\verb"residual_tolerance"} (which has been chosen to be <math>10^{-8}</math>). Otherwise, perform the next iteration of the fitting procedure by returning to the first step.

 \chapter{Scattering Theory}

==Scattering Cross-Section==

In a scattering experiment one is interested in a detailed analysis of the scattering pattern as a function of the characteristics of the incident beam. Monochromator and collimator specify direction and energy of the incident radiation.  The radiation interacts with the sample and receives thereby a momentum transfer <math>\hbar {\underline{ Q}}</math>. By this process the radiation receives beside a direction change also an energy change. The result is described with the help of a cross-section.


 FIGURE DELETED

A detector with an efficiency <math>\epsilon</math> measures for this the number of the scattered neutrons or photons in a given direction <math>'''k'''_0+\bf Q</math>.  The distance between detector and sample should be large in comparison to the linear dimension of the detector, so that the solid angle included by the detector element <math>\Delta\Omega</math> is small.

If the incident beam has a homogeneous, continuous flow density <math>\Phi_0</math> (<math>[\Phi_0 ] = </math> neutron (photons -) per cm<math>^2</math> and second) and if the beam contain <math>N</math> identical particles, then the counting rate <math>C</math> of the detector is proportional to all these quantities. The proportionality constant is called differential scattering cross-section <math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega } = \frac{C}{\Phi_0 \, \epsilon \, N \, \Delta\Omega } </math>.  In the case of inelastic scattering the counting rate is in a certain interval <math>\delta E</math> of transferred energy in addition proportionally to <math>\delta E</math>. The appropriate proportionality constant is the partial (or double) differential scattering cross-section

{{eqn|<math>\frac{d^2\sigma}{d\Omega\, dE} = \frac{C}{\Phi_0\, \epsilon\, N\, \Delta\Omega \,\Delta E } = \rm \frac{\mbox{\parbox[b]{9.4cm}{\footnotesize Number of neutrons (photons), which are scattered per second into the solid angle <math>d\Omega</math> toward <math>\phi, \theta</math> with an energy between <math>E</math> and <math>E+dE</math>}} }{N \Phi_0\, d\Omega\, dE}</math>|dummy}}

The total differential cross-section is defined as

{{eqn|<math> \sigma_t = (\mbox{total number of scattered neutrons (photons) per sec})/\Phi_0 . </math>|dummy}}

The three different cross-section are related to each other by

{{eqn|<math> \sigma_t = \int d\Omega\, \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega} \qquad \mbox{and} \qquad \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega} = \int_0^{\infty}dE\, \frac{d^2\sigma}{d\Omega\, dE} . </math>|dummy}}

==Scattering of neutrons on atoms==

The scattering of neutrons can be explained by two types of interactions between neutrons and matter; once the strong spin dependent nuclear forces between nuclei and neutron (interaction range <math>\sim 10^{-15}</math>m) and secondly the dipole-dipole interaction  between the magnetic moment of the neutron and that of an unpaired electron, or nuclei accordingly. Both interactions have in common that their interaction potential <math>V({\underline{ r}})</math> for <math>r \rightarrow \infty</math> decays faster than <math>1/r</math>. For this kind of interaction potential the quantum mechanical scattering theory <ref name="hittmair"> O. Hittmair. ''{Lehrbuch der Quantentheorie''}. Verlag Karl Thiemig, 1972.

</ref> yields as an asymptotic approximation for the Schr\"odinger equation of the wave function

{{eqn|<math> \displaystyle \psi_{{\underline{ k}}_0}({\underline{ r}}) \xrightarrow[r\rightarrow\infty]{} \displaystyle \frac{1}{(2\pi)^{3/2}} \left[e^{\imath{\underline{ k}}_0{\underline{ r}}} + f(\theta,\phi)\frac{e^{\imath k_0 r}}{r}\right] \quad . </math>|dummy}}

For low energy particles and short range potential the partial waves methods yields for the scattering amplitude <math>f(\theta,\phi) = -b</math>, whereby the so called scattering length <math>b</math> can be determined experimentally. Via another Ansatz, the Born approximation, one gets a series expansion for <math>f(\theta,\phi)</math>. The first term of this series expansion is give by

{{eqn|<math> \displaystyle f^{(0)}(\theta,\phi) = - \frac{m_N}{2 \pi \hbar^2} \int d{\underline{ r}}\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}} V({\underline{ r}}) \quad , </math>|dummy}}

with <math>{\underline{ Q}} = {\underline{ k-k}}_0</math>. A comparison of this result with the partial wave method (<math>f(\theta,\phi)=-b</math>), shows that the pseudo potential <math>V({\underline{ r}}) = \frac{2\pi\hbar^2}{m_N} \, b \, \delta({\underline{ r}})</math> (Fermi's pseudo potential) has an equivalent solution. For an ensemble of <math>N</math> atoms, e.g. a crystal, the total potential is in kinematic approximation the sum of the individual potentials

{{eqn|<math> \displaystyle V({\underline{ r}}) = \frac{2 \pi \hbar^2}{m_N} \sum_{j=1}^{N} b_j \delta({\underline{ r-r}}_j) \quad ,  </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>{\underline{ r}}_j</math> describes the position of nuclei <math>j</math> with scattering the length <math>b_j</math> and <math>N</math> the number of scattering atoms. The scattering length <math>b_j</math> depends on the element or isotope which is the scattering center. Furthermore it can depend on the spin state of the neutron and the nuclei and on unpaired electrons in non fully occupied atomic electron shells. In the kinematic approximation it is assumed that the intensity of the incoming beam is identical at each scattering center, i.e. that the scattered intensity does not attenuate the incoming beam. Furthermore it is assumed that the incoming beam is only scattered once and multiple scattered can be neglected.

In the static approximation, i.e. for a scattering process which does not change the state of the scattering center and therefore is an elastic scattering process, the differential scattering cross-section can be described in terms of a scattering amplitude <math>f^{(0)}(\theta,\phi)</math> by

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N}\, {\left\vert \right\vert}{f^{(0)}(\theta,\phi)}^2 = \frac{1}{N}\, \left( \frac{m_N}{2\pi\hbar^2}\right)^2 \, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\int d{\underline{ r}}\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}\, V({\underline{ r}})}^2 \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

Using Fermi's pseudo potentials (eq. ) leads to the expression

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}})  = \frac{1}{N} \, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\sum_{j=1}^N\, b_j\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_j}}^2  = \frac{1}{N}\,\sum_{i,j} b_i\, b_j\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_i}\, e^{-\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_j}  </math>|dummy}}

Let us now consider a new system of scatterers, which are only different to those from eq.  that the scattering length of the nuclei are exchanged. Hereby both the position and fraction of the scattering length <math>b_i</math> are kept the same. For a large number of scattering centers the average over the cross-sections of all possible systems of scatterers which are identical to the one in  can be described by

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N}\, \sum_{i,j} \overline{b_i\, b_j}\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_i} \, e^{-\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_j} \qquad .  </math>|dummy}}

If the scattering length <math>b_i</math> occur in the same fraction <math>x_i</math>, whereby <math>\sum_i x_i = 1</math>, so that the averages <math>\overline{b}</math> and <math>\overline{b^2}</math> can be written as

{{eqn|<math> \overline{b} = \sum_i x_i\, b_i \mbox{    and    }  \overline{b^2} = \sum_i x_i b_i^2 \quad . </math>|dummy}}

Under the condition that there are no correlations between the scattering lengths of the individual nuclei one can write

{{eqn|<math> \overline{b_i\, b_j} = \overline{b}^2 \mbox{ for } i \neq j \qquad \mbox{ and } \qquad \overline{b_i\, b_j} = \overline{b^2} \mbox{ for } i = j </math>|dummy}}

From this it follows for the differential cross-section

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N} \left( \overline{b^2} + \overline{b}^2 \sum_{i,j \atop i\neq j} e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_i} \, e^{-\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_j} \right) = \underbrace{\frac{1}{N}\left( \overline{b^2}-\overline{b}^2\right) }_{\frac{d\sigma_{\rm inc}}{d\Omega}} + \underbrace{\frac{1}{N}\, \overline{b}^2 \, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\sum_i e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_i}}^2 }_{\frac{d\sigma_{\rm coh}}{d\Omega}} </math>|dummy}}

===Nuclear scattering=== The simplest system of scatterers consist of only one isotope with nuclear spin <math>I</math>. As the spin of the neutron is <math>s = \pm 1/2</math> only two orientations are possible: parallel spins of neutron and nuclei, i.e. a total spin of <math>J_{(+)}=I+1/2</math> or antiparallel spins, i.e <math>J_{(-)}=I-1/2</math>.  The corresponding scattering length are named <math>b_{(+)}</math> and <math>b_{(-)}</math>.\footnote{For coherent scattering the spin of the neutrons keep constant in contrast to incoherent scattering where a part come along with a spin-flip. This can e.g. be used to change the ration between coherent and incoherent scattering.}

The number of possible states for the total spin <math>J_{(\pm)}</math> are <math>2J_{(+)}+1 = 2I+2</math> and <math>2J_{(-)}+1 = 2I</math>. In case of unpolarized neutrons and/or random oriented nuclear spins parallel and antiparallel spin states have the same probability. The fraction <math>x_{(\pm)}</math> of the scattering lengths <math>b_{(\pm)}</math> is therefore proportional to the corresponding number of states

{{eqn|<math> x_{(+)} = \frac{2J_{(+)}+1}{\left(2J_{(+)}+1\right) + \left(2J_{(-)}+1\right)} = \frac{I+1}{2I+1} \mbox{ and } x_{(-)} = \frac{I}{2I+1} \quad . </math>|dummy}}

For the average scattering length <math>\overline{b}</math> we therefore get

{{eqn|<math> \overline{b} = \sum_{i=(+),(-)} x_i\, b_i =\frac{1}{2I+1}\left[ (I+1)\, b_{(+)} + I\, b_{(-)} \right] \quad . </math>|dummy}}

For a mixture of different elements and isotopes of type <math>l</math> with nuclear spin <math>I_l</math> and the fraction  <math>x_l</math> (with <math>\sum_l x_l = 1</math>) the averages can be written as \begin{eqnarray} \overline{b} &=& \sum_l \frac{x_l}{2I_l+1} \left[ (I_l+1)\, b_{l(+)} + I_l\, b_{l(-)} \right] \\\overline{b^2} &=& \sum_l \frac{x_l}{2I_l+1} \left[ (I_l+1)\, \left(b_{l(+)}\right)^2 + I_l\, \left(b_{l(-)}\right)^2 \right] \quad . \end{eqnarray}

===Magnetic Scattering=== In magnetic materials the contribution of the interaction between neutrons and atomic magnetic dipole moments to the scattering length has the same order of magnitude than the nuclear scattering length. The magnetic scattering is based on the interaction of the magnetic moment of the neutron <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_n</math> with the magnetic moment of the scattering atom <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_A</math>. The magnetic interaction potential <math>V({\underline{ r}})</math> is described by

{{eqn|<math> V({\underline{ r}}) = -\mbox{\boldmath<math> \mu</math>}_n \cdot {\underline{ B}}({\underline{ r}}), </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_n=\gamma\frac{e \hbar}{2 m_p}\mbox{\boldmath</math> \sigma<math>}=\gamma\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_N</math> is the magnetic dipole moment\footnote{neutron magnetic moment: <math>\mu_n=-0.966 236 45 \times 10^{-26}</math> JT<math>^{-1}</math>, neutron magnetic moment to Bohr magneton ratio{{eqn|<math>\mu_{\rm n}/\mu_{\rm B}=-1.041 875 63 \times 10^{-3}</math>, nuclear magneton: <math>\mu_{\rm n} = 5.050 783 43 \times 10^{-27}</math> JT<math>^{-1}</math>, Bohr magneton: <math>\mu_{\rm B}=\frac{e\hbar}{2m_e}=927.400 949 \times 10^{-26}</math> JT<math>^{-1}</math>, proton mass: <math>m_p=1.672 621 71 \times 10^{-27}</math> kg, neutron mass: <math>m_{\rm n}</math>: <math>1.674 927 28 \times 10^{-27}</math> kg, electron mass: <math>m_{\rm e} 9.109 3826 \times 10^{-31}</math>kg, elementary charge: <math>e=1.602 176 53 \times 10^{-19}</math> C, Planck constant over <math>2\pi</math>: <math>\hbar=h/2\pi=1.054 571 68 \times 10^{-34}</math> J s}  of the neutron, \mbox{\boldmath<math> \sigma</math>} Pauli's spin operator, <math>\gamma</math> the neutron magnetic moment to nuclear magneton ratio\footnote{neutron magnetic moment to nuclear magneton ratio <math>\gamma = \mu_{\rm n}/\mu_{\rm N}-1.913 042 73</math>} and <math>{\underline{ B}}({\underline{ r}})</math> the magnetic field of an atom at the position of the neutron. An atom generates a magnetic field due to the magnetic dipole moment <math>\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_S</math> of its electrons <math>{\underline{ B}}_S({\underline{ r}})</math>

{{eqn|<math> {\underline{ B}}_S({\underline{ r}}) = \mbox{\boldmath<math> \nabla</math>} \times {\underline{ A}} \,\mbox{  with } {\underline{ A}} = \frac{\mu_0}{4\pi}\, \frac{\mbox{\boldmath<math> \mu</math>}_S \times{\underline{ r}}}{r^3} </math>|dummy}}

and due to the orbital angular momentum of the electrons <math>{\underline{ l}} = -{\underline{ p}}\times{\underline{ r}}</math> which generates a field of <math>{\underline{ B}}_L({\underline{ r}})</math>

{{eqn|<math> {\underline{ B}}_L({\underline{ r}}) = -\frac{\mu_0}{4\pi}\, \frac{2\mu_B}{\hbar}\, \frac{{\underline{ p}}\times{\underline{ r}}}{r^3}. </math>|dummy}}

The magnetic interaction potential <math>V({\underline{ r}}) = -\mbox{\boldmath</math> \mu<math>}_n\cdot({\underline{ B}}_S({\underline{ r}})+{\underline{ B}}_L({\underline{ r}}))</math> is a weak long range potential which also can be treated with Born's approximation. Compared to the nuclear scattering amplitude the corresponding magnetic scattering amplitude <math>b_M</math> is given by the Fourier transformation of the magnetic interaction potential <math>\mathcal{F}[V({\underline{ r}})]</math>

{{eqn|<math> b_M = -\frac{m_n}{2\pi\hbar^2}\, \mbox{\boldmath<math> \mu</math>}_n \cdot \int d^3r \, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}\, ({\underline{ B}}_S({\underline{ r}})+{\underline{ B}}_L({\underline{ r}})). </math>|dummy}}

The Fourier transformation of the magnetic field is related in case of a static magnetic field to the Fourier transformation of the local magnetization <math>{\underline{ M}}({\underline{ Q}}) = \mathcal{F}[{\underline{ M}}({\underline{ r}})]</math> <ref name="book:Squires"> G. L. Squires. ''Thermal Neutron Scattering''. Cambridge University Press, 1978.

</ref> by

{{eqn|<math> {\underline{ B}}({\underline{ Q}}) = \mu_0\, \frac{{\underline{ Q}}\times [{\underline{ M}}({\underline{ Q}})\times{\underline{ Q}}]}{Q^2} = \mu_0\, {\underline{ M}}_{\bot}({\underline{ Q}}), </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>{\underline{ M}}_{\bot}({\underline{ Q}})</math> is the component of <math>{\underline{ M}}({\underline{ Q}})</math> perpendicular to <math>{\underline{ Q}}</math> and <math>\mu_0 = 4\pi\, 10^{-7}</math> Vs/Am the magnetic constant. For the magnetic scattering amplitude\footnote{Frequently the magnetization is given in units of Bohr magnetons (<math>\mu_B=\frac{e\hbar}{2m_e}=927.400 949 \times 10^{-26}</math> J/T, 1[J/T]=1[Am<math>^2</math>]) per atomic volume <math>\Omega</math> so that the magnetic scattering length density can be written as <math>b_M=D_{\mu}\sum_i c_i{M_i}/\Omega_i</math> with <math>D_\mu=2.69914\times 10^{-15}</math>m<math>^2</math>. The two constants are related via <math>D_\mu=D_M\mu_0\mu_B</math>.} <math>b_M</math> we find than

{{eqn|<math> b_M = D_M\, \mu_0\, \mbox{\boldmath<math>\sigma</math>}\cdot {\underline{ M}}_{\bot}({\underline{ Q}}) \mbox{  with  } D_M = -\gamma\, \frac{m_n}{2\pi\hbar^2}\, \mu_N = 2.31605 \times 10^{14} \, \frac{1}{\mbox{m<math>^2</math> Tesla}}. \quad </math>|dummy}}

For scattering on magnetic structures always two interactions have to be considered, nuclear scattering which is caused by fluctuations in the number density and composition and magnetic scattering caused by fluctuations in amplitude and/or orientation of the local magnetization. In case of a preferred orientation, e.g. the direction of an external applied magnetic field <math>{\underline{ H}}</math>, the magnetic scattering depends on the spin state {\boldmath <math>\sigma</math>} of the neutron. If <math>{\underline{ e}}_x</math> describes the direction of the preferred axis and (<math>+</math>) and (<math>-</math>) the neutron spin polarisation antiparallel and parallel to <math>{\underline{ e}}_x</math> than the scattering can be described by four scattering processes; these are two spin non-flip (<math>++</math>,<math>--</math>) and two spin flip (<math>+-</math>,<math>-+</math>) processes. Moon, Riste und Koehler <ref name="scatt91"> R. M. Moon, T. Riste, and W. C. Koehler. Polarization analysis of thermal-neutron scattering. ''Physical Review'', 181(2):920--931, May 1969.

</ref> have shown that for coherent scattering the four scattering length are given by \begin{eqnarray} b_{\pm\pm} &= b_N \mp D_M\, \mu_0\, M_{\bot x}\\b_{\pm\mp} &= - D_M\, \mu_0\, (M_{\bot z} \pm \imath \, M_{\bot y}). \end{eqnarray} whereby <math>b_N</math> is the nuclear scattering length. In case of unpolarized neutrons the differential scattering cross-section can be written as

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma_{\rm unp}}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{d\sigma_{\rm nuc}}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) + \frac{d\sigma_{\rm mag}}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) , </math>|dummy}}

because <math>(b_{++}^2+b_{--}^2+b_{+-}^2+b_{-+}^2)/2 = b_N^2+D_M^2 \mu_0^2 M_{\bot}^2</math>. For unpolarized neutrons the interference contribution only has an influence on the degree of polarization of the scattered neutrons but not on the scattering intensity.  \\

==Scattering of x-ray at atoms==

The scattering of x-rays on matter practically exclusively depends on the interaction of the incoming radiation with electrons. The contribution on the nuclei is negligible small because the mass of the nuclei is more than <math>10^3</math> times larger than the mass of an electron and the energy of the nuclear scattering more than <math>10^6</math> smaller than the energy of the scattering on electrons.

The frequency <math>\nu_0 = c/\lambda</math> of the incoming x-ray beam is in general large against the resonance frequency of the electrons. In this case the electrons can be considered to be free and the special properties of the chemical binding are of no importance. These are the conditions for Thomson-scattering. J.J. Thomson developed a simple classical model for this type of scattering. Under the influence of an electric field of x-rays electrons start to oscillate. For an incoming plane and monochromatic wave with an electric field <math>{\underline{ E}} = {\underline{ E}}_0 e^{\imath ({\underline{ k}}_0{\underline{ r}} - \omega t)}</math> the amplitude <math>E_s</math> of a wave scattered on a free electron is

{{eqn|<math> E_s = -E_0 \frac{e^2}{m_e c^2} \frac{1}{r} \sin \psi . </math>|dummy}}

Hereby <math>e</math> and <math>m_e</math> are the charge\footnote{<math>e = 1.602 176 53 \times 10^{-19}</math> C} and mass\footnote{<math>m_e = 9.109 3826 \times 10^{-31}</math> kg} of the electron, respectively. <math>c</math> is the speed of light\footnote{<math>c = 299 792 458</math> m s<math>^{-1}</math>}, <math>r</math> the distance between sample and detector and <math>\psi</math> the angle between the direction of the accelerated electrons by the incoming wave and the direction of the scattered wave.

Analogously to the neutron scattering length for an electron the x-ray scattering length (far field of a Hertz dipole) is defined as

{{eqn|<math> b_{\rm x-ray} = \frac{e^2}{m_e c^2} \sin\psi = r_0 \sin\psi </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>r_0 = e^2 / (m_e c^2) = 2.82 \times 10^{-13}</math> cm is the classical electron radius. For small angle scattering <math>\psi \simeq \pi/2</math> whereby the angle dependent polarization factor is approximately 1.

For calculating the scattering amplitude of an atom with <math>Z</math> electrons one has to sum up the scattered waves from the different electrons with the correct phase. To do this an electron density distribution <math>\rho_e({\underline{ r}})</math> can be introduced which describes the time average probability distribution of the electron in the atom. The scattering amplitude of an atom is than

{{eqn|<math> f_a({\underline{ Q}}) = r_0\, \underbrace{L(Q)}_{{\rm {polarization\atop {factor}}}\sim 1} \int d{\underline{ r}} \, \rho_e({\underline{ r}})\, e^{\imath {\underline{ Qr}}} \stackrel{Q\rightarrow 0}{=} r_0\, Z.  </math>|dummy}}

The charge distribution in an atom can be described in good approximation by a radial symmetric function so that

{{eqn|<math> f_a(Q) = r_0 \int_0^{\infty} \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr} \, \rho_e(r) \, 4\pi r^2\, dr  \stackrel{Q\rightarrow 0}{=} r_0 \int_0^{\infty} \rho_e(r) \, 4\pi r^2\, dr  = r_0 Z .  </math>|dummy}}

In small angle scattering the scattering length of an atom is therefore <math>f_a = r_0 Z</math> or in units of "electon units [e.u.]" <math>f=f_a/r_0=Z</math>.  \\

===Anomalous scattering of x-rays===

The relation <math>f=Z</math> for atomic scattering length is only valid as long as the energy of the incoming radiation is much larger than the energy of the K-, L-, etc. shells. In case that the absorption edge of an atom is close to the energy of the incoming beam the scattering length has to be corrected by a dispersion term. In general the scattering length of an atom depends on the energy of the x-rays and is a complex number

{{eqn|<math> f(E) = Z + f'(E) + \imath f''(E) \quad . </math>|dummy}}

The correction terms <math>f'</math> and <math>f''</math> change the scattering length <math>f</math> near a K<math>_{\alpha}</math> absorption edge typically up to 30\Figure  shows the energy dependency of the scattering length of iron <math>f'_{\rm Fe}</math> und <math>f''_{\rm Fe}</math>. The dispersion terms are related via the Karamer-Kronig relation

{{eqn|<math> f'(E) = \frac{2}{\pi}\, \int_0^{\infty} dE'\, \frac{E'\, f''(E')}{E'^2-E^2}.  </math>|dummy}}


 FIGURE DELETED In general the imaginary part <math>f''</math> can be determined experimentally by the mass absorption coefficient <math>\mu_m(E)=\frac{2N_A}{A}\, \lambda\, r_0\, f''(E)</math> (<math>N_a</math> Avogadro number, <math>A</math> mass of atom in resonance) to calculate than with equation  the real part of the scattering length <math>f'(E)</math>.

==Small angle scattering==

In small angle scattering geometry the structural unit down to single atoms can not be resolved, only structures larger than several atomic layers can be seen by that method. Small angle scattering techniques measure the beam scattered close to forward direction whereby the beam divergence is in the order <math>\lambda/L \ll 1</math>. For a wavelength of  <math>\lambda = 1</math> nm and a characteristic dimension <math>L = V^{1/3}</math> of the scatterer of about 10 nm the divergency of the beam is  about <math>\lambda/L = 0.1 \mbox{ rad} \simeq 6.3^{\circ}</math>.

In principle with small angle scattering  both information on size as well as shape of the scatterer and information on the relative arrangement of the scatterer can be obtained. In the following an overview of the theoretical basics to analyze small angle scattering data will be given.

In section  the scattering of an ensemble of atoms has been discussed. There interference terms between the waves scattered by the individual atoms were important for the differential scattering cross-section (eq.  ,).

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N} \, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\sum_{j=1}^N\, b_j\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}_j}}^2 \qquad \,\mbox{ or} \qquad \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N} {\left\vert \right\vert}{\sum_{j=1}^{N} f_{a,j}({\underline{ Q}})\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}}^2  . </math>|dummy}}

Small angle scattering normally does not resolve dimension down to atomic dimensions. Therefore the summation over the individual atoms can be replaced by an integration over the illuminated volume <math>V</math>:

{{eqn|<math> \frac{d\sigma}{d\Omega}({\underline{ Q}}) = \frac{1}{N}\, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\int_V d{\underline{ r}}\, \rho({\underline{ r}})\, e^{\imath {\underline{ Qr}}}}^2 = \frac{1}{N}\, I({\underline{ Q}}) \quad ,  </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>\rho({\underline{ r}})</math> is the local scattering length density and <math>F({\underline{ Q}})</math> the scattering amplitude. The differential scattering cross-section is mathematically the square of the modulus of the Fourier transformation of the scattering length density. The scattering length density <math>\rho({\underline{ r}})</math> is proportional to the locally averaged scattering potential <math>\bar V({\underline{ r}})</math>. Thats why equation  is except a constant identical to equation . The scattering intensity <math>I({\underline{ Q}}) = {\left\vert z\right\vert}^2 = z\, z^*</math> can therefore be written as

{{eqn|<math> I({\underline{ Q}}) = \int \!\!\! \int d{\underline{ r}}_1\, d{\underline{ r}}_2\, \rho({\underline{ r}}_1) \, \rho^*({\underline{ r}}_2) \, e^{\imath{\underline{ }}{Q(r_{\rm 1}-r_{\rm 2})}} \quad . </math>|dummy}}

By using the substitution <math>{\underline{ r}} = {\underline{ r}}_1 - {\underline{ r}}_2</math> one get

{{eqn|<math> I({\underline{ Q}}) = \int d{\underline{ r}} \, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}} \underbrace{ \int d{\underline{ r}}_1\, \rho({\underline{ r}}_1) \, \rho^*({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}}) }_{\displaystyle \Gamma({\underline{ r}}) =  \rho({\underline{ r}}) \circledast \rho({\underline{ r}}) } = \int d{\underline{ r}}\, \Gamma({\underline{ r}})\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}} \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

<math>\Gamma({\underline{ r}})</math> is the autocorrelation function\footnote{Normally the autocorrelation function is defined as <math>\rho ({\underline{ r}}) \circledast \rho ({\underline{ r}}) = \int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, \rho ({\underline{ r}}_1)\, \rho^{*}({\underline{ r}}_1+{\underline{ r}})</math>, so that in fact <math>\Gamma ({\underline{ r}})</math> should be <math>\Gamma ({\underline{ r}}) = \rho(-{\underline{ r}}) \circledast \rho (-{\underline{ r}})</math>.} In case of a real scattering length density eq.  is the convolution integral <math>\Gamma({\underline{ r}}) = \rho ({\underline{ r}}) * \rho ({\underline{ r}}) = \int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, \rho ({\underline{ r}}_1)\, \rho ({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}})</math> of the scattering length density and is called Patterson function\footnote{Here the Patterson function is defined as the autocorrelation of the scattering length density <math>\rho({\underline{ r}})</math>. Instead of defining the cross-section via a scattering length density as in  in neutron scattering the differential cross-section is often defined as <math>{d\sigma_{\rm coh}({\underline{ Q}})}/{d\Omega} = ({\sigma_{\rm coh}}/{4 \pi\,N})\, \int d{\underline{ r}}\,  \rho({\underline{ r}}) \circledast \rho({\underline{ r}}) \,\exp(\imath{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ r}})</math>, whereby <math>\sigma_{\rm coh} = 4 \pi\, \overline{b}^2</math> is the coherent cross-section and <math>\rho({\underline{ r}})</math> the particle number density. The Patterson function is than the autocorrelation of the particle number density. This function is independent of the scattering lengths and only dependent on the geometric arrangement of the scattering centers. The Fourier transformation of the Patterson function is therefore sometimes called Structure factor <math>S({\underline{ Q}})</math> and is related in case of a static approximation to the differential cross-section by the equation <math>d\sigma_{\rm coh}({\underline{ Q}})/d\Omega = (\sigma_{\rm coh}/4\pi N)\, S({\underline{ Q}})</math>.}.

==Autocorrelation function <math>\Gamma({\underline{ r==

})</math> and <math>\gamma({\underline{ r}})</math>}

In the following we will make use of two simplifications. Firstly the scattering system is assumed to be isotropic. Hereby the isotropy can have its origin both in the shape of the scatterer or being a consequence of the temporal change of the particle orientation. The consequence is that <math>\Gamma({\underline{ r}})</math> only depends on the modulus of <math>r</math> and <math>e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}</math> can be averaged over all orientations of <math>{\underline{ r}}</math>. (The second simplification will follow further below)

===Isotropic averages===

If <math>\alpha</math> is assigned to the angle between <math>{\underline{ Q}}</math> and <math>{\underline{ r}}</math> and if all orientations <math>\alpha</math> are equal probable than the probability <math>p_{\alpha}\, d\alpha</math> that <math>{\underline{ Q}}</math> and <math>{\underline{ r}}</math> include the angle <math>\alpha</math> is equal to <math>\frac{1}{2}\, \sin\alpha\, d\alpha</math>.

{{eqn|<math> p_{\alpha}\, d\alpha = \frac{2\, \pi\, R\, \sin\alpha}{4\, \pi\,R^2}\, R\, d\alpha = \frac{\sin\alpha}{2} \, d\alpha </math>|dummy}}

The average of <math>e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}</math> over all orientations of <math>{\underline{ r}}</math> is \begin{eqnarray} \overline{e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}}}^{ {\underline{ r}}} &=&\int_0^{\pi}\, d\alpha\,\frac{\sin\alpha}{2}\, e^{\imath Qr\cos\alpha} = \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr}\\&=& \underbrace{\int_0^{\pi}\, d\alpha\, \frac{\sin\alpha}{2}\, \cos(Q\,r\,\cos\alpha)}_{\rm symmetric to \alpha=\pi/2} + \imath \underbrace{\int_0^{\pi}\, d\alpha\, \frac{\sin\alpha}{2}\, \sin(Q\, r\, \cos\alpha)}_{{\rm antisymmetric to }\alpha=\pi/2 \Rightarrow  = 0}\\&=& 2 \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}}\, d\alpha\, \frac{\sin\alpha}{2}\, \cos(Q\, r\, \cos\alpha) = \frac{1}{Qr}\, \int_0^{Qr}\, du\, \cos u = \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr}  \end{eqnarray} and equation  is simplified to

{{eqn|<math> I(Q) = \int dr\, 4\pi\, r^2\, \Gamma(r) \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr} \quad . </math>|dummy}}

===Absence of long range order===

As a second simplification it will be assumed that long range order is absent. The consequence is that the autocorrelation <math>\Gamma(r)</math> becomes constant for large values of <math>r</math>. It converges to <math>\bar\Gamma = \bar \rho^2 V</math>. <math>\bar \rho</math> is the average scattering length density defined by

{{eqn|<math> \int \, d{\underline{ r}}\, \left( \rho({\underline{ r}}) - \bar\rho \right) = \int\, d{\underline{ r}}\, \eta({\underline{ r}}) = 0  \quad . </math>|dummy}}

Structural information is therefore only contained in a finite range of <math>\Gamma({\underline{ r}})</math> where it deviates from the average value <math>\bar \Gamma</math>. This is due to the fact that only  a deviation of <math>\eta({\underline{ r}})</math> from the average <math>\bar \rho</math> leads to a scattering contribution for <math>Q\not= 0</math>. An additional constant value <math>\bar \rho</math> only contributes to the scattering at <math>Q=0</math> and is therefore not accessible. Consequently the average scattering length density can be subtracted without loss of generality and only deviations <math>\eta({\underline{ r}}) = \rho({\underline{ r}}) - \bar\rho</math> have to be considered. The autocorrelation function is therefore defined as \begin{eqnarray} \gamma(r) &=& \frac{1}{V}\,\overline{\eta({\underline{ r}}) \circledast \eta({\underline{ r}})}^{ {\underline{ r}}} =\frac{1}{V}\,\overline{(\rho({\underline{ r}})-\bar\rho)\circledast (\rho({\underline{ r}})-\bar\rho)}^{ {\underline{ r}}} \\[2mm] \Rightarrow \quad I(Q) &=& V \int_0^{\infty} dr \, 4\pi\, r^2\, \gamma(r) \,\frac{\sin Qr}{Qr} .  \end{eqnarray} <math>V\gamma ({\underline{ r}})</math> is different from <math>\Gamma({\underline{ r}})</math> due to the definition of <math>\bar \rho</math> (eq. ) only by a constant term <math>\bar\rho^2\,V</math>, i.e. <math>V\,\gamma({\underline{ r}}) = \Gamma({\underline{ r}})-\bar\rho^2\,V</math> because \begin{eqnarray} \Gamma({\underline{ r}}) &=& \int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, \rho({\underline{ r}}_1)\, \rho^{*}({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}}) = \int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, (\bar\rho +\eta({\underline{ r}}_1))(\bar\rho + \eta({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}}))^{*}\\&=& \bar\rho^2 V + \underbrace{\int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, (\bar\rho\,  \eta({\underline{ r}}_1)+ \bar\rho^{*}\, \eta^{*}({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}}))}_{\mbox{= 0  due to def. of <math>\eta({\underline{ r}})</math> in eq. }} + \int\, d{\underline{ r}}_1\, \eta({\underline{ r}}_1) \, \eta^{*}({\underline{ r}}_1-{\underline{ r}})\\&=& \bar\rho^2 V + \gamma({\underline{ r}})\, V . \end{eqnarray}

===Limits <math>r=0</math> and <math>r=\infty</math>===

The limits <math>r=0</math> and <math>r\rightarrow\infty</math> for the autocorrelation <math>\gamma({\underline{ r}})</math> are <math>\gamma(0) = \overline{\eta^2}</math> and <math>\gamma(\infty)=0</math>. The limit <math>r\rightarrow\infty</math> is zero because <math>\eta({\underline{ r}})</math> is defined as the deviation of the average scattering length density <math>\bar\rho</math>. As long range correlation is assumed not to be present  <math>\eta({\underline{ r}}) \circledast \eta({\underline{ r}}) \stackrel{r\rightarrow\infty}{=} \bar \eta^2=0</math>. <math>\gamma(r)</math> can be calculated by the inverse Fourier transformation of the scattering intensity <math>I(Q)</math> (eq. )

{{eqn|<math> V \, \gamma(r) = \frac{1}{2\pi^2} \, \int_0^{\infty} dQ\, Q^2\, I(Q)\, \frac{\sin Qr}{Qr} \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

An important special case is <math>r=0</math> for which equation  can be simplified to

{{eqn|<math> V\, \gamma(0)               = V\, \overline{\eta^2} =  \frac{1}{2\pi^2} \int_0^{\infty} dQ\, Q^2 \,I(Q) = \frac{\tilde Q}{2\pi^2} \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

The integration of the intensity <math>I(Q)</math> in reciprocal space is therefore directly related to the average quadratic deviation of the scattering length density <math>\overline{\eta^2}</math> but independent to the shape of the scatterers. If for example the scattering particle undergoes deformation the scattering pattern may change drastically but the integral <math>\tilde Q</math> in eq.  keeps invariant against such a deformation.

==Volume fraction==

The average quadratic deviation of the scattering length density is directly related in a two-phase system to the scattering contrast <math>\Delta\eta</math> and volume fraction <math>f_p</math> of one or the other <math>(1-f_p)</math> phase. The average quadratic deviation of the scattering length density is defined as

{{eqn|<math> \overline{\eta^2} = \eta_1^2 \, f_p + \eta_2^2\, (1-f_p) ,  </math>|dummy}}

whereby <math>\eta_1</math> and <math>\eta_2</math> are the scattering length density differences of the two phases from the average value <math>\bar\rho</math> and the scattering contrast is than defined as <math>\Delta\eta = \eta_1+\eta_2</math>. The average value <math>\bar\rho</math> is given by \begin{eqnarray} \bar\rho = (\bar\rho-\eta_1)f_p + (\bar\rho+\eta_2)(1-f_p) &\Leftrightarrow& f_p\, (\eta_1+\eta_2) = f_p\, \Delta\eta=\eta_2 \\

&{\rm or}& (1-f_p)\, \Delta\eta = \eta_1 \quad .

\end{eqnarray} Replacing <math>\eta_1</math> and <math>\eta_2</math> in eq.  leads to

{{eqn|<math> \overline{\eta^2}=\Delta\eta^2 \, f_p (1-f_p). </math>|dummy}}

The second moment of the scattering intensity, the so-called scattering invariant <math>\tilde{Q}</math> from eq. , can therefore be related to the volume fractions of the two phases <math>f_p</math> and <math>(1-f_p)</math> by

{{eqn|<math> \tilde{Q} = \int dQ \,Q^2\, I(Q) = 2\pi^2\,V\,\Delta\eta^2\,f_p(1-f_p)  \quad . </math>|dummy}}

Due to the Babinet principle in a scattering experiment the volume fractions can not be uniquely assigned to one or the other phase. A system with exchanged phases would have the same invariant A unique solution of eq.  for the volume fraction can only be obtained if either one already knows from somewhere else that the volume fraction of one phase is much smaller than the volume fraction of the other (dilute case) or if time resolved experiments are performed during the formation of the structure and when it is known that one phase is growing on the cost of the other one (Ostwald ripening).

==Interparticle interferences==

The square of the Fourier transformation of the scattering length density of a sample with a volume <math>V</math> is equal to the scattering intensity <math>I({\underline{ Q}})</math>

{{eqn|<math> I({\underline{ Q}}) = {\left\vert \right\vert}{\int_V\, d{\underline{ r}}\, \rho({\underline{ r}})\, e^{\imath {\underline{ Qr}}}}^2 \quad . </math>|dummy}}

The integration has to be carried out over the whole illuminated sample volume <math>V</math>. If the sample volume contains <math>N</math> particles embedded in a matrix with a constant scattering length density <math>\rho_M</math> and if  <math>{\underline{ R}}_i</math> defines the center of particle <math>i</math> with a constant scattering length density  <math>\rho_{P,i} = \Delta\eta_i + \rho_M</math> the scattering intensity can be written as 

\begin{eqnarray} I({\underline{ Q}}) &=& {\left\vert \right\vert}{\sum_{i=1}^N\, F_i({\underline{ Q}})\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Q\,R}}_i}}^2\\\,\mbox{ with   } F_i({\underline{ Q}}) &=& \int\limits_{V_i({\underline{ R}}_i)} d{\underline{ r}}\, \Delta\eta_i\, e^{\imath{\underline{ Q}}({\underline{ r-R}}_i)} = \Delta\eta_i\, \int\limits_{V_i({\underline{ 0}})} d{\underline{ r}} \, e^{\imath{\underline{ Qr}}} = \Delta\eta_i\, V_i \, f_i({\underline{ Q}}). \end{eqnarray} 

<math>V_i({\underline{ R}}_i)</math> describes the integration volume of scatterer <math>i</math> located at <math>{\underline{ R}}_i</math> and <math>V_i({\underline{ 0}})</math> describes the integration volume of the scatterer <math>i</math> moved to the origin of the coordinate system. <math>\Delta\eta_i</math> and <math>V_i</math> are the scattering contrast and particle volume, respectively. The square of the modulus in eq.  can be rewritten to

 \begin{eqnarray} I({\underline{ Q}}) &=& \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j=1}^N\, F_i({\underline{ Q}})\, F_j^{*}({\underline{ Q}})\, e^{\imath {\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij}}\\&=& \sum_{i=1}^N {\left\vert \right\vert}{F_i({\underline{ Q}})}^2 + \\&& \underbrace{ 2\, \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\left[ \Re\left( F_i({\underline{ Q}})\, F_j^{*}({\underline{ Q}}) \right) \, \cos{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij} - \Im\left( F_i({\underline{ Q}})\, F_j^{*}({\underline{ Q}}) \right) \, \sin{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij} \right]}_{\,\mbox{ }{<math>\Psi({\underline{ Q}})</math>}}  \end{eqnarray} 


The first term for which <math>i=j</math> the phase factor is identical to 1 and describes the sum of the scattering intensity of individual particles. The double sum in the second term describes interference effects of the scattering amplitudes scattered from different particles which depends on their relative arrangement <math>{\underline{ R}}_{ij} = {\underline{ R}}_i-{\underline{ R}}_j</math>. The scattering amplitude <math>F_i({\underline{ Q}})</math> is among other things dependent on the scattering contrast <math>\Delta\eta_i</math> and on the normalized form factor <math>F_i({\underline{ Q}}) / \Delta\eta_i V_i = f_i({\underline{ Q}})</math>, whereby <math>f_i({\underline{ Q}})</math> is a real-valued function with <math>f_i(Q=0)=1</math>. Analytical expressions for scattering intensities <math>i_0(Q) = \overline{{\left\vert f(Q)\right\vert}^2}</math> of simple geometric bodies are listed in the appendix . In case <math>\Delta\eta_i</math> is complex valued, i.e. the scatterer has an absorption contrast <math>\Delta\eta_i''</math>, the scattering contrast can be written as <math>\Delta\eta_i = \Delta\eta_i' + \imath\Delta\eta_i''</math> and for the interference term we get 

\begin{eqnarray} 
\Psi({\underline{ Q}}) &= 2 \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\, f_i({\underline{ Q}})\, f_j({\underline{ Q}})\, V_i\, V_j\,\\&&

\times \left[ (\Delta\eta_i'\, \Delta\eta_j'  + \Delta\eta_i''\,\Delta\eta_j'') \, \cos{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij} - (\Delta\eta_i'\, \Delta\eta_j'' - \Delta\eta_i''\,\Delta\eta_j' ) \, \sin{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij} \right] \quad . 
\end{eqnarray}

If now all particle have an identical scattering contrast <math>\Delta\eta=\Delta\eta_i=\Delta\eta_j</math> we get for the scattering intensity (eq. ) the expression 

\begin{eqnarray} 
I({\underline{ Q}}) &
= \sum_{i=1}^N\,V_i^2\, (\Delta\eta'^2+\Delta\eta''^2)\, {\left\vert \right\vert}{f_i({\underline{ Q}})}^2\\&& 

+ 2 \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\, f_i({\underline{ Q}})\, f_j({\underline{ Q}})\, V_i\, V_j\, (\Delta\eta'^2 + \Delta\eta''^2) \, \cos{\underline{ Q}}{\underline{ R}}_{ij} \quad . 
\end{eqnarray} 

For identical scattering contrasts there is consequently no interference between the scattering amplitude of waves scattered at the real and at the imaginary (absorption) part of the scattering contrast.

===Isotropic ensemble of particles===

In the following we assume as another simplification an isotropic ensemble of particles. Such a system of scatterer is defined as follows: If <math>{\underline{ R}}_i</math> defines the position of any particle  <math>i</math> and <math>{\underline{ R}}_{ij}</math> is the difference vector between the position of particle <math>i</math> and <math>j</math> than a system of particles is called isotropic if all vectors <math>{\underline{ R}}_{ij}</math> of the same length will take with equal probability any direction. Under this simplification the interference term <math>\Psi({\underline{ Q}})</math> in eq.  can be averaged over all directions <math>{\underline{ R}}_{ij}</math>. This average yield for <math>\overline{\sin{\underline{ QR}}_{ij}}^{ {\underline{ R}}_{ij}}=0</math> and for <math>\overline{\cos{\underline{ QR}}_{ij}}^{ {\underline{ R}}_{ij}} =  \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}}</math> (compare with eq. ). The interference term can then be written as

{{eqn|<math> \Psi({\underline{ Q}}) = 2 \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\, f_i({\underline{ Q}})\, f_j({\underline{ Q}})\, V_i\, V_j\, (\Delta\eta_i'\, \Delta\eta_j'  + \Delta\eta_i''\,\Delta\eta_j'') \, \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}} . </math>|dummy}}

Therefore also for an isotropic ensemble of scatterers no interferences between waves scatterer at the real and imaginary part of the scattering contrast appears as already shown for systems of particles with identical scattering contrast.

==Influence of the relative arrangement of scatterers on interparticle interferences==

The expression for <math>\Psi({\underline{ Q}})</math> can be further simplified if the scattering system consist of identical particles which fulfill the condition that for all of them each orientation be likewise probable and furthermore the relative position of two particles do not have an influence on their orientation. The second part of the assumption is for radial symmetric particle automatically fulfilled. However, in case of a system of close packed ellipsoidal particles with half axis <math>R</math>, <math>R</math> and <math>\nu R</math> with <math>\nu>1</math> distance of <math>2R</math> between the centers of the ellipsoids are possible, but for such an arrangement not all orientations of the ellipsoids are allowed anymore. That means that the relative distance has an influence on allowed orientations of the particles. The general case without the restrictions made in this paragraph are discussed by Guinier and Fournet in <ref name="book:Guinier:Fournet"/>. For only slightly anisotropic and not to closely packed systems the assumptions made here are at least fulfilled in first approximation. Under the made assumptions the averaging over the particle orientations can be separated from the averaging of the particle positions. As a result from the average one gets for the scattering intensity

{{eqn|<math> \overline{I(Q)} = N \overline{{\left\vert F(Q)\right\vert}^2} + 2\, {\left\vert \right\vert}{\overline{F(Q)}}^2\, \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\, \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}}  </math>|dummy}}

For low concentrations this averaging is in first approximation also valid for particles with a size distribution. The probability to find a particle at the position <math>{\underline{ R}}_i</math> is in average <math>\frac{N}{V}\, d{\underline{ R}}_i</math> and the probability to find at the same time another particle at position <math>{\underline{ R}}_j</math> is <math>\frac{N}{V}\, d{\underline{ R}}_i\, \frac{N}{V}\, d{\underline{ R}}_j</math>. A deviation from this is considered by <math>P(R_{ij})</math> so that the double sum in eq.  can be written as

{{eqn|<math> \sum_{i=1}^N\sum_{j>i}^N\, \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}} = \int_V \!\! \int_V \frac{\sin Q R_{ij}}{Q R_{ij}}\, P(R_{ij}) \, \frac{N^2}{V^2}\, d{\underline{ R}}_i\, d{\underline{ R}}_j \quad . </math>|dummy}}

For isotropic media the function <math>P(R_{ij})</math> is independent of the indices <math>i</math> and <math>j</math> and only a function of the distance R. <math>P(R)</math> has the property to converge for large distance against one. By the substitution <math>P(R) = 1-(1-P(R))</math> one gets for the interference term 

\begin{eqnarray} 
\Psi(Q) &
= {\left\vert \right\vert}{\overline{F(Q)}}^2 \frac{N^2}{V^2} \int_V\!\! \int_V \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}} \, d{\underline{ R}}_i\, d{\underline{ R}}_j 
- {\left\vert \right\vert}{\overline{F(Q)}}^2 \frac{N^2}{V^2} \int_V\!\!\int_V \frac{\sin QR_{ij}}{QR_{ij}} (1-P(R_{ij})) \, d{\underline{ R}}_i\, d{\underline{ R}}_j . 
\end{eqnarray} 

The first term can be interpreted as the scattering of a particle with the volume <math>V</math> and the average scattering contrast <math>\overline{F(Q)}\frac{N}{V}</math>. As the illuminated sample volume <math>V</math> is relatively large this contribution is practically zero for all experimental accessible scattering angles. As for isotropic media the integration over <math>d{\underline{ R}}_i</math> is independent from the integration over <math>d{\underline{ R}}_j</math> and <math>(1-P(R))</math> quickly converges against zero the first integrationin the second term (neglecting side effects) can be written as

{{eqn|<math> \int_0^{\infty} dR\, \frac{\sin QR}{QR}\, (1-P(R))\, \frac{N^2}{V^2}\, 4\, \pi\,R^2. </math>|dummy}}

The second integration only yields an additional multiplication factor <math>V</math>. Finally one gets for the scattering intensity the expression

{{eqn|<math> \overline{I(Q)} = N\, \left\{ \overline{{\left\vert F(Q)\right\vert}^2} - {\left\vert \right\vert}{\overline{F(Q)}}^2\, \underbrace{\frac{N}{V}\, \int dR\, 4\pi\, R^2\, (1-P(R)) \, \frac{\sin QR}{QR}}_{\Upsilon(Q)} \right\} \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

===Formula from Prins and Zernicke===

For radial symmetric identical scatterer the quare of the average form factor <math>{\left\vert \right\vert}{\overline{F(Q)}}^2</math> and the average of the squared form factor <math>\overline{{\left\vert F(Q)\right\vert}^2}</math> are the same so that one get for the expression from Prins and Zernicke <ref name="scatt163"> F. Zernike and J. A. Prins. {Die Beugung von R\"ontgenstrahlen in Fl\"ussigkeiten als Effekt der Molek\"ulanordnung}. ''Zeitschrift f\"ur Physik'', XLI:184--194, 1927.

</ref> and from Debye und Mencke <ref name="scatt186"> P. Debye. {\"Uber die Zerstreuung von R\"ontgenstrahlen an amorphen K\"orpern}. ''Physik. Z.'', 28:135--141, 1927.

</ref>

{{eqn|<math> \overline{I(Q)} = N\, F^2(Q)\, \underbrace{\left\{ 1 - \frac{N}{V}\, \int\, dR\, 4\pi\,R^2\, (1-P(R))\, \frac{\sin QR}{QR} \right\} }_{S(Q) = 1 - \Upsilon(Q)} \quad .  </math>|dummy}}

The problem of applying eq.  or  is the evaluation of <math>P(R)</math>. <math>P(R)</math> depends on the geometric arrangement of the scatterer. For liquid emulsions the geometric arrangement can be related to thermodynamic quantities like concentration, temperature and interaction potential between the particles. In a theory of Raman <ref name="scatt188"> C. V. Raman. ''Phil. Mag.'', 47:941, 1924.

</ref> the geometric arrangement is associated to the interaction potential  <math>U(r)</math> in a simple way. According to his theory the potential is the sum of two-body interactions and he finds for <math>P(r)</math> the relation <math>P(r) = \exp (-U(r)/kT)</math>. For larger concentrations many-body interactions have to be taken into account. In a more general theory of Born and Green <ref name="scatt142"> M. Born and H. S. Green. A general kinetic theory of liquids {I. The} molecular distribution functions. ''Proc. Roy. Soc.'', A188:10--18, 1946.
